==PAGE:3==
Summary (English)
The purpose of this project is to investigate model checking for verifying distrib-
uted railway control systems with respect to safety.
The investigation is facilitated by developing a generic state transition sys-
tem model in three steps, of which the first is an abstract model of the system
behaviour. The two next steps are refinements of the abstract model, extending
the model with an explicit communication scheme between system components
and finally refining the model to a more restricted control protocol. Each of the
model versions is instantiated and checked against generic properties, asserting
the safety and consistency of the system.
The models have been specified in a subset of the RAISE Specification Lan-
guage, called RSL-SAL, which is translatable to SAL, the input language of the
Symbolic Analysis Laboratory Model Checker, which has been used for the veri-
fication of the model instances.
The outcome of the project is firstly a generic system model which can be in-
stantiated with concrete configuration data and subsequently verified. During the
verification of different instances of the model versions, measurements of execution
time and memory usage were produced. These results are useful for investigating
the effects of state space explosion and the performance of the model checking
tool when using translated input files. Furthermore, the modelling process of the
project gave rise to several experiments with different specification approaches
being conducted. The result of these experiments are some generally applicable
recommendations for writing specifications in RSL-SAL which can optimise the
model checking process.
==PAGE:4==
ii
==PAGE:5==
Summary (Danish)
Formålet med dette projekt er at undersøge model checking som en verifika-
tionsmetode for distribuerede jernbanekontrolsystemer mht. sikkerhed.
Undersøgelsen faciliteres ved at udvikle en generisk tilstandstransitionssystem-
model i tre skridt, hvor det første skridt er en abstrakt model af systemfunktion-
aliteten. De to næste skridt er forfininger af den abstrakte model, som udvider
modellen med eksplicit kommunikation mellem systemkomponenterne og til slut
forfiner modellen til en yderligere skærpet kontrolprotokol.
Hver af modelver-
sionerne instantieres og model checkes mod generiske egenskaber, som udtrykker
sikkerhed og konsistens for systemet.
Modellerne er blevet specificeret i en delmængde af sproget RAISE Specific-
ation Language, som kaldes RSL-SAL, hvilket er oversætteligt til SAL, input-
sproget til Symbolic Analysis Laboratory Model Checkeren, som bruges til veri-
fikationen af modelinstanserne.
Resultatet af projektet er for det første en generisk systemmodel, som kan
instantieres med konkrete konfigurationsdata og efterfølgende verificeres. Under
verficeringen af forskellige instanser af modelversionerne, produceredes målinger
af tids- og hukommelsesforbruget. Disse resultater er brugbare for undersøgelsen
af state space explosion og ydeevnen for model checking-værktøjet, når der bruges
oversatte input-filer. Endvidere gav projektets model checking-proces anledning
til, at adskillige eksperimenter med forskellige fremgangsmåder for specifikation-
erne blev udført. Resultaterne af disse eksperimenter er nogle generelt anvendelige
anbefalinger om at skrive specifikationer i RSL-SAL, som kan optimere model
checking-processen.

Preface
This master’s thesis was prepared at the department of Applied Mathematics and
Computer Science at the Technical University of Denmark in fulfillment of the
requirements for acquiring a M.Sc. degree in Computer Science and Engineering.
The thesis deals with the investigation of model checking for verification of
safety properties in a geographically distributed railway interlocking system.
The thesis was written in the period from August 1 2017 to January 1 2018
under the supervision of associate professor Anne Elisabeth Haxthausen, and is
worth 30 ECTS credits.
Kongens Lyngby, January 1, 2018
Signe Geisler (s123992)

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
In today’s world, we rely immensely on technology for most of our every-day tasks
and habits. Both hardware and software systems have become ingrained in many
people’s lives, so much so that we take it for granted that the systems continuously
function properly and correctly, and become puzzled, or even angered when some
system inevitably malfunctions. In some cases, system malfunctions are more
serious than in others, namely when the system is a so-called safety-critical system.
When a failure happens in a safety-critical system, it has the potential to lead to
the loss of human lives [22]. Naturally, ensuring correct and safe functionality of
such systems is crucial.
When seeking to ensure correctness and safety of a system, the system must
be verified, i.e.
with a high degree of certainty, the desired properties of the
system must be shown to be fulfilled. The verification process can happen through
thorough testing, formal verification methods or a combination thereof.
Many automated or semi-automated systems are safety-critical: self-driving
cars, aeroplane control computers, driver-less metro systems etc.
Automation
is becoming increasingly popular – in fact, automation is on the Forbes list of
technology trends that will dominate 2017 [7]. This is one of the reasons that the
relevance of verification methods, especially formal verification, is growing. The
formal verification method model checking has proved to be an effective way of
verifying the safety of complex systems [6]. Model checking is a method where
a mathematical specification of a system is rigorously explored and checked by a
so-called model checker in order to determine the fulfilment of the desired system
properties.
This thesis is concerned with investigating model checking as a verification
method for a railway control system. Railway control systems are in charge of
controlling the trains and the position of track forks in a railway network in a
manner ensuring the safety of the trains (and thereby their passengers).
The
control systems, or interlocking systems, must prevent trains from colliding and
derailing. Without a doubt, railway control systems are safety-critical and must
be verified before being used.
To drive the investigation of this thesis, a geographically distributed inter-
locking system is used as a case study. As opposed to a centralised interlocking
system, the interlocking tasks of a geographically distributed interlocking system
are handled by collaboration between multiple control components placed at dif-
==PAGE:18==
2
1 Introduction
ferent physical locations in the railway network. The distribution of interlocking
tasks is a promising way of reducing the cost of implementing the interlocking
system, and distributed interlocking systems are therefore, especially for localised
railways with smaller budgets, worth of investigation.
1.1
Purpose of the Project
The task of a railway interlocking system is to control trains and the position of
track junctions such that the railway traffic is safe, which essentially means that
it should prevent trains from colliding and derailing. The purpose of this pro-
ject is to investigate model checking for verifying distributed railway interlocking
systems with respect to safety. A railway interlocking system will be modelled
in RSL-SAL. Safety requirements for the model will be formulated and model
checked.
The project also investigates the best way of writing formal RSL-SAL spe-
cifications when a later translation to, and model checking in, SAL is the goal.
Recommendations will be given based on experiments conducted by the author
during the project.
1.2
Overview of Chapters
Chapter 2 – Background
explains the necessary background knowledge for
the reader and motivates the choice of tools.
Chapter 3 – Related Work
introduces some relevant pieces of work which
are related to this thesis.
Chapter 4 – The Case Study
explains the engineering concept and other
details of the case study which is considered in this thesis.
Chapter 5 – Method
gives a general overview of the development methods
which were applied in the modelling phase of the project.
Chapter 6 – Modelling
presents the generic system models which were de-
veloped throughout the project along with the generic system properties, against
which instances of the models should be checked.
Chapter 7 – Debugging Process
explains some essential steps in the debug-
ging process of the system models.
==PAGE:19==
1.3 Assumptions About the Reader
3
Chapter 8 – Model Checking
presents the verified model instances along
with the verification results and verification metrics for each of them.
Chapter 9 – Experiments
presents the experiments with different approaches
to specification which have been conducted, along with the performance results.
Chapter 10 – Discussion
discusses the results of the verification and the
experiments; critically assesses the design choices which were made; and proposes
future work items.
Chapter 11 – Conclusion
summarises and concludes the thesis.
1.3
Assumptions About the Reader
It is assumed that the reader of this report has basic knowledge about logical op-
erators and transition systems. Familiarity with model checking is an advantage
but is not strictly necessary. It is assumed that the reader has sufficient know-
ledge about formal specification languages or functional programming languages
to grasp the meaning of RSL specifications.
The necessary domain details regarding railways and railway interlocking will
be explained, as will the details of how to use the specific model checker tool
chosen.
==PAGE:20==
4
==PAGE:21==
CHAPTER 2
Background
This chapter will provide the reader with the required background knowledge for
understanding the thesis work. Sections introducing concepts known to the reader
beforehand can be skipped. The chapter also motivates the choice of verification
approach – model checking – and choice of specification language and model
checking tool.
2.1
An Introduction to Railways
This section will briefly introduce some railway terms and the concept of railway
interlocking. In the real world, the operation and control of railways are handled
in many different ways depending on the standards given in that particular coun-
try, on the size of the railway system in question etc. This section will present
some basic notions common to most railway control systems.
2.1.1
Railway Components
Railway tracks are typically divided into two main classes: those tracks which are
used for regular train movements, called main tracks and those which are used
for e.g. storing trains, called secondary tracks. Main tracks include loops which
are track sections used for passing and overtaking trains.
up
down
Secondary track
Loop
Main track
Figure 2.1: Railway network.
==PAGE:22==
6
2 Background
Tracks can be bi-directional or be restricted to a single driving direction.
Therefore, a notion of direction must be introduced.
The two directions are
typically denoted as up and down. See Figure 2.1 for an illustrated example.
At junctions, where the railway can branch into different directions the point
(position) can be switched. The position of the point determines in which direc-
tion a passing train drives. The position can be either plus or minus, see Figure
2.2 for an illustrated example. During the switching of the point, the position
may also be intermediate, i.e. between the two absolute positions. The fixed part
of the point can be referred to as the stem of the point, whereas the positions the
point can be switched between can be referred to as the branches.
minus
plus
Figure 2.2: A point in the minus position.
A railway control system, or interlocking system, has the primary task of
ensuring the safety of the trains on the railway tracks. This is typically done by
placing signals next to the tracks which indicate to the train drivers whether they
can safely enter a specific track segment. Signals are usually placed on either side
of junctions, i.e. places where multiple track segments meet, see Figure 2.3 for an
illustrated example. Signals can also be placed on either side of crossings where
e.g. a road for cars intersects the railway tracks.
Signal
Figure 2.3: Interlocking signals.
The railway interlocking system controls the interlocking signals in order to
ensure that trains do not collide and do not derail (i.e. try to pass a point which
can be in an intermediate position).
There are many different types of inter-
locking systems [23]. Originally, interlocking systems were electrical (relays), but
they are becoming more digitalised. The interlocking systems were also typically
centralised, but it is possible to make (geographically) distributed systems as well,
which is what this project is concerned with.
==PAGE:23==
2.2 Verification of Software
7
2.1.2
Geographically Distributed vs. Centralised Railway Interlocking
This section will introduce the concept of a geographically distributed railway
interlocking system, especially underlining the differences from a centralised in-
terlocking system.
For a centralised interlocking system, there is a global notion of the state
space of the system. The control computer can observe the total state of the
system and base its interlocking decisions and signal changes on this. In contrast
to this is the geographically distributed approach, where each train is equipped
with a train control computer and control components are distributed through-
out the railway network. This project is concerned with distributed interlocking
systems where the control components along the railway tracks are referred to
as switchboxes. Each train control computer and switchbox has their own local
state space. This means that there is data which is not directly observable for
each of the control components. In order for the control computers to take safe
decisions, communication between the control computers must be introduced.
Clearly, adding communication between the control computers gives rise to new
“points of failure” against which the final system must protect.
An effect of distributing the interlocking system geographically is that there
is no need for physical signals. Because the control decisions are taken by the
train control computers in collaboration with the switchboxes along the tracks,
the “signals” can be shown to the train driver directly in the train. Alternatively,
it is possible to automate the driving of the train entirely.
The motivation for using a distributed interlocking system over a centralised
one is, among other things, the lower cost, making it available as a solution for
small, localised networks [16]. Furthermore, a centralised system has a single point
of failure, where a distributed system might, depending on the implementation
details, be able to control parts of the railway network even with a faulty control
component in another part of the network. Finally, distribution of the control
task can allow for better availability of the railway network since trains are able
to “merge” routes, by only reserving a single or a few track segments at the time,
as opposed to the traditional reservation of the full extent of the routes.
Distribution of the interlocking tasks also poses a disadvantage though. The
distribution means that data about e.g.
reservations are distributed over the
different switchboxes and the different train control computers, rather than all
being saved globally. Naturally, this means that there will be a certain amount
of duplicated data if one were to combine all data of the control components.
2.2
Verification of Software
This section will motivate the use of formal verification as part of the software
development process and introduce some important concepts about and model
checking.
==PAGE:24==
8
2 Background
Formal verification is a part of the collection of techniques formal methods,
which can be used for the specification, design and verification of both software
and hardware [4].
2.2.1
Ensuring the Correctness of Software
The correctness of software can be ensured through several means and combina-
tions thereof. In this section a few widely used techniques for for verification are
briefly introduced.
2.2.1.1
Testing
Testing is an informal method for validating the functionality of a system. It
is usually performed by feeding the system some form of input and checking
if the result is as expected.
The input can both be input which is expected
in normal use of the system (validation testing), and obscure input which is
designed to expose defects of the system (defect testing). Testing can be used
for test-driven development, which is a very popular development method, where
the specification of test cases is interleaved with the development of the system.
Test cases are specified before the functionality to be tested is implemented. The
test case is run before the implementation of the functionality, confirmed to fail
(as expected at that point), the functionality is implemented, and the test case is
run again. At this point, the test should pass. This should lead to systems with
very high test coverage. However, even with this rather systematic approach to
testing systems, tests can only ever show the presence of bugs – never the absence
[22]. This is one of the motivations for using formal verification methods.
2.2.1.2
Theorem Proving
Theorem proving is a formal verification method. In order to use theorem proving,
the system to be verified is modelled as a set of mathematical definitions. The
properties of the system are then shown to be derivable from the definitions.
To handle the trivial steps of the derivations, so-called theorem provers are used.
However, the process is not fully automated, and humans are needed for directing
the theorem prover towards the desired proof. Complex systems are typically
handled very well with this proof-based approach, but the disadvantage is the
need for human interaction. Furthermore, theorem provers are not able to give
counter-examples when proofs fail to be derived because it cannot be determined
whether the property in question is simply not derivable or if the theorem prover
has not been steered in the correct direction (yet) [1].
==PAGE:25==
2.2 Verification of Software
9
2.2.1.3
Model Checking
Model checking is another formal verification method. Similarly to theorem prov-
ing, the system to be verified is modelled using mathematical definitions. How-
ever, model checking takes a state-based approach to verification, rather than a
proof-based approach. Beside the formal specification of the system, one must
also formally specify the desired properties of the system. The model checking
process then involves exhaustively exploring the states of the specified system and
checking whether the properties hold. This process is fully automated. If one of
the properties is not satisfied in some state of the system, the model checking tool
will produce a counter-example, usually showing the path (sequence of states and
the transition between them) to that state. The path can then be investigated in
order to discover the unintended behaviour [3].
2.2.2
The Model Checking Approach
In this thesis, the formal verification method model checking is applied and invest-
igated. As mentioned, the advantage of model checking is, among other things,
that the verification process is fully automated, as opposed to for example the-
orem proving. However, model checking also has disadvantages. Especially the
well-known problem of state space explosion is a challenging part of using model
checking for verification.
In order to use model checking, the system to be verified must be formally
specified in an input language for the chosen model checking tool. The system
is specified as a state transition system. Furthermore, the desired properties of
the system must be specified as assertions about the model. The assertions are
typically formulated in Linear Temporal Logic (LTL), Computation Tree Logic
(CTL) or similar.
Generally, the temporal logics used for formulating assertions about the system
allow the specification of properties over paths in the system. The operators G
and F are temporal modalities, where G means globally or always, and F means
eventually or in the future. They each express the following meaning.
F(p) expresses that the property p will hold at some point (state) in the future.
G(p) expresses that the property p holds now and in every future state.
The state space explosion problem is a major challenge of using the model
checking approach. In systems with many variables or many interacting compon-
ents the problem can easily occur because of the many different possible states.
Since the model checker tool must explore all states of the system, models of
relatively complex systems can quickly become too large to model check, because
they simply require so much computing power and memory [3, 6].
==PAGE:26==
10
2 Background
2.3
Language and Model Checker
This section will concern the tools that are to be used in the project. As previ-
ously established the aim of the project is to formally specify a geographically
distributed interlocking system in a specification language – the model will be
model checked in order to determine the safety and correctness of the system.
There are many possible model checkers with each their own input specification
language, and suitable ones for the purpose of the project should be chosen. In the
following the choice of specification language and model checker will be presented
and motivated.
2.3.1
Choice
The RAISE Specification Language (RSL) is a formal specification language with
high readability and expressive power. RSL is not by itself an input language for
a model checker, but a possibility of using it as such already exists in the form of a
translator from a specialisation of RSL called RSL-SAL [9] to the input language
of the Symbolic Analysis Laboratory (SAL) model checker [8]. With RSL-SAL it
is possible to specify models in the subset of RSL, translate them to the input
language of the SAL model checker, and subsequently model check the specified
system.
RSL-SAL was chosen as the specification language primarily because of its
expressive power as mentioned above, but the fact that the author of this thesis
has knowledge about RSL beforehand also contributed to the decision.
The SAL model checker is a natural starting point for investigating model
checking of (translated) RSL specifications. It is part of the RAISE tool-set, and
RSL-SAL was the first extension to RSL allowing symbolic model checking to be
applied to RSL specifications. Since the RSL-SAL extension, support for other
model checking back-ends such as RT-Tester [11] and nuXmv [5] have been added
to the tool-set as well.
2.3.2
RSL and Formal Specification
The RAISE Specification Language (RSL) is a formal specification language allow-
ing the user to capture the requirements and express the functionality of software
in an unambiguous manner [12].
RSL allows the user to specify their own types and values (including functions),
but also contains several built-in types such as Bool and Int along with several
type-expressions for e.g. maps and lists.
RSL will not be explained in detail here. The reader is advised to consult the
book on the RAISE specification language ([12]) for more information.
==PAGE:27==
2.3 Language and Model Checker
11
2.3.3
RSL-SAL
RSL-SAL is the subset of RSL which is translatable to SAL but is also used to
refer to the translator tool itself. The translator tool enables the model checking
of RSL specifications by taking an RSL file as input and generating three different
SAL translations [10]. These translated SAL files can then be used as input files
for the SAL tools explained in Section 2.3.4, including the SAL symbolic model
checker.
2.3.3.1
Versions of the Translation
As mentioned, the RSL-SAL translator tool outputs three different translations
of the input RSL file. The first version can be used to verify the system under the
assumption of confidence condition satisfaction. All partial functions are viewed
as total, and type-wellformedness is assumed. The two other versions – called
the CC-version and the simple CC-version – allows the user to check confidence
conditions, where the simple version gives less diagnostic information [10]. The
confidence conditions are automatically generated by the RSL-SAL translator.
2.3.3.2
Transition System Specifications
When writing transitions systems in RSL, they must be specified in accordance
with the following grammar [10].
Transition_system_decl ::= “transition_system” {Base_module}+
,
Base_moddule ::= “[” id “]” [“in” variable_decl” ] “local” variable_decl
“in” Transition_decls “end”
Transition_decls ::= {Transition_decl}+
[=]
Transition_decl ::= Single_guarded_command | Multiple_guarded_command
Single_guarded_command ::= [“[” id “]”] logical_value_expr “==>”
Update_exprs | [“[” id “]”] “else” “==>” Update_exprs
Multiple_guarded_command ::= “([=]” variable_decl “:-”
Single_guarded_command “)”
Update_exprs ::= {id “:=” value_exprs}∗
,
The variables declared in “in” are considered inputs to the transition system
and will be initialised by the model checker. The variables declared as “local”
represent the start state of the transition system, which, if not initialised, will be
given values by the model checker.
2.3.3.3
Non-translatable Constructs
This section mentions some problematic and non-translatable constructs of RSL
which are relevant to the specifications of this thesis. The list presented here is
==PAGE:28==
12
2 Background
not an exhaustive list, and the reader should consult the RAISE user guide [10]
for this.
• The translator does not correctly deal with product types, product expres-
sions or product patterns. The user is advised to use records instead.
• List definitions, list type definitions and list expressions are not accepted,
but maps and sets are translatable. Note that this problem has been at
least partially remedied by the extension of RSL-SAL with arrays by Jacob
Hansen [13], which is mentioned in Section 3.2.
2.3.3.4
Chris Georges Advice on RSL-SAL Specifications
Chris George is one of the main contributors to RAISE [10] and has given some
advice for formulating more checkable RSL-SAL specifications based on his own
experience.
The advice is specified in an e-mail which was forwarded to this
author by Anne Haxthausen. The full e-mail can be found in Appendix H.
The first advice given by George is the following.
If your state contains some records then split into separate state
components, so you have more variables in the transition system
Even though the record type is translatable to SAL – the RAISE User Guide
even recommends using records instead of product types – using records proved
to slow down the model checking (see Section 9.2 for further information on this).
The record type was initially used to collect related variables. This approach
makes it easy to pass around the state data to the different functions, but it has a
major drawback. When changes had to be made to, for example, just one record
field, all the other fields of the record essentially had to be re-assigned to their
values (see Section 9.2 for an elaboration). George remarks, as part of his advice:
Overall, the lesson seems to be that it is not just the raw size of the
state space in terms of number of variables that is important. Having
more variables, and splitting transitions so that each only affects a few
of them, is the way to go.
When a transition in the transition system had to make an update to a re-
cord type, this went against this piece of advice since an unnecessary amount of
variables would be updated.
Another piece of advice from George is the following.
Split the transition system when you can by effectively unfolding
the top level functions, when the splits will affect fewer state variables.
He gives the following example for clarification: If one has a top-level function
f of the form
==PAGE:29==
2.3 Language and Model Checker
13
f (...) ≡if c then a else b end
pre p
Then, instead of one transition
p =⇒s′ = f(s)
Have two transitions
p ∧c =⇒s′ = a(s)
[=]
p ∧∼c =⇒s′ = b(s)
where, as he writes ‘s′ = ... each time is really a collection of state updates,
and the split ones are smaller collections than the original ones (because you don’t
include the updates that leave the variable unchanged)’.
2.3.4
SAL Tools
This section briefly describes each of the tools of Symbolic Analysis Laboratory
which were used throughout this project. Each of the tools is part of SALenv (the
SAL environment), which provides a set of batch commands for development
and analysis of SAL specifications [17]. In general, the documentation for SAL
is somewhat sparse, so the explanations given here are limited to the informa-
tion from the SAL Tutorial [17], the SAL FAQ [21] and what was deduced from
experimenting with the tools.
2.3.4.1
Symbolic Model Checker
The symbolic model checker of SALenv is called sal-smc. The symbolic model
checker can be used for verifying specified LTL properties (and CTL if one spe-
cifies the system directly in SAL). The symbolic model checker is used by using
commands such as sal-smc [context name] [assertion name].
Depending
on whether the model satisfies the assertion, sal-smc will either output proved.
or produce a counter-example [17].
2.3.4.2
Bounded Model Checker
The SALenv also contains a bounded model checker called sal-bmc which only
supports LTL assertions. The bounded model checker can, for example, be used
for finding states violating assertions within a certain depth, i.e. within a cer-
tain amount of transitions. The bounded model checker is used similarly to the
==PAGE:30==
14
2 Background
symbolic model checker, i.e. using commands such as sal-bmc <context name>
<assertion name>. The default behaviour is to look for counter-examples up to
depth 10, but this can be set with the option -depth=<number>. The bounded
model checker will either produce a counter-example or, in the case where no viola-
tion of the assertion is found within the given depth, output no counterexample
between depths: [0,<number>] [17].
It should be noted that the bounded model checker may fail to find a counter-
example for a G-property which can be falsified within a few steps if the bounded
model checker is, for example, set to depth 10 and the model deadlocks before
that. In that case, the bounded model checker can be set to look for violations
on smaller depths [21].
2.3.4.3
Deadlock Checker
The sal-deadlock-checker can be used to ensure that the given model is free
of deadlocks. The tool is used by writing sal-deadlock-checker -v <number>
<context name> <module name>, where the -v option determines the verbosity.
The deadlock checker is an essential tool since the model checkers may produce
unsound results when the transition relation is not total, i.e. when there exist
deadlocks in the model, at least for liveness properties [21, 17].
2.3.4.4
Path-finder
The sal-path-finder is a tool which can be used for generating random traces
for SAL modules. In order to produce a trace, the command sal-path-finder
[context name] [module name] can be used [17].
2.3.4.5
Simulator
The SALenv contains a simulator, which can be used to explore different execution
paths of a system. The SAL simulator can be started by entering the command
sal-sim. To simulate a system, one must import the file with the SAL specific-
ation with the command (import! "<file name>").
The simulation can be
started with the command (start-simulation! "<module name>"). One now
has the options of the following commands.
• (display-curr-trace) prints one of the traces in the set of current traces.
• (display-curr-states) prints the set of current states. The command
(display-curr-states) <number> can be used to display more than the
10 default states.
• (step!) performs a simulation step by appending the successors of the set
of current states in the current trace [17].
Some commands have been omitted since they were not used in this thesis.
==PAGE:31==
2.4 Refinement of State-based Systems
15
2.3.5
SML Tests
A subset of RSL denoted RSLSML is translatable to SML. RSLSML allows the
specification of test cases in RSL, which can then be translated to SML and the
tests can be performed. Test cases are written under the test_case keyword and
are specified as follows.
test_case
[testname] <test expression>
Testing of function specifications can be done by writing test cases of the
following form for functions with Bool return type.
[expected_true] function(<arguments>),
[expected_false] ∼function(<arguments>)
This way, the test cases will output true if the results are as expected. Simil-
arly, testing of function specifications which have other types as their return type
can be specified as follows.
[ test ]
function(<arguments>) = <expected val>
If one wishes to test a scenario or a sequence of function calls the values on
which the functions are called must be specified under the variable keyword.
The effects on variable values then persist from one test case to the following test
cases. In the following example, one can imagine that the function changes the
myData variable. The [print] test case then outputs the changed value of the
variable.
variable
myData : DataType := <inintial val>
test_case
[ test1 ] function(myData),
[print] myData
2.4
Refinement of State-based Systems
This thesis deals with the development of a state transition system model of a
real-world system.
During the development of state-based systems, it is very
common to perform so-called refinement steps, where a model on a higher level of
abstraction is transformed into a more concrete model, typically by adding details
==PAGE:32==
16
2 Background
and increasing the complexity of the model. This section will briefly explain the
concept of refinement.
Firstly, a formal description of a state based system must be given. A state
based system can be described as a Kripke structure, M = (S, R, s0, AP, L), where
• S is a set of states.
• R ⊆S × S is a total, binary relation on the state space describing possible
state changes, i.e. for all s ∈S there is a state s1 ∈S such that (s, s1) ∈R.
• s0 ∈S is the initial state.
• AP is a set of atomic propositions.
• L : S →2AP is s labelling function which relates a set L(s) ∈2AP of
atomic propositions to any state s. L(s) corresponds to exactly those atomic
propositions a ∈AP which are satisfied by state s [3, 6, 14].
The notion of a simulation relation can be used to relate a structure to an
abstraction of that structure. If structure M is simulated by structure M ′ then
M is a refinement of M ′. More specifically, given to structures M and M ′ with
AP ⊇AP ′ a relation H ⊆S × S′ is a simulation relation between the two
structures if and only if for all s and s′, if H(s, s′) then
1. L(s) ∩AP ′ = L′(s′).
2. For every state s1 such that R(s, s1), there is a state s′
1 with the property
that R(s′, s′
1) and H(s1, s′
1).
If there exists a simulation relation H such that for the initial states, s0 and
s′
0, H(s0, s′
0), then M ′ simulates M, denoted by M ⪯M ′ [6].
Informally, if M ′ simulates M, then every time something can happen in M, it
can happen in M’, and if two states are related by H such that H(s, s′) then the
conjugation of the atomic propositions which hold in s imply those of s′. Thus,
if M ′ simulates M, and thereby M is a refinement of M ′, any properties shown
to hold in M ′ are also fulfilled in M.
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 3
Related Work
This chapter introduces some pieces of related work to this thesis. Formal veri-
fication of railway interlocking systems has been investigated extensively using
different approaches. In the pieces of work presented here, the interlocking sys-
tems are generally specified as generic models.
Depending on the verification
approach, the generic models may be instantiated with concrete configuration
data describing the railway network and the trains in the network.
3.1
A Formally Verified Distributed Railway Control
System
The article Formal Development and Verification of a Distributed Railway Con-
trol System [16] by Anne Haxthausen and Jan Peleska is used as the basis for
this thesis. Haxthausen et al. present an engineering concept for a geographically
distributed railway interlocking system. With basis in the RAISE formal method
and toolset [12, 15] the interlocking system is modelled in several refinement
steps. The model is successfully verified using theorem proving. As emphasised
by Haxthausen et al. in their conclusion, their verification concept is independ-
ent of the size of the underlying network topology. This is in contrast to using
model checking for verification, with which state space explosion quickly becomes
unmanageable with the increase in complexity of the network.
In Chapter 4 (The Case Study) the engineering concept is presented in depth,
and the verification approach is contrasted with that of this thesis.
3.2
An Extension to RSL-SAL
Jacob Bøgelund Hansen authored the thesis A Formal Specification Language for
Generic Railway Control Systems [13] where a formal specification language based
on RSL-SAL has been defined. The formal specification language supports the
specification of generic models of railway interlocking systems. To facilitate this,
RSL-SAL has been extended with arrays and transition rule prioritisation.
Hansen developed a generic model of an interlocking system, which was in-
stantiated with different examples of configuration data. The instantiations were
==PAGE:34==
18
3 Related Work
translated to SAL and model checked using the SAL symbolic model checker. Per-
formance of the model checking process with SAL was measured and compared
with the performance of the RT-Tester model checking tool. Results indicate that
only smaller examples are checkable within a reasonable amount of time with SAL
and that RT-Tester generally has better performance.
3.3
Model Checking Distributed Railway Control Systems
with UMC
The thesis Model Checking Geographically Distributed Railway Control Systems
[18] by Michel Nielsen is concerned with investigating model checking as a means
of verification of distributed railway control systems. The overall goal of Nielsen’s
thesis is in alignment with that of this thesis. However, the engineering concept of
the interlocking system differs, especially in regards to the control and allocation
scheme. The concept devised by Nielsen allocates the full extent of a train’s route
in the network before starting the train movement and therefore uses a two-phase
commit protocol for the communication between the control components.
Nielsen’s thesis describes a number of experiments on instantiations on the
developed generic model of the interlocking system. The experiments are con-
cerned with the performance of the UMC model checking tool and the scalability
of the model. Even though different real-world systems are modelled in Nielsen’s
thesis and this thesis, respectively, the performance results generated by Nielsen
are still usable for comparison with the results of this thesis. Generally, it seems
that UMC on Nielsen’s model can verify larger and more complex systems than
what has been achieved in this thesis.
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER 4
The Case Study
The starting point of the system modelled in this project is the geographically
distributed interlocking system described in the article Formal Development and
Verification of a Distributed Railway Control System by Anne Haxthausen and
Jan Peleska [16]. The article describes the engineering concept and the control
strategy for a distributed interlocking system to be used in small, local railway
networks. The article served as familiarisation with the railway and railway inter-
locking domain, as explained further in Section 4.1 and Section 4.2. Besides this,
the article is also the inspiration for the control system modelled in this project.
Section 4.3 elaborates on the main differences between the article and this thesis.
One of the advantages of using an already developed model as the basis for
developing a new system model is that the overall domain and control strategy
are in place from the start. This leaves more time available for the modelling task,
for experimentation etc. Thus, it may also be possible to discover other ways of
modelling the same real-world system, which are more optimal in some respects.
Another advantage is that the two system models can be compared – in the case
of this project, model checking will be applied as the verification method, whereas
the article uses theorem proving for verification. Since the same type of system is
modelled in both cases, advantages and disadvantages of each verification method
may be derived.
4.1
Engineering Concept
This section will explain the engineering concept of the geographically distributed
railway interlocking system to be modelled in this project. The physical compon-
ents of the system are briefly introduced in Section 4.1.1, and the idea of the
control protocol is explained in Section 4.1.2.
4.1.1
Physical Components
Physically the system consists of several different components which will be in-
troduced in the following.
==PAGE:36==
20
4 The Case Study
Track Segments
which are the components on which the train drives. The
connected segments form a network.
Points
which are the places where segments are connected with other segments,
i.e. there is a point at a junction, for example. The points can change position,
connecting the stem of the point with either one of its branches. There are also
points at the endpoints of the network, i.e. at the ends of segments which are not
connected to any other segments. These points cannot change their position, of
course.
A Railway Network
consisting of all the connected track segments of the
system. The network dictates how the trains of the system can move.
Trains
which are the entities moving around in the network. The trains must be
kept safe (i.e. prevented from derailments and collisions) by the control strategy.
Sensors
which observe each point and determine whether a train is present or
not.

Figure 4.1: System components.
4.1.2
Control Components and Strategy
The control strategy must ensure the safety of trains, i.e. prevent derailment and
collisions. The strategy presented here is based on not allowing any two trains
to enter the same track segment at the same time and to ensure that points
are positioned correctly before letting trains pass them. This is achieved by the
reservation of segments and the locking of points.
The control components of the system collaborate in order to carry out the
control strategy. The control components consist of train control computers, one
in each train, and switchboxes for each point in the network.
Train Control Computers
The main task of the train control computers is to
issue requests for reserving segments and locking points to the switchboxes. The
train control computers also decide when it is safe for their associated train to
move, and allows the train to do so.
==PAGE:37==
4.1 Engineering Concept
21
Switchboxes
The main task for the switchboxes is to control the point associ-
ated with each of them. The switchboxes also observe the state of their associated
sensor and keeps track of reservations for trains.
The division of responsibility between different control components is essen-
tially what makes the system distributed, rather than centralised. It is clear to
see that the system is geographically distributed since the switchboxes are placed
at different physical locations.
For the control components to make decisions, they must save the relevant
data on which to base their decisions. Therefore, each train control computer and
each switchbox (computer) has their own, local state space. Each train control
computer has the data components shown in Table 4.2. Likewise, each switchbox
has the data components shown in Table 4.3. The values of the data components
comprise the state space for each train control computer and switchbox, respect-
ively.
Table 4.2: The data components of a train in [16]
Train
POS
The train position.
ROUTE-SEGMENTS
An ordered list of the track segments of the route.
ROUTE_SBs
An ordered list of the switchboxes along the route.
RESERVATIONS
A map containing the set of reserved segments at
each switchbox.
LOCKS
A set of switchboxes of which the train has locked
the point.
Table 4.3: The data components of a switchbox in [16]
Switchbox
CONNECTED
The two segments currently connected by the point at
the switchbox.
LOCKED_BY
The identifier of the train (if any) which has locked the
point at the switchbox.
SENSOR
The current value of the sensor (either passive or active),
determining whether a train is at the point.
RES Sn
For each of the segments, Sn, connected to the point of
the switchbox, the train (if any) that has reserved the
segment.
Each train control computer can observe its own state space in order to make
decisions on whether a request for a specific event should be issued. In the same
manner, switchboxes observe their own state space and comply with or deny
==PAGE:38==
22
4 The Case Study
requests based on this information. The events of the system are comprised of
track reservation, locking and switching points and train movement.
Track Segment Reservation
A train must reserve a segment at the relevant
switchboxes before being able to enter that segment. Segments must be reserved
at both the switchboxes which are associated with the segment (i.e. the switch-
boxes at both ends of the segment). When a segment is reserved for a train, no
other trains can reserve that segment before the reservation is released. When
a reservation request is issued by the train control computer, the switchbox will
observe the reservations in its own state space, and based on this either make the
reservation or deny the reservation. If the reservation is made, the switchbox will
update its state space with the reservation, and so will the train control computer.
Locking and Switching Points
A train must lock a point at the relevant
switchbox before being able to pass that point.
The locking of a point also
involves switching the point to the correct position, if necessary. When a point
is locked for a train, no other train can obtain a lock for that point, neither can
the point be switched, before the lock is released. Just as for reservations, when
a lock request is issued by the train control computer, the switchbox will observe
the locked by component in its own state space and make a decision based on this,
to either lock the point for the requesting train, or to deny the request. Before
locking the point, the switchbox will also decide whether the point needs to be
switched (have its position changed) for the train. Switching of a point can only
happen if the associated sensor does not detect any train. Only after the point
has been switched, will it be locked. If the point is locked, the switchbox and
train control computer will update their state spaces with the lock.
Train Movement
When deciding whether it can move, a train observes its own
state space and checks if it has the relevant reservations and the relevant lock.
Only if the next segment (in the train’s route) is reserved at the two upcoming
switchboxes (in the train’s list of switchboxes along its route), and if the train has
a lock for the point at the next switchbox, is the train able to move. When the
train moves, it will pass critical sections. This happens when any part of the train
is in contact with the point (detected by the sensors). While the train is here, the
point must of course not be switched. When the train has fully passed the critical
section (and thereby has passed the switchbox at which it had locked the point),
all reservations and locks at that switchbox for that train are released, both in
the train’s state space and in the state space of the switchbox. Furthermore, the
train route and the train’s list of switchboxes are updated by removing the first
elements (i.e. the segment and the switchbox, respectively, which are now behind
the train).
To summarise, the trains and switchboxes must adhere to the following rules:
==PAGE:39==
4.2 Familiarisation: Drawing an Example
23
• A train must have reservations at all necessary switchboxes before entering
a critical section.
• A train must have a lock for a point at a switchbox before passing that
point. This involves the point being in the correct position.
• A switchbox can only reserve a segment for a train if the segment has not
already been reserved by another train.
• A switchbox can only switch a point when it is not locked.
An example of a sequence of events which comply with the control strategy is
presented in the following Section 4.2.
4.2
Familiarisation: Drawing an Example
In order to ensure a better understanding of the geographically distributed railway
interlocking system and the control strategy used in the project, a step-by-step
illustration with associated explanations was created. The illustration shows an
example of a train driving the extent of its route in a small network, during
which it issues any necessary requests for events, which are then handled by the
switchboxes.
Generally speaking, the train control computers must reserve segments at the
switchboxes before being able to move onto the segments. Furthermore, before
passing a point at a switchbox, the train control computer must have a lock for
the point at the switchbox, such that the point cannot be switched while a train
is on it.
The example is shown in Figure 4.4, where each sub-figure (4.4a-4.4f) is cap-
tioned with a brief description of the events of that step.
Changes from the
previous step are highlighted in yellow. The tables below the switchboxes and
above the train show the variables, or rather, the state space for each switchbox
and train, respectively. Following is also a detailed description of the operations
performed by the train and the switchboxes from each step (i.e. sub-figure of
Figure 4.4) to the next.
From Figure 4.4a to Figure 4.4b
As it can be seen from the ROUTE SEGs, the
train must drive from S1 to S4 via S2. The train starts out with only one reser-
vation at SB1 for its current position, S1. The point of the next switchbox, SB1,
connects S1 and S3, so before the train can move to the next segment, the train
must make a request for switching and locking the point at SB1. Furthermore,
the train must reserve S2 at both SB1 and SB2.
(1) T1 sends a request for reserving S2 to both SB1 and SB2.
(2) SB1 checks whether its RES S2 component is empty.
==PAGE:40==
24
4 The Case Study
(3) Since (2) is true, SB1 changes the value of RES S2 to T1 and returns an
OK to T1.
(4) SB2 checks whether its RES S2 component is empty.
(5) Since (4) is true, SB2 changes the value of RES S2 to T1 and returns an
OK to T1.
(6) T1 updates its RESERVATIONS component accordingly.
(7) T1 sends a request to SB1 for switching the point at SB1 to connect S1
and S2.
(8) SB1 checks whether its CONNECTED component is already connecting S1 and
S2.
(9) Since (8) is false, SB1 checks whether its LOCKED BY component is empty.
(10) Since (9) is true, SB1 changes its CONNECTED component to connect S1 and
S2 and returns an OK to T1.
(11) T1 sends a request to SB1 for locking the point at SB1 for T1.
(12) SB1 checks whether its LOCKED BY component is empty.
(13) Since (12) is true SB1 changes its LOCKED BY component to T1 and returns
an OK to T1.
(14) T1 changes its LOCKS component accordingly.
From Figure 4.4b to Figure 4.4c
T1 is now allowed to move forward, par-
tially onto S2.
(1) T1 checks whether its RESERVATIONS component contains S2 for both SB1
and SB2.
(2) T1 checks whether its LOCKS component contains SB1.
(3) Since (1) and (2) are true, the train can move forward.
The POSITION component is updated to contain both track segments S1 and
S2. The SENSOR component of SB1 changes to active, since a train is detected.
From Figure 4.4c to Figure 4.4d
T1 moves fully onto S2 and has passed
SB1. The SENSOR component of SB1 changes to passive. Since the train has
passed SB1, the switchbox removes T1 from its LOCKED BY, RES S1 and RES
S2 components. Likewise, T1 removes SB1 from its RESERVATIONS and LOCKS
components. Note that this removes the reservation for S2 at SB1 as well. T1
removes the head of both the ROUTE SEGMENTS and the ROUTE SBs lists, and the
POSITION component is updated to S2. T1 must now make a reservation for the
next route segment, S4.
(1) T1 sends a request for reserving S4 to both SB2 and SB3.

(2) SB2 checks whether its RES S4 component is empty.
(3) Since (2) is true, SB2 changes the value of RES S4 to T1 and returns an
OK to T1.

(5) Since (4) is true, SB3 changes the value of RES S4 to T1 and returns an
OK to T1.

removes the head of both the ROUTE SEGMENTS and the ROUTE SBs lists, and the

4.2 Familiarisation: Drawing an Example
29
4.2.1
Experience from the Example
The step-by-step illustration in Figure 4.4 shows a successful execution of a control
scheme and therefore only gives a partial overview. However, it proved very useful:
both acting as a basis for discussions between the author and the supervisor, and
acting as a starting point for the very first formal specification of a model.
The example made it easier to extract logical propositions to be used as guards
for the possible operations of the train.
However, given the simplicity of the
example, it naturally does not cover all of the edge cases which are later discovered
during the modelling process.
During the drawing process, several questions arose. These gave rise to con-
siderations, both about the rules the system must adhere to, but also about how,
specifically, the system should be specified in terms of, for example, data types
and data structures. The example started thoughts about deeper design details
about the underlying logic of the system – not just when should the train be al-
lowed to move forward?, but rather how does the train “know” where it is?. Many
of the answers to these questions were abstracted away from in the article [16] and
thus, simply not answerable by reading it. Following are some essential questions
and the considerations made in regards to them.
How early is the train allowed to make reservations for track segments?
It must be considered whether reservations should only be made in a just in time-
manner, or if they can be made as long as the local state space of the switchbox
allows it (i.e. no other train has reserved the segment already). Clearly, problems
can arise if a train makes reservations far along its route if these reservations
block another train, e.g. coming from the opposite direction, from reserving the
same segments (which might be at the very start of its route). However, reserving
segments only just in time may lead to problems as well: consider the scenario
of two trains driving in opposite directions, which must pass the same segment.
If both trains only make their reservations just as they approach their upcoming
segments, it can result in a deadlock state, which is clearly not desirable.
How does the train “know” when it has passed a critical section?
Is the
train informed by the switchbox (which observes the sensor value) that the train
must now have driven fully past the critical section, or does the train observe,
for example, a GPS tracker by itself? The answer to this question affects how
the system is modelled, even if the specific hardware such as sensor types are
abstracted away from – the system model must still reflect whether the train
observes something in its own state space or is communicating with one or more
switchboxes.
When are reservations (and locks) released?
In the article it is stated
that the lock and all reservations made by the train at a switchbox are released
as soon as the train has passed the switchbox. Safety of the train is ensured
==PAGE:46==
30
4 The Case Study
since trains must have a reservation at, in the case of this example, both relevant
switchboxes, so the train still has a reservation at the switchbox in front of it,
ensuring that no other train can obtain both reservations for the track segment
the train is currently on. However, consider that the article only shows SB1 and
SB2; when the train passes SB2, it will no longer have any reservation for S4.
Therefore, two extra switchboxes SB0 and SB3 were included.
How is it decided which track segment and which switchbox is “next
up”?
The article states that this is handled either by GPS or by track com-
ponent signalling the train about its position. The question, however, is mostly
concerned with how to model this, especially if the model is to abstract away
from such sensors. This invokes a consideration of the data structures used in
the model along with how and when they should be updated. Viewing the train
route and the switchboxes associated with the route as lists, the next segment
for the train is the head of the list when the current position has been removed.
Similarly, the next relevant switchboxes will be given by the head of the list of
switchboxes and the head of the list of the switchboxes with the next switchbox
removed from the list. These lists should be updated, in particular by having the
heads removed, when passing a switchbox.
It should be noted that the example is only concerned with one specific order of
operations for the train and the switchboxes. The intention for the first version of
the formal model of the system is that it should be flexible in terms of the allowed
order of events and not adhere to a restrictive control protocol. For example, the
train should be able to make reservations in any order and not be restricted to
reserving only the upcoming segment.
4.3
Differences
This section presents and emphasises the main differences of this thesis from the
article [16] which was used as inspiration.
The main difference of this thesis is the model checking approach to the veri-
fication of the system model, as opposed to theorem proving, which was used in
the article. This is in line with the purpose of this thesis, which indeed is to
investigate model checking as a verification tool.
In the article, the authors applied a stepwise development paradigm, and
as a result, reached a specification of state spaces (for train control computers
and switchboxes) and functions for system events. Then, system behaviour was
modelled using process algebra.
In this thesis, the specification of state spaces and functions is based on a
study of the article. It should be noted that the full specification from the article
has not been made available to the author of this thesis, and therefore, only
the (sometimes limited) detail provided in the article itself have been accessible.
==PAGE:47==
4.3 Differences
31
From this study, a system representation has been specified independently. The
representation is, of course, similar to that of the article, but differs on a few,
insignificant points. The main difference of the specification produced as part
of this thesis is that it is more suitable for the state transition system to be
modelled.
The way the state transition system is specified affects the model
checking performance, so steps have been taken to optimise the state transition
system specification as much as possible in this regard. See Chapter 9 for some
performance experiments with different specification approaches.
==PAGE:48==
32
==PAGE:49==
CHAPTER 5
Method
This chapter will present the development methods which were applied in this
project. The methods will be described in general terms, i.e. without relating it
to the modelling of a railway interlocking system. The methods presented here
are applicable for modelling state-based systems and could be useful for anyone
else undertaking similar complex modelling tasks.
5.1
Overview
When undertaking any complex development task, well-defined design patterns
or development patterns are essential tools. A development pattern can provide
a systematic approach to a given problem, such that the task can be solved in
a structured manner. This often prevents the developer from making too many
mistakes and results in a thought-out product.
This section presents an overview of the methods used in this project along
with the outcome of each method and their interlinking, cf. Figure 5.1. The
first method applied is an incremental method where stepwise evolutions of a
system model are developed until a model capturing the desired features on an
abstract level is reached. This method is presented in Section 5.2 and referred to
as Method 1 in Figure 5.1.
The outcome of Method 1 is adjusted and bug-fixed such that the desired
properties can be successfully verified using a model checker. This produces a
model which is used as the input for Method 2, presented in Section 5.4. The
method of event refinement is used to transform the input model into a model
closer to the real-world system which is being modelled, i.e. including essential
details from which the model previously abstracted away. The model captures a
general idea of the system in the sense that e.g. precise algorithms are not defined
in the model, but rather, the model captures all legal interleavings of actions.
The general model produced by Method 2 can be fed into Method 3,
presented in Section 5.5, to be concretised. This method should reduce the non-
determinism of the model by defining e.g. a concrete algorithm for controlling
the order of actions.
==PAGE:50==
34

5.2
Incremental Building of a Full System Specification
In order to ease the task of specifying the first draft of a model specification
which includes all the essential features of the system to be modelled, one can
apply an incremental development method. The development method presented
here involves a step-wise evolution of system models, where each evolved version
comes closer to being a full system specification, i.e. a (perhaps very) abstract
specification capturing the desired functionality and properties.
5.2.1
Key Steps
The idea is to start out with an extremely simple, and thereby clear, system
model which does not take every complex feature into account. The first model
should simply include the most basic actions (functions) and entities (types) of
the system.
From there, more complex functionality and any entities needed
for this are gradually added in as many steps as desired. The key steps in the
==PAGE:51==
5.2 Incremental Building of a Full System Specification
35
development method are as follows:
• Create the simplest possible model with only the most basic actions
and entities included. This model version can be further developed in that
small adjustments can be made, but major changes cause the model to
evolve to the next version (see the next item).
• Create a new version as soon as it becomes necessary to make more
radical changes to the model in order to develop it – e.g. redefinitions of
types or the inclusion of a new entity leading to multiple new types and
function specifications.
• Repeat the above item as necessary until a model version capturing the
desired state space and overall features has been reached.
The method shares similarities with the incremental development method
presented in Sommerville’s Software Engineering [22], where the system under
development concurrently undergoes specification, development and validation in
order to finally reach a version of the system, which satisfies the required prop-
erties. The method presented here can also be compared with The RAISE De-
velopment Method [15], which is a stepwise development paradigm. The RAISE
method makes additions to the model in each step and subsequently verifies that
the new specification is a refinement of the former.
The important thing to note is that the method presented here is simply
used as a tool for reaching an initial specification of the system behaviour. This
model can then be checked/verified against the desired properties, and any bugs
or errors can be fixed. The starting specification does not necessarily capture all
system behaviours, and might not take all nuances into account. Therefore, each
new model version is not simply a superset of the former version. A new version
might, for example, alter types in order to add new functionality, which brings
with it changes that cannot necessarily be shown to be refinements of the former
version.
5.2.2
Benefits and Output
An important part to understand about this development method is that it does
not focus on creating each intermediate model version such that they are verifiable
with respect to the desired properties. As mentioned, the method is simply used
as a way of “getting something down on paper”. Therefore, one can approach the
modelling task rather naively, not having to take each of the (non-obvious) edge
cases into account. Of course, one should still attempt to make the models as
correct as possible, but capturing complete correctness during the development
steps is not the essential aim of the method. Rather, it should be seen as a tool
easing the beginning stages of modelling more complex systems, which can be
hard to get a complete overview of.
==PAGE:52==
36
5 Method
The development method should result in a system specification which con-
tains the necessary types and functions for capturing the state space and essential
functionality and behaviour of the system, but which may not include anywhere
near all details of the system. That is, the system specification may be very ab-
stract, and may e.g. hide intermediate steps of actions by modelling them as a
single, atomic action.
5.3
Preparations
The method presented in Section 5.2 produces an abstract model which is not
necessarily correct in terms of satisfaction of e.g. safety properties. One should
attempt to verify the model using a model checking tool, and adjust and correct
the model according to the results until the desired properties (e.g. safety prop-
erties or liveness properties) are fulfilled by the model. This model can then be
used as the starting point for applying the development method explained in 5.4.
5.4
Event Refinement
Going from an abstract model to a model closer to the real-world (a refinement)
can present a challenge. By the nature of modelling and formal specification, a
model will most often abstract away from at least some real-world properties, e.g.
properties which do not directly affect the characteristics of the system that one
is interested in. The method of event refinement or action refinement [19] is a
way of transforming an abstract model, where subdivisions of actions are “hidden
behind” one, major action performing the full set of sub-actions as one atomic
step, into a refined model where the sub-actions are performed as separate actions.
This set of new actions can be allowed to interleave with other actions, or one
can preserve the atomicity of the overall action and not allow any interleaving,
depending on the desired level of abstraction [19].
5.4.1
Key Idea
The key idea of the method is to refine the model at hand by sub-dividing major
actions into its sub-actions. The approach is illustrated by example in Figure
5.2. If the abstract model contains some major action A, changing the state from
s1 to s2, which consists of the hidden sub-actions a1..an, the refined system will
replace A by the sequence of actions a1; a2; ...; an. This may involve adding extra
variables to the state space for keeping track of how far the system is in the
sequence of actions a1; a2; ...; an.
The refinement can be accomplished in two different manners: as an atomic
refinement or a non-atomic refinement. In the atomic refinement approach the
atomicity of the action A is preserved, as no other actions (b1, b2 on Figure 5.2)
==PAGE:53==
5.4 Event Refinement
37
s1
s1
. . .
s2
s1
. . .
s2
s2
Abstract
Atomic refined
Non-atomic refined
A
a1
a2
an
a1
a2
an
b1
b2
b1
b2
b1
b2
b1
b2
b1
b2
Figure 5.2: Atomic and non-atomic refinement of an abstract model. The ma-
jor action A is sub-divided into actions a1..an. s1, s2 denote states which were
present in the abstract model, while the unnamed black nodes denote the new,
intermediate states.
are allowed to interleave with the sub-actions a1..an, i.e. there is a single, unique
path from s1 to s2 via n −1 intermediate states, where n is the number of sub-
actions. From an outside perspective, that means that the unnamed intermediate
states are not observable. It is easy to realise, that any state invariants which may
have been proved for the abstract model, will also hold in the observable states of
the refined model, but not necessarily in the new intermediate states. However,
as the refinement is atomic, and no interleavings with other events are allowed
in the intermediate states, the properties of the system are not compromised, as
long as it can be verified that the sequence of actions a1..an is always completed.
With non-atomic refinement interleavings of other actions and the sub-actions
a1..an are allowed as illustrated on Figure 5.2, but the approach to the partitioning
of action A is the same as for atomic refinement.
The choice between atomic and non-atomic refinement entirely depends on the
desired abstraction level of the refined model and the nature of the system which
is being modelled. For instance, if the system requires mutual exclusion on some
shared resource in order to avoid incorrect behaviour, and the refined actions use
the shared resource, then interleaving with any other actions using the shared
resource should naturally not be allowed. Opposed to this is the case where the
“other actions” (b1, b2 in Figure 5.2) are fully independent on the refined action
==PAGE:54==
38
5 Method
– when this is the case, there is no reason to restrict the interleaving of those
actions.
5.4.2
Considerations and Output
Typically, the event refinement method may result in a model with a larger state
space. The increase in the size of the state space is not, in itself, something to
be desired. However, the refinement is necessary in order to bring the model’s
behaviour closer to that of the real-world system. The intermediate states which
are added to the system when partitioning (refining) actions will often be import-
ant in regard to the verification of the system. It is often in these states that e.g.
safety properties of the system are harder to ensure or verify, simply because of
the problems with events stopping part-way through.
One must consider whether properties should be satisfied in the intermediate
states, or if they can only be ensured when a major action (i.e. a full sequence of
inter-related sub-actions) has completed. This depends on the choice of atomic
or non-atomic refinement. As mentioned previously, in case of atomic refinement,
it is not strictly necessary to be able to verify the system properties in the inter-
mediate states because no interleavings with other actions are allowed to happen.
For non-atomic refinement, however, it is in principle necessary to successfully
verify the intermediate states.
5.5
Restriction
An abstract model version might capture several legal orderings of the actions of
a system. One might have a model with a library of possible system behaviours
which adhere to some rule-set of the system, but which are not restricted to a
certain sequence of actions happening in some specific order. To make the model
more concrete, it is possible to restrict the system model to essentially only model
a specific order of actions which correspond to following a certain control scheme.
5.5.1
Key Idea
The idea of this refinement method is to take, as input, a flexible system model,
i.e. a model which allows several different interleavings of actions and allow only
the subset of actions which adhere to a certain control scheme. This is achieved
by restricting the possible actions of the system by strengthening the guards of
the actions one wishes to restrict. In Figure 5.3a, an example of a system with
multiple possible interleavings of actions is shown. The aim of the method is to
only allow the path through the system illustrated in black in Figure 5.3b.
==PAGE:55==
5.5 Restriction
39
(a) A general and flexible system. The wavy lines illustrate multiple
transitions, possibly with different interleavings.
(b) Event restriction as a chosen path (in black) in the flexible
system of which the rest is in grey).
Figure 5.3: A flexible version of a system model compared to an event restricted
version.
5.5.2
Output
The application of the method should result in a smaller model in terms of state
space, but with more complex guard expressions for the restricted actions.
Given that the input transition system has been verified e.g. with respect to
safety conditions, it is possible with the event restriction approach to be certain of
safety of the event restricted system. This is because its states and transitions are
a subset of the states and transitions of the input system, and each strengthened
guard must imply the corresponding, weaker guard from the input system.
==PAGE:56==
40
==PAGE:57==
CHAPTER 6
Modelling
This section concerns the modelling part of the project. The modelling process
has followed the steps presented in Chapter 5, so there are different versions of
the system model to present with each their own abstraction level. Each model
version is specified as a generic system specification, i.e. without any configuration
data. Each model version can be instantiated with configuration data, and these
instantiations of the generic models are needed for the verification and model
checking process (presented in Chapter 8). The configuration data is a dataset
describing the network, trains (and their data) and switchboxes (and their data).
In this chapter, the generic models will be presented and explained, and for any
types and variables where it is relevant, an example of a possible instantiation will
be given. For these example instantiations, the same small example configuration
will be used. The configuration data will describe the small network along with
the single train and the switchboxes presented in Chapter 4.
Chapter Overview
First, an overview of the different system models will be
given, providing a context in which the models should be viewed. The system
models will be related to the methods presented in Chapter 5. Before presenting
the model specifications in more detail, the overall abstractions and assumptions
made for all the models related to this project will be introduced and motivated
in Section 6.2. Then, Section 6.3 will explain the path to building a full system
specification.
The first full system specification will be explained in depth in
Section 6.4, and the refined versions of this will be explained in Section 6.5 and
6.6.
6.1
Overview of the System Models
Three different system models will be presented in this chapter. Overall, the aim
has been to experiment with keeping the system models as general and flexible
as possible by specifying property oriented transition rules. In other words, the
system has been specified in terms of a set of rules which, when observed, ensure
the continuous safety of the system. This approach is opposed to the one of spe-
cifying a system which follows a particular control algorithm. The last refinement
==PAGE:58==
42
6 Modelling
of the system specification (Section 6.6) has, however, been restricted such that
the only possible transitions correspond to following a control protocol.
Section 6.3 briefly presents the building of a first full system specification based
on Method 1 as presented in Section 5.2. A model version with a very abstract
view of the train interlocking system is presented in Section 6.4.
The model
abstracts away from the actual communication signals sent between train control
computers and switchboxes. The model is refined using Method 2 as presented
in Section 5.4, which results in a model with explicit communication between
control components. However, the model is still kept general and flexible. This
refined version of the model is presented in Section 6.5. Finally, the refined model
including communication is refined further by restricting the possible events of
the system, following Method 3 as presented in Section 5.5. This restriction
reduces the number of possible events at a given time in the system and forces
the system behaviour to follow a specific event order.
