6.2
Abstractions and Assumptions
This section will introduce the overall abstractions and assumptions which have
been made and are common for all the model versions of this project. Abstractions
are necessary since not all real-world circumstances can be captured in a formally
specified model of a system.
Abstractions, along with assumptions, can also
==PAGE:59==
6.2 Abstractions and Assumptions
43
contribute to reducing the complexity of a model. For example, an abstraction
can “hide” a complex part of a real-world system which is assumed not to have
an effect on the part of the system to be verified.
When making assumptions, one must carefully consider whether assuming a
particular property of a system model will limit the usefulness of the model in the
sense that one can end up assuming a property which, when fulfilled, will more
or less guarantee some safety property of the system. For example, if a model
assumes that trains never crash, then that model becomes useless for proving the
safety property of no collisions. Assumptions are often necessary, however, since
models quickly become very complex if they are to take all external influences on
the system into account.
The system models all generally abstract away from or assume the following
factors. The reasoning is explained for each abstraction or assumption.
Sensors as separate entities
The sensors, which are used to detect the passing
of trains, have been modelled as part of the switchboxes. The sensors are, in fact,
separate entities communicating with the switchboxes. However, it is assumed
that the communication between sensors and switchboxes is instant and therefore
it is reasonable to consider them as a single entity. The communication between
the switchboxes and the sensors happen over a wired connection, so considering
the communication signals as instantaneous is not far from reality.
Points as separate entities
The points which can be switched are modelled as
part of the switchboxes as well. In the real-world system, the switchboxes control
the points, but the points are not directly part of the switchbox entity.
The
switchboxes must communicate with the points when they need to be switched
– the switchbox must send a signal to the point, requesting a switch, which is
then (in the successful case) acknowledged by the point, and only then can the
switchbox return with an acknowledgement to the train. This abstraction assumes
that the switching of a point is instant, which is not the case in the real world. The
communication itself can be assumed to be instant, similarly to that between the
switchboxes and the sensors, as the communication happens over a wired channel.
However, since the physical components of the point must move from one position
to another the switching of a point must take a non-negligible amount of time.
It is clear, then, that this abstraction is quite a simplification, but it should not
affect the safety of the system since it simply changes whether the switchbox
must communicate with the point before sending an acknowledgement to the
train or if the switchbox can directly update its own state space and send the
acknowledgement.
Exact train location
The precise location of each train in the network is
abstracted away from. The models are simply concerned with the current position
of each train in terms of which track segment(s) the train occupies and whether
==PAGE:60==
44
6 Modelling
the train is in a critical section.
It is assumed that trains are always shorter
than the track segments and the trains are assumed to be longer than the switch
points.
This means that a train can at most touch two sections at the time,
and e.g. cannot be in two critical sections at the same time. If a train is not
in a critical section, then it is on one single track segment. The abstraction is
very valuable since the complexity of a model with precise train placement would
be immense. In order to verify the absence of derailment and collisions, merely
modelling the current position in terms of a track segment and whether each train
is in a critical section or not is sufficient. It does have the effect, though, that only
one train can reside on a track segment at the time – depending on the length of
the track segments, this can be perfectly acceptable or less desirable. The latter
being the case e.g. if track segments are multiple kilometres long and trains are
relatively short compared to that length. At the very least, this would pose an
inconvenience regarding the scheduling of trains since multiple trains would easily
be able to fit on a single segment.
The granularity of information about the train position in the model matches
the amount of knowledge normally accessible to an interlocking system.
The
interlocking system is typically only able to observe whether trains are somewhere
in a critical section or they are fully outside any critical section. Thus, the system
does not differentiate between the different train placements shown in Figure 6.2
– as long as some part of the train is in the critical section, the train is considered
to be in the critical section.
Critical section
Figure 6.2: Three different train positions, all viewed as the train being located
in the critical section.
The assumption about the train lengths is reasonable for the modelling of
small, local train networks. However, should one wish to extend the modelling
to include regional lines, this assumption should be reconsidered since trains on
these lines may be several hundred metres long.
Speed and time
Much in the same manner that the precise placement of
trains would make the model of the system unnecessarily complex, so would the
inclusion of factors such as the speed of the train and time in the system. The
speed of the train is a very relevant factor in the real-world system since it affects
the last point where the train has time to brake if it does not have permission to
enter the upcoming track segment. However, since the models are not concerned
==PAGE:61==
6.2 Abstractions and Assumptions
45
with the fine-grained position of the train, the braking distance becomes rather
meaningless. Rather, the train “braking” is simply modelled by the train not
being able to move onto the upcoming segment.
Trains are assumed to have
Automatic Train Protection systems installed which brake the trains, should they
come near the last braking point.
Time would be an especially meaningful factor if one were concerned with the
scheduling of trains. These models are, however, not, and time has been left out
of the system.
Sensor extent
In a real-world system, the detection of whether trains are in a
critical section is handled by e.g. axle counters placed on each side of the critical
section. Simply put, the first axle counter passed by a train reaches some count,
and the train is known to have left the critical section when the next axle counter
reaches the same count. The model of the system abstracts away from this by
simply having a built-in sensor unit for each switchbox which is either active or
passive, depending on whether a train is in the critical section or not. This means
that the sensors, and thereby the critical sections, are viewed as having no extent.
In other words, they are viewed as (geometric) points, rather than segment pieces,
cf. Figure 6.3.
Critical section
(a) Sensors/critical sections as points as viewed in the model.
Critical section
(b) Critical sections with an extent as in the real-world system.
Figure 6.3: Model view of sensors/critical sections vs. a representation of the
real-world system.
Similarly to the assumption about the train lengths, this is a reasonable as-
sumption for small, local networks, but should be reconsidered for larger regional
networks.
Track Directions
As mentioned in Section 2.1.1, the track segments may be
directional such that trains are only allowed to drive in a specific direction (either
up or down) on that particular track.
This is not taken into account in the
==PAGE:62==
46
6 Modelling
system models of this thesis. Effectively, this means that all track segments are
bi-directional and that trains can drive in either direction on all track segments
of the railway network.
6.3
From Example to Full System Specification
One of the major challenges of this project was how to approach the modelling
task. Another important task was to get a feel for the technical tools to be used
in the project, such as the RSL-SAL language, the SAL model checker and other
features of SAL.
Writing a formal specification, even for a relatively small system, is not a
trivial task. The complexity of the full system specification for the geographic-
ally distributed railway interlocking system, which this project is concerned with,
makes the task of writing the specification from start to end infeasible. The limit-
ations of the expressiveness of RSL-SAL compared to RSL, mentioned in Section
2.3.3, add an extra layer of difficulty to the modelling task. Therefore, the method
of incrementally building a full system specification, as presented in Section 5.2,
was applied.
The starting point was to model a very simple system specification without
switchboxes (and thereby without reservations, switching and locks). The system
thereby omits critical sections and, really, any interlocking functionality.
The
only possible operation is train movement, only restricted by the route specified
for the train and whether the train tries to enter a track segment not connected
to its current position. The specification for this system with the network and
train data as shown in 4.4 can be found in Appendix G.
One could argue that this first model without any interlocking functionality
does not seem very useful in this context since the goal is to model an interlocking
system. However, along with establishing the basis for the incremental building of
a full system specification, it was useful for familiarising oneself the workarounds
for avoiding the non-translatable data structures and built-in types from RSL.
Furthermore, the model was used to experiment with the syntax of transition
systems and LTL assertions in RSL-SAL (even though this is not part of the
method presented in Section 5.2).
In total, four model versions were specified as part of the application of
Method 1. The models will not be explained in depth since they merely serve as
tools for reaching a full system specification with the desired interlocking features,
which can then be model checked, verified and refined. A brief overview of the
model versions is given in Figure 6.4, where the model versions are referred to as
M1-M4.
• M1 is the simplest model it was possible to come up with which still cap-
tured some system functionality, and essentially the only functionality in-
cluded in the model is the movement of trains.
==PAGE:63==

• M2 is an attempt at including most of the needed types for future versions.
This version does not actually add any new functionality.
• M3 adds functionality for reservation of track segment and switching and
locking of points.
• M4 adds final details such as allowing trains to move into and out of critical
sections. Also adds a description of the placement of the switchboxes in the
network and their associated segments.

6.4
V1: First System Model
This section concerns the first full system model, i.e. model V 1. This model
specification can be divided into different parts, each with its own purpose. Each
part will be described in detail in the following sections. The model consists of
two specification files: one for the base functionality, i.e. containing the type spe-
cifications and function specifications, and one for the transition system variables,
rules and assertions. The generic base specification can be found in Appendix A.1,
and the generic specification for the transition system can be found in Appendix
A.2.
The system is specified such that the order of the operations (move, reserve)
etc. are not dependent on a certain control protocol. The rules of the system
must of course still be observed, so a train must have e.g. the correct reservation
before being able to move. Thus, there is no specific ordering of the operations
but rather, properties are specified which must be fulfilled before executing a
certain operation. This is mirrored nicely in a transition system in RSL-SAL,
==PAGE:64==
48
6 Modelling
with the properties used as guards for the transitions, and the operations as the
transition effects.
The system model at this point is very abstract. In fact, the specified model
is abstract to such a level that it can be said to correspond to a centralised
control computer with a distributed state space, rather than actually distributed
control computers. Any operations happening in the transition system change
both the state space of the train control computer and the switchbox in question
in the same atomic action.
This is clearly not an accurate representation of
a geographically distributed railway interlocking system, especially since it hides
some of the challenges introduced by such a distributed approach. For example, a
distributed interlocking system would need some concept of communication where
trains can request reservations and switchboxes can respond to such requests,
both with a “successful” answer and an “unsuccessful” one. Such communication,
rather than atomically changing both state spaces, introduces new intermediate
states, e.g. “midway” in a reservation where all desired properties of the system
may not hold.
6.4.1
Types and Values for Static Configuration Data
Several types have been defined to describe the static configuration data of the
system. The static configuration data contains data about the network configur-
ation, including information about the placement of switchboxes, and the static
information about trains and switchboxes (i.e. which trains and switchboxes are
included in the configuration).
6.4.1.1
The Track Network
The network consists of interconnected segments. A segment is a linear piece
of train track which connect to other segments in points. A type representing
segments is declared:
type Segment
A type representing connections between each of the segments is declared:
type Connection ==
conn(fst : Segment, snd : Segment) |
endpoint(e : Segment)
The type is defined as a variant type such that it can either be used to describe
an actual connection between two segments or to describe that a segment is an
endpoint (i.e. not connected to another segment in one end).
The network configuration itself is a value of a type consisting of several con-
nections:
==PAGE:65==
6.4 V1: First System Model
49
type Network = Connection-set
value network : Network
The network is oriented, and it is assumed that all segment connections of the
network must all be given in the same direction: the up-direction. Even though
all track segments are bi-directional, it is still necessary to know which direction
is the up-direction to be able to deduce the network configuration from the given
data.
In the generic version of the specification, the segment type is simply given
as a sort, and the network value is not initialised. In order to create an instanti-
ation of the model, one must further specify these. For the example system, the
initialisations would look as follows:
type Segment == s1 | s2 | s3 | s4
value network : Network =
{conn(s1,s2),conn(s1,s3),conn(s2,s4),conn(s3,s4),
endpoint(s1),endpoint(s4)}
The segment type enumerates the possible values of the type, and the network
value determines the actual configuration of the segments. As can be seen, e.g.
s1 and s2 are connected, and s1 is also an endpoint. Since the network is given
in the up-direction, it can be deduced that s1 has no connection on its down-side
and is connected to s2 on its up-side.
The network value must satisfy several wellformedness conditions which will
be specified and explained below, in Section 6.4.3.
6.4.1.2
Switchboxes
Similarly to how the segments of the system must be defined along with a de-
scription of how they are interconnected, the switchboxes of the system must also
be defined, and their placement in the network described. For representing the
switchboxes of the system, a type is defined:
type SwitchboxID
For the example instantiation of the system, the type would be initialised with
the possible switchbox identifiers:
type SwitchboxID == sb0 | sb1 | sb2 | sb3
Each switchbox is placed at a point, i.e. at the connections between each of the
segments of the network. Switchboxes are also placed at the endpoints of segments.
==PAGE:66==
50
6 Modelling
A switchbox can either be associated with three segments (at junctions), two
segments (e.g. at crossings) or only a single segment (at endpoints). A type is
defined for describing which segments are related to a switchbox:
type SwitchboxConnection ::
stem : Segment
branches : Segset
The type is a record (since product types are not translatable to SAL), and
consists of a stem segment and some number of branches in a set of segments. The
placement of the switchboxes are given by defining which segments are associated
with each switchbox identifier using the following type:
type SwitchboxDesc = SwitchboxID →
m SwitchboxConnection
value switchboxes : SwitchboxDesc
The value of this type would, for the example instantiation, be initialised as
follows:
value switchboxes : SwitchboxDesc =
[sb0 7→mk_SwitchboxConnection(s1,{}),
sb1 7→mk_SwitchboxConnection(s1,{s2,s3}),
sb2 7→mk_SwitchboxConnection(s4,{s2,s3}),
sb3 7→mk_SwitchboxConnection(s4,{})]
As can be seen, the switchboxes sb0 and sb3 have no branches since they are
placed at the endpoint ends of the segments s1 and s4, respectively. The two other
switchboxes each have two branches since they are placed at junctions. Note that
these “switchbox connections” are not given in the up-direction – if they were,
the stem would always have to point to the left, and the branches to the right.
6.4.1.3
Trains
Finally, a type for the trains of the system has been defined:
type TrainID == t_none | _
In the generic version of the model, the TrainID type is a variant type with at
least a single possible value, namely t_none. This value represents the absence
of any train and is needed in the specification of several functions which will be
explained below. For the example system, the type would have an additional
value:
==PAGE:67==
6.4 V1: First System Model
51
type TrainID == t_none | t1
That is, the type will have an additional value for each train in the instance
of the system.
6.4.2
Types for Dynamic Configuration Data
Beyond the static configuration data regarding the network, placement of switch-
boxes etc. the system is also configured with dynamic data which changes e.g.
when trains move. The values of these data types collectively comprise the state
space of the system. The types can be divided into those associated with trains
and those associated with switchboxes.
6.4.2.1
Train Control Computer
A train control computer’s state space contains information about the train’s
position, route, switchboxes along the route, locks and reservations. For each of
these a type has been defined.
The position of a train is either given by a single segment (the train is not
in a critical section), or two segments (the train is in a critical section between
those two segments, where the front of the train is in the second segment of the
position, i.e. seg2 below). A type describing this has been defined:
type Position ==
single (seg : Segment) | double(seg1 : Segment, seg2 : Segment)
A train’s route is simply a list of segments along which the train should drive.
However, since lists are not translatable to SAL, the route type has been defined
as a mapping from segment to segment:
type Route = Segment →
m Segment
Since maps are, as opposed to lists, unordered, the next segment for the train
to enter is not as simple as taking the head of the list. Instead, the train’s position
can be used as an “entry pointer” to the next segment. Using the segment of the
position (second segment in case of a double position) as a key in the route map,
the corresponding value will be the next segment in the route.
The switchboxes along a train’s route is also simply a list of switchbox iden-
tifiers, so the type has, like the route, been defined as a mapping, now from
SwitchboxID to SwitchboxID:
type SboxMap = SwitchboxID →
m SwitchboxID
==PAGE:68==
52
6 Modelling
In this case, the train does not have any information to use as the “entry
pointer” for the map, so the notion of a next switchbox has been introduced for
the train. This value is simply of the type SwitchboxID, and is used in the same
manner as the segment of the position in the route map.
The train’s locks are an unordered collection of switchboxes where the train
has currently locked the point. The following type is defined:
type Sboxset = SwitchboxID-set
Finally, the train’s reservations are collections of reserved segments for each
switchbox (identifier).
The following type, where Segset is a Segment-set, is
defined:
type ResMap = SwitchboxID →
m Segset
For modelling the state spaces of each train control computer types of the form
TrainID →
m [value type] have been defined, where the value type are of the types
just presented:
type
TRouteState = TrainID →
m Route,
TPosState = TrainID →
m Position,
TNextsbState = TrainID →
m SwitchboxID,
TSboxesState = TrainID →
m SboxMap,
TResState = TrainID →
m ResMap,
TLocksState = TrainID →
m Sboxset
The maps are a way of specifying, for each train identifier, which route, pos-
ition etc. is associated with that identifier. For example, the map TRouteState
will hold a mapping from a train identifier to the route of that train for each of
the trains of the system. The maps are simply an abstraction, and the value from
each of the maps, using some train identifier as key, is the state space for that
corresponding train control computer.
If these maps were merged into one, the type specification could look as follows:
type
TState = TrainID →
m TrainData,
TrainData ::
route : Route
position : Position
nextsb : SwitchboxID
sboxes : SboxMap
reservations : ResMap
locks : Sboxset
==PAGE:69==
6.4 V1: First System Model
53
This way of structuring the data is however not advisable when attempting
to model check the system. As mentioned in Section 2.3.3, Chris George advises
that only a few variables are changed per transition rule. The use of the above
mapping would cause all of the variables of a train data value to be updated, even
if only e.g. the position had to be changed, since the record type specification of
the train data must be updated by using the mk_TrainData constructor.
It would also be possible to have multiple variables for each part of the train
data and for each train, such that, for example, there would be two route variables,
route1 and route2, if there were two trains in the system. This approach cannot
be made generic, however, and all the variables would have to be declared when
instantiating the system. It would also mean that transition rules could not be
specified in the same generic manner as they are now (see Section 6.4.7). The
advantage of the maps specified above is that they are generic.
Variables of the state types are used when creating instances of the generic
models by entering concrete specification data. The variables are specified as local
variables in the transition system. This will be further elaborated on in Section
6.4.6.
6.4.2.2
Switchbox
The state space of the switchbox contains information about the segments cur-
rently connected at the point of the switchbox (if any), the sensor state, the lock
and the reservations. For each of these a type has been defined.
The currently connected segments at the point of a switchbox is given by the
connection type described earlier. A switchbox is associated with either a point
(i.e. junction) or an endpoint. Using the connection variant type here allows the
switchbox to either connect two segments given by the conn value of the type or
simply be placed at an endpoint by using the endpoint value of the type.
The lock of a switchbox is simply given by a train identifier representing the
train which currently has a lock for the point at the switchbox. Therefore, no
new type is needed. The aforementioned value of the TrainID type, t_none is
used when no train has a lock for the point at the switchbox.
The switchbox sensor detects whether a train is in the critical section (i.e.
overlaps the point) of a switchbox. Depending on whether a train is present or
not, the sensor is either active or passive. A variant type is defined:
type Sensor == active | passive
Finally, the reservations of a switchbox are given for each of the segments
associated with the switchbox.
The identifier of the train which has reserved
the segment is given for each segment. If no train has reserved a segment, the
value t_none is instead given. A type which is a mapping from segments to train
identifiers is defined:
==PAGE:70==
54
6 Modelling
type SbResMap = Segment →
m TrainID
For modelling the state spaces of each switchbox, types of the form Switch-
boxID →
m [value type] have been defined, where the value types are of the types
just presented:
type
SbConnState = SwitchboxID →
m Connection,
SbLockState = SwitchboxID →
m TrainID,
SbSensorState = SwitchboxID →
m Sensor,
SbResState = SwitchboxID →
m SbResMap
Similarly to the maps for the trains’ state spaces, these maps are a way of
specifying, for each switchbox identifier, which data is associated with that iden-
tifier.
In the same manner as for trains, variables of these types are used when
creating instances of the generic models and are specified as local variables in the
transition system.
6.4.3
Wellformedness and Consistency Functions
The configuration data for instantiating the model must fulfil certain requirements
of wellformedness and consistency.
Wellformedness is the notion of the data
“making sense” and e.g. forming a meaningful network, whereas consistency has
to do with data being in “mutual agreement”. The wellformedness and consistency
properties are invariants of the system, so choosing a transition from a state where
all wellformedness and consistency properties hold should result in going to a state
where the properties also hold.
The properties have been formulated as functions each concerned with the
wellformedness or consistency of a specific part of the system. The functions will
be used when writing assertions about the transition system, see Section 6.4.8.
The wellformedness functions can be divided into two kinds. First, there are
the wellformedness functions for the static data, i.e. the network configuration
and the switchbox description. These wellformedness functions can be verified
once and for all since the data is static. Therefore they do not strictly need to be
model checked but can simply be verified to hold via e.g. SML testing.
The other wellformedness functions are concerned with the wellformedness of
the dynamic configuration data with respect to the static configuration data. For
example, the wellformedness of the route of a train should be ensured with respect
to the network.
The consistency functions check the mutual consistency of the dynamic data,
i.e. the state variables, both from different state spaces and from the same state
==PAGE:71==
6.4 V1: First System Model
55
space. The consistency functions are used to ensure that e.g. the reservation data
for a train agrees with the reservation data for each of the switchboxes.
6.4.3.1
Network Wellformedness
The static network configuration data must adhere to the following wellformedness
properties:
• The network must not be empty.
• The network connections must be anti-symmetric, i.e.
if the connection
(s1, s2) is specified, then the connection (s2, s1) must not be a network
connection.
• The network connections and endpoints must all be directional with respect
to the network. See further explanation below.
• The network must form a completed network (which can technically be
several disjunct completed networks). See further explanation below.
• The network endpoints must only be connected to connections in one end –
otherwise they are not actual endpoints. See further explanation below.
Connection Directionality
As stated earlier, the network connections are as-
sumed to be given in the up-direction. Only with this assumption is it possible
to infer the orientation of the network components (i.e. the segments). Further-
more, it must be ensured that the trains do not suddenly change direction – a
train driving in the up-direction should keep this direction for the entirety of
its route. This is where the notion of directionality of the network connections
becomes relevant.
For the connections of the network to be directionally correct, there cannot
exist in the network a “turn-around” loop such as the one shown in Figure 6.5.
In this case, the connections are specified such that one can drive along s2 and
s4 going in the up-direction, but once entering s3 one is driving against the
up-direction. Since the direction of the segments and connections are not given
explicitly, other characteristics of connections forming such an illegal network
configuration must be identified.
Some of the network components can be excluded from the examination de-
termining the correct directionality. First of all, endpoints are always directional
since they simply define an end of a segment not being connected to another
segment. They can be excluded from further examination. Furthermore, if a con-
nection is connected to only endpoints, it is automatically directional – nothing
can go wrong in regards to directionality in this case since there cannot exist a
loop. If a connection is only connected to one other connection (i.e. is connected
to an endpoint in the other end), then it is also always directional since this pre-
vents a loop with that connection involved as well. Therefore, it is sufficient to
==PAGE:72==
56
6 Modelling
SB0
SB1
SB2
SB3
SB4
s1
s2
s5
s3
s4
up
Figure 6.5: An example of a network with connections breaking the directionality
requirement.
check whether a connection is connected to an endpoint to exclude the connection
from further examinations.
All other connections must be checked further. Looking at the example on
Figure 6.5, the problem should be identified by examining the connections (s2, s4)
and (s4, s3) in relation to the rest of the network. The network configuration is
specified as follows:
{(s1), (s5), (s1, s2), (s1, s3), (s2, s4), (s2, s5), (s4, s3)}
(6.1)
The connection (s2, s4) in itself is completely legal – it is the connection (s4, s3)
which constitutes the problem. Considering this connection in relation to the
other connections, it becomes clear that the problem is that it connects the up-
ends of two other connections. More formally, if there exists in the network two
connections (s, t) and (u, v) then both the connection (t, v) and the connection
(v, t) is illegal.
Note that there is more than one way of specifying the network shown on
Figure 6.5 depending on which connection is “chosen” to be the problematic one.
The configuration could be specified as in (6.1) or e.g. as follows in (6.2) and (6.3).
All the faulty configurations, however, will be caught by the presented logic.
{(s1), (s5), (s1, s2), (s2, s4), (s2, s5), (s4, s3), (s3, s1)}
(6.2)
{(s1), (s5), (s1, s2), (s1, s3), (s2, s4), (s2, s5), (s3, s4)}
(6.3)
Network Completeness
In order for the network to be complete each network
connection must be connected to either another connection or an endpoint in each
end, i.e. for each segment of the connection, the network must contain either an
endpoint of that segment or another connection with that segment. An example
of a network breaking this rule is shown in Figure 6.6a.
Furthermore, each endpoint must be connected to a connection, i.e. two end-
points cannot “stand alone” as shown in Figure 6.6b. The two examples from
Figure 6.6 are missing a connection between the endpoints, and the endpoints
==PAGE:73==
6.4 V1: First System Model
57
s1
s2

network {(s1, s2)}.

for simplicity.
themselves, respectively. A minimal network with the two segments s1, s2 can
be specified as
{(s1), (s1, s2), (s2)}
(6.4)
It should be noted that a complete network may well contain several (also com-

perfectly legal.
