Endpoints
The endpoints of the network must be actual endpoints. This means
that for each endpoint, the other connections connecting to the endpoint must
only connect to the endpoint in exclusively the up-direction, or exclusively in
the down-direction. Consider, for example, the following network configuration
specification:
{(s0), (s1), (s2), (s0, s1), (s1, s2)}
(6.5)
In the network configuration shown in (6.5), the endpoint (s1) is included in
both connections (s0, s1) and (s1, s2) – in one connection the endpoint is in the
up-direction, whereas in the other, the endpoint is in the down-direction. From
the connections it seems that the segment s1 has connections in both ends, but
this means that the endpoint (s1) must not be correctly specified.
Given the observations made above, the network wellformedness function con-
sists of the following propositions.
• The non-emptiness of the network is expressed by
==PAGE:74==
58
6 Modelling
net ̸= {}
• The anti-symmetry of the connection is expressed by
(∀connection : Connection • connection ∈net ⇒
case connection of
conn(segm1,segm2) →conn(segm2,segm1) /∈net
...)
• The directionality of the network connections is expressed by
(∀connection : Connection • connection ∈net ⇒
case connection of
conn(segm1,segm2) →is_conn_directional(connection,net)
...)
where the auxiliary function is_conn_directional
– Returns true if the connection is connected to an endpoint.
– Otherwise, checks if there exists two other connections such that the
given connection connects their up-direction segments.
• The completeness of the network is expressed by
(∀connection : Connection • connection ∈net ⇒
is_connected(connection,net))
where the auxiliary function is_connected
– For connections checks that for both segments of the connection there
exists another connection or endpoint with which that segment is
shared.
– For endpoints checks that there exists a connection where the segment
of the endpoint is included.
• The endpoint connection correctness is expressed by
(∀connection : Connection • connection ∈net ⇒
case connection of
endpoint(e) →
(∼is_fst_in_conn(e,net) ∧is_snd_in_conn(e,net)) ∨
(is_fst_in_conn(e,net) ∧∼is_snd_in_conn(e,net))
... )
==PAGE:75==
6.4 V1: First System Model
59
where the auxiliary functions is_fst_in_conn and is_snd_in_conn check,
respectively, whether a segment is part of a connection as the down-direction
or part of a connection as the up-direction.
Altogether this results in the following specification of the network wellformed-
ness function.
network_wf : Network →Bool
network_wf(net) ≡
net ̸= {} ∧
(∀connection : Connection • connection ∈net ⇒
is_connected(connection,net) ∧
case connection of
conn(segm1,segm2) →
conn(segm2,segm1) /∈net ∧
is_conn_directional(connection,net),
endpoint(e) →
(∼is_fst_in_conn(e,net) ∧is_snd_in_conn(e,net)) ∨
(is_fst_in_conn(e,net) ∧∼is_snd_in_conn(e,net))
end)
6.4.3.2
Switchbox Description Wellformedness
The static switchbox configuration data, i.e. the specification of the placement of
the switchboxes in the network, must adhere to the following properties, which
will be explained in depth below:
• The segments associated with each switchbox must be adjacent in the net-
work.
• The switchbox description must be exactly complete, i.e. contain switch-
boxes for all endpoints and connections in the network, but not contain
switchboxes which cannot be “matched” with a connection or endpoint. As
a consequence, the switchbox description must not contain duplicate switch-
box connections.
• The switchbox description cannot contain duplicate switchbox connections
for different switchboxes.
Stem and Branches Adjacency
Each switchbox connection is given as a
stem segment and a (possibly empty) set of branch segments. Clearly, in order
for the switchbox description to be wellformed with respect to the network, each
(stem, branch)-pair of each switchbox must indeed be neighbouring segments in
==PAGE:76==
60
6 Modelling
the network. In other words, the segments must pairwise be included as connec-
tions in the network, either as the connection (stem, branch) or as the connection
(branch, stem).
Switchbox Description Completeness
The switchbox description specifies
the placement of the switchboxes in the network. There must exist a correspond-
ing switchbox for each connection and endpoint in the network for the switchbox
description to be complete since, by definition, the switchboxes are placed at
each adjoining segment and at each endpoint. The converse must also hold, such
that each switchbox can be “paired” with exactly one connection or endpoint in
the network. Finally, no two switchboxes can have the same switchbox connec-
tion since this would mean two switchboxes pairing with the same connection or
endpoint.
Unique Switchbox for Each Point
The switchbox description is only well-
formed if there exists one, and exactly one, switchbox for each point.
There-
fore, two different switchboxes cannot have the same switchbox connection in the
switchbox description. That would correspond to two switchboxes at the same
point in the network, and that way, there is not a unique switchbox responsible
for the segments connecting at the point.
The resulting specification of the switchbox description wellformedness func-
tion is as follows, where the first three lines of the function body concern the
adjacency of segments in each switchbox connection, the two middle quantifica-
tions concern the completeness of the switchbox description, and the final lines
concern the uniqueness of switchbox connections in the switchbox description.
sb_desc_wf : SwitchboxDesc × Network →Bool
sb_desc_wf(sbdesc,net) ≡
(∀sbconn : SwitchboxConnection • sbconn ∈rng(sbdesc) ⇒
(∀seg : Segment • seg ∈branches(sbconn) ⇒
are_neighbors_in_net(stem(sbconn),seg,net)))
∧
(∀c : Connection • c ∈net ⇒
(∃sbconn : SwitchboxConnection •
sbconn ∈rng(sbdesc) ∧
conn_matches_sb_conn(c,sbconn)))
∧
(∀sbconn : SwitchboxConnection •
sbconn ∈rng(sbdesc) ⇒
∃c : Connection • c ∈net ∧
conn_matches_sb_conn(c,sbconn)))
∧
(∀sbid : SwitchboxID • sbid ∈dom(sbdesc) ⇒
==PAGE:77==
6.4 V1: First System Model
61
∼(∃sbid2 : SwitchboxID • sbid2 ∈dom(sbdesc) ∧
sbid2 ̸= sbid ∧
sbdesc(sbid) = sbdesc(sbid2)))
The auxiliary function are_neighbors_in_net simply checks whether the two
given segments are neighbours in either direction in the network.
The auxiliary function conn_matches_sb_conn checks that a connection or
endpoint matches a switchbox connection, i.e.
• For an endpoint checks that the given switchbox connection’s stem should
match the segment of the endpoint and have no branches.
• For a connection checks that the given switchbox connection allows the
switchbox to connect the two segments of the connection, i.e. one of the
segments is the stem of the switchbox connection, and the other segment is
in the set of branches.
6.4.3.3
Train Route Wellformedness
For each train, the dynamic train route data should be wellformed both in itself,
but also with respect to the static network configuration data. The route must
adhere to the following wellformedness properties:
• The route must be connected.
Each (key, value)-pair in the route map
must at least overlap one other (key, value)-pair (except if it is the only
(key, value)-pair in the route), i.e. the key of the pair must be the value in
another pair or vice versa.
• The route must correspond to a sequence of segments in the network, i.e.
each (key, value)-pair must be neighbours in the network.
The resulting specification of the route wellformedness function is as follows.
route_wf : Route × Network →Bool
route_wf(route,net) ≡
(∀seg : Segment • seg ∈dom(route) ⇒
are_neighbors_in_net(seg,route(seg),net) ∧
(segments_of_route(route\{seg}) = {} ∨
{seg,route(seg)} ∩segments_of_route(route\{seg}) ̸= {}))
The auxiliary function are_neighbors_in_net has been explained above, and
the auxiliary function segments_of_route simply extracts the segments of the
route map as a set.
==PAGE:78==
62
6 Modelling
6.4.3.4
Train Position Wellformedness
For each train, the dynamic train position data should be wellformed with re-
spect to the static network configuration data. The position must adhere to the
following wellformedness properties:
• For a single segment position, the network must contain a connection which
contains that segment.
• For a double segment position, the two segments must be neighbours in the
network.
The resulting specification of the position wellformedness function is as follows.
pos_wf : Position × Network →Bool
pos_wf(pos,net) ≡
case pos of
single (seg) →(∃conn : Connection • conn ∈net ∧
seg ∈segments_of_conn(conn)),
double(seg1,seg2) →are_neighbors_in_net(seg1,seg2,net)
end
The auxiliary function are_neighbors_in_net has been explained above, and
the auxiliary function segments_of_conn simply extracts the segments of the
connection as a set.
6.4.3.5
Train Switchboxes Wellformedness
For each train the dynamic train switchbox data should be wellformed with re-
spect to the static switchbox description data. The switchbox map must adhere
to the wellformedness property that the order of the switchboxes in the map
is indeed representative of a sequence of segments in the network. Firstly, this
means that there must be a switchbox connection in the switchbox description for
each of the switchboxes in the switchbox map. Secondly, it means that for each
(key, value)-pair in the switchbox map, the set of segments of the switchbox con-
nection corresponding to the key-switchbox should intersect the set of segments
of the switchbox connection corresponding to the value-switchbox.
The resulting specification of the train switchbox map wellformedness function
is as follows.
train_sbs_wf : SboxMap × SwitchboxDesc →Bool
train_sbs_wf(sboxes,sbdesc) ≡
(∀sb : SwitchboxID • sb ∈sbs_of_sboxmap(sboxes) ⇒
sb ∈dom(sbdesc))
∧
==PAGE:79==
6.4 V1: First System Model
63
(∀sb : SwitchboxID • sb ∈dom(sboxes) ⇒
segments_of_sbconn(sbdesc(sb)) ∩
segments_of_sbconn(sbdesc(sboxes(sb))) ̸= {})
The auxiliary function sbs_of_sboxmap extracts the switchboxes of the do-
main and range of the map as a set and the auxiliary function segments_of_sbconn
extracts the segments of the switchbox connection as a set.
6.4.3.6
Switchbox Connected Component Wellformedness
For each switchbox the dynamic connected component (i.e. the data describing
which two segments are currently connected at the point of the switchbox) should
be wellformed with respect to the static switchbox description data. The switch-
box connected component must adhere to the wellformedness properties.
• If the switchbox connected component is connecting two segments, the cor-
responding switchbox connection from the switchbox description should be
able to connect the two segments, i.e. one segment is the stem segment, and
the other segment is included in the set of branch segments.
• If the switchbox connected component is not connecting two segments (i.e.
is an endpoint), the corresponding switchbox connection should have the
endpoint segment as its stem and have no branch segments.
The resulting specification of the train switchbox map wellformedness function
is as follows.
sb_connection_wf : SwitchboxID × Connection × SwitchboxDesc →Bool
sb_connection_wf(sbid,connection,sbdesc) ≡
sbid ∈dom(sbdesc) ∧
case connection of
conn(fst,snd) →is_possible_conn(fst,snd,sbdesc(sbid)),
endpoint(e) →branches(sbdesc(sbid)) = {}
end
The auxiliary function is_possible_conn checks that the two given segments
are connectible by a given switchbox connection, i.e. that one of the segments is
the stem segment and that the other segment is in the set of branch segments.
6.4.3.7
Train Switchboxes and Route Consistency
For each train, the train’s switchboxes and the train route must be consistent
with each other. In order for the two data components to be mutually consistent,
each of the switchboxes must be “pair-able” with a (key, value)-pair of segments
from the route and vice versa. There is a special case for the last segment of the
==PAGE:80==
64
6 Modelling
route, where two of the switchboxes must be associated with that segment. In
fact, this is the case for all segments in the route besides the first, but the explicit
check is only necessary for the last segment in the route since all other segments
(except the first) occur in two different (key, value)-pairs in the route because it
is stored in a map.
Had the segments of the route and the switchboxes been stored in ordered
lists, the task of determining whether the switchboxes are indeed along the route
would have been simpler. The task would be to determine whether the switchbox
at the head of the switchbox list is actually able to connect the head of the route
and the head of the tail of the route (i.e. the first and second elements of the list).
This process would then continue recursively on the tails of each list. However,
because both the switchboxes and the route segments are saved in maps, there
is no notion of the first elements. Therefore, the static switchbox description is
needed to determine which switchbox to check against which route (key, value)-
pair.
The specification of the train switchbox map and route consistency function
is as follows.
train_sbs_route_consistent : SboxMap × Route × SwitchboxDesc →Bool
train_sbs_route_consistent(sboxes,route,sbdesc) ≡
sbs_pair_with_segments(route,sboxes,sbdesc) ∧
segments_pair_with_sbs(route,sboxes,sbdesc)
The auxiliary function sbs_pair_with_segments checks that each of the non-
endpoint switchboxes can be paired with two segments in sequence in the train
route. The segments_pair_with_sbs checks that every two segments in sequence
in the route can be paired with one of the train’s switchboxes and for the last
segment of the route that there are two associated switchboxes in the switchbox
map.
6.4.3.8
Train Position and Route Consistency
For each train, the train position must be consistent with the train route. For
this to be the case, the following properties must hold.
• For a single segment position, the route must either be empty, corresponding
to the train reaching its final destination, or the segment must be one of
the key-values in the route map.
• For a double segment position, the first segment of the position must be
one of the key-values in the route map, while the second segment must be
the corresponding route map value for that key.
The specification of the train position and route consistency function is as
follows.
==PAGE:81==
6.4 V1: First System Model
65
consistent_pos : Position × Route →Bool
consistent_pos(pos,route) ≡
case pos of
single (seg) →route = [] ∨seg ∈dom(route),
double(seg1,seg2) →seg1 ∈dom(route) ∧route(seg1) = seg2
end
6.4.3.9
Train Next Switchbox and Position Consistency
For each train, the train’s next switchbox must be consistent with the position of
the train. Furthermore, in the same manner as the position and the route, the
next switchbox must be consistent with the train’s switchboxes. The following
properties must hold about the next switchbox.
• If the train position is a single segment position, the switchbox connection
of the next switchbox must be associated with the segment, either in the
stem or the branches.
• If the train position is a single segment position, the switchbox map must
either be empty, corresponding to the train reaching its final destination, or
the next switchbox must be one of the key-values in the switchbox map.
• If the train position is a double segment position, the next switchbox must
be one of the key-values in the switchbox map.
• If the train position is a double segment position, either the first or second
segment of the position must be associated with the next switchbox.
The specification of the train’s next switchbox, position and switchboxes con-
sistency function is as follows.
consistent_next_sb : SwitchboxID × Position × SboxMap ×
SwitchboxDesc →Bool
consistent_next_sb(sbid,pos,sboxes,sbdesc) ≡
sbid ∈dom(sbdesc) ∧
case pos of
single (seg) →
(sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∨sboxes = []) ∧
seg ∈segments_of_sbconn(sbdesc(sbid)),
double(seg1,seg2) →
sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∧
(seg1 ∈segments_of_sbconn(sbdesc(sbid)) ∨
seg2 ∈segments_of_sbconn(sbdesc(sbid)))
end
The auxiliary function segments_of_sbconn has been explained above.
==PAGE:82==
66
6 Modelling
6.4.3.10
Train Reservation and Route Consistency
For each train, the train’s reservations must be consistent with the train’s route.
The following properties must hold.
• The reservations must never be empty.
• The reservations should only be for segments along the (rest of the) route,
except if the route is empty.
The specification of the train reservation and route consistency function is as
follows.
consistent_t_reservations : ResMap × Route →Bool
consistent_t_reservations(res,route) ≡
res ̸= [] ∧
(route = [] ∨
(∀sbid : SwitchboxID • sbid ∈dom(res) ⇒
res(sbid) ⊆segments_of_route(route)))
The auxiliary function segments_of_route has been explained above.
6.4.3.11
Train Locks and Switchboxes Consistency
For each train, the train locks must be consistent with the switchboxes of the
train. The following properties must hold.
• The train should only have locks for switchboxes along the (rest of the)
train’s route.
• The train should never have a lock for the last of the train’s switchboxes.
The specification of the train locks and switchboxes consistency function is as
follows.
consistent_t_locks : Sboxset × SboxMap →Bool
consistent_t_locks(locks,sboxes) ≡
locks ⊆sbs_of_sboxmap(sboxes) ∧
(∀sbid : SwitchboxID • sbid ∈(rng(sboxes) \ dom(sboxes)) ⇒
sbid /∈locks)
The auxiliary function sbs_of_sboxmap has been explained above.
==PAGE:83==
6.4 V1: First System Model
67
6.4.3.12
Train and Switchbox Reservations Consistency
For each train, the train’s reservations must be consistent with the reservations
of the switchboxes. Likewise, for each switchbox, the reservation of the switchbox
must be consistent with the reservations of the trains. In more detail, the following
consistency properties must be fulfilled.
• If a train’s reservations have segments reserved at a switchbox, then the
reservations of the switchbox has each of the segments reserved for the
train.
• If the reservations of a switchbox have segments reserved for a train, then
the train’s reservations have the segments reserved at the switchbox.
The specification of the train reservations and switchbox reservations consist-
ency function is as follows.
cons_res : TResState × SbResState →Bool
cons_res(tResState,sbResState) ≡
(∀sbid : SwitchboxID • sbid ∈dom(sbResState) ⇒
(∀tid : TrainID • tid ∈dom(tResState) ⇒
cons_t_sb_res(tid,sbid,tResState,sbResState) ∧
cons_sb_t_res(tid,sbid,tResState,sbResState)))
The auxiliary function cons_t_sb_res checks that a train’s reservations are
consistent with the reservations of all switchboxes, whereas the auxiliary function
cons_sb_t_res checks that the reservations of a switchbox are consistent with
the reservations of all trains.
6.4.3.13
Train and Switchbox Locks Consistency
For each train, the train’s locks must be consistent with the locks of the switch-
boxes. Likewise, for each switchbox, the lock of the switchbox must be consistent
with the locks of the trains. In more detail, the following consistency properties
must be fulfilled.
• If a train’s locks contain a reference to a switchbox, then the lock of the
switchbox is a reference to the train.
• If the lock of a switchbox has a reference to a train, then the train’s locks
contain a reference to the switchbox.
The specification of the train locks and switchbox locks consistency function
is as follows.
==PAGE:84==
68
6 Modelling
cons_locks : TLocksState × SbLockState →Bool
cons_locks(tLocksState,sbLockState) ≡
(∀sbid : SwitchboxID • sbid ∈dom(sbLockState) ⇒
(∀tid : TrainID • tid ∈dom(tLocksState) ⇒
sbid ∈tLocksState(tid) ⇒sbLockState(sbid) = tid ∧
tid = sbLockState(sbid) ⇒sbid ∈tLocksState(tid)))
6.4.3.14
Switchbox Sensor and Train Position Consistency
For each train, the train’s position must be consistent with the sensor status of
the train’s next switchbox. Likewise, for each switchbox the sensor status of the
switchbox must be consistent with the position of the trains. In more detail, the
following consistency properties must be fulfilled.
• If a train’s position is a double segment position (i.e. is in a critical section)
then the sensor of the train’s next switchbox should be active.
• If the sensor of a switchbox is active, then there must exist a train with
a double segment position consisting of the stem segment and one of the
branch segment of that switchbox.
The specification of the train position and switchbox sensor consistency func-
tion is as follows.
cons_sensor : TPosState × TNextsbState × SbSensorState ×
SwitchboxDesc →Bool
cons_sensor(tPosState,tNextsbState,sbSensorState,sbdesc) ≡
(∀sbid : SwitchboxID • sbid ∈dom(sbSensorState) ∧
sbid ∈dom(sbdesc) ⇒
(∃tid : TrainID • tid ∈dom(tPosState) ∧
case sbSensorState(sbid) of
active →
(∃seg : Segment •
seg ∈branches(sbdesc(sbid)) ∧
(tPosState(tid) = double(stem(sbdesc(sbid)),seg) ∨
tPosState(tid) = double(seg,stem(sbdesc(sbid))))),
passive →true
end))
∧
(∀tid : TrainID • tid ∈dom(tPosState) ⇒
case tPosState(tid) of
double(segm1,segm2) →sbSensorState(tNextsbState(tid)) = active,
single (seg) →true
end)
==PAGE:85==
6.4 V1: First System Model
69
6.4.4
Guards and State Updater Functions
For all the possible events of the system, guard and updater functions have been
specified. The functions are used for easing the task of specifying the transitions
in the transition system (presented in Section 6.4.7). The system has the following
events:
• A train moves.
• A track segment is reserved.
• A point is switched.
• A point is locked.
The guard functions restrict when the different events can take place, i.e.
when the updater functions can be applied. For each event, e, two different guard
functions of the form t_can_e and sb_can_e are specified. The functions of the
form t_can_e determine the legality of the event with respect to a train’s state
space, whereas the functions sb_can_e determine the legality of the event with
respect to the state space of the switchbox. The exception to this rule is the move
event, which is not restricted by the values of the state space of any switchbox,
so there is just a single can_move function.
The updater functions update the relevant parts of the state spaces of trains
and switchboxes. In the same manner as guards, there is, for each event, e, two
different updater functions of the form t_e, updating the relevant variables in
the train’s state space, and sb_e, updating the relevant variables in the state
space of the switchbox. At least, in theory, this would be the case. In practice,
however, one of the events only change the state space of the switchbox, one
event is simple enough to not need a separate function capturing the functionality
etc. See further explanation below where each of the event updater functions is
described separately.
The division of the guard and updater functions for each event into one con-
cerned with the train’s state space and one concerned with that of the switchbox
is a preparation for the following refinement of the model. In this version of the
system, each event transition uses the conjunction of both guard functions as the
guard and the sequencing of both updater functions as the effect. However, since
the aim is to develop a refined system model, which should include some model-
ling of communication between the switchboxes and the train control computers,
all events (except move) have been partitioned into two separate sub-events to
make the refinement step simpler.
6.4.4.1
Reservation Guards
From a train’s point of view, a reservation of a segment at a switchbox can happen
as long as the following properties hold.
==PAGE:86==
70
6 Modelling
• The train does not already have a reservation for the segment at the switch-
box.
• The requested reservation is at a switchbox along the route.
• The requested reservation is for a segment in the train’s route.
This is expressed in the following guard function.
t_can_reserve : SboxMap × SwitchboxID × Segment × Route ×
ResMap →Bool
t_can_reserve(sboxes,sbid,segment,route,res) ≡
∼(sbid ∈dom(res) ∧segment ∈res(sbid)) ∧
(sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∨sbid ∈rng(sboxes)) ∧
segment ∈segments_of_route(route)
The parameters of the guard function belong to two categories. Firstly, the
data for the reservation event, i.e. which switchbox the reservation should happen
at (sbid) and which segment should be reserved (segment). This data corresponds
to the data that would be exchanged via communication between the train and
the switchbox. Secondly, there is the data needed to determine the legality of the
event, i.e. the train’s switchboxes (sboxes), route (route) and reservations (res).
This data corresponds to the data components inside the train’s own state space
which are observed in order to determine the event legality.
From the point of view of a switchbox, a reservation request of a segment can
be satisfied when the following properties hold.
• The switchbox is associated with the segment.
• The segment is not reserved already by any train at the switchbox.
This is expressed in the following guard function.
sb_can_reserve : SbResMap × Segment →Bool
sb_can_reserve(res,seg) ≡seg ∈dom(res) ∧res(seg) = t_none
The parameters of the guard function are the segment which should be re-
served (seg) and the reservations of the switchbox itself (res).
Note that, even though the aim for this version of the model is to impose the
least amount of control possible (while still only allowing safety-preserving events),
the reservation is only accepted if the segment is not reserved by any train. This
was originally handled in a more flexible manner, where the reservation would be
accepted if there was an existing reservation for the segment as long as it was
made for the requesting train. However, this approach gave rise to problems with
multiple cycles through the same event during the model checking process.
==PAGE:87==
6.4 V1: First System Model
71
6.4.4.2
Switch Guards
From a train’s point of view, the switching of a point at a switchbox to a specific
connection can happen as long as the following properties hold.
• The train cannot have already locked the point at the switchbox.
• The switchbox is one of the switchboxes along the train’s route.
• The requested connection corresponds to two adjacent segments in the
train’s route.
• The train has a reservation at the switchbox for both the segments of the
connection.
This is expressed in the following guard function.
t_can_switch : Connection × Route × SboxMap × ResMap ×
SwitchboxID × Sboxset →Bool
t_can_switch(c,route,sboxes,res,sbid,locks) ≡
sbid /∈locks ∧sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∧
(∃seg : Segment •
seg ∈dom(route) ∧c = conn(seg,route(seg)) ∧
sbid ∈dom(res) ∧
route(seg) ∈res(sbid) ∧seg ∈res(sbid))
The parameters of the guard function are firstly the switchbox which should
switch the point (sbid) and the connection to which the point should be switched
(c). Secondly, there are the data components from the train’s state space, i.e. the
route (route), the switchboxes (sboxes) and the reservations (res).
From the point of view of a switchbox, a switch request of a segment can be
satisfied when the following properties hold.
• There is no train in the critical section, i.e. the sensor of the switchbox is
passive.
• The point at the switchbox is not locked.
• The requested connection is possible for the switchbox, i.e. the segments of
the connections are associated with the switchbox as the stem and a branch,
respectively.
This is expressed in the following guard function.
sb_can_switch : Connection × SwitchboxID × TrainID × Sensor ×
SwitchboxDesc →Bool
sb_can_switch(c,sbid,lock,sensor,sbdesc) ≡
sensor = passive ∧lock = t_none ∧
is_possible_conn(fst(c),snd(c),sbdesc(sbid)),
==PAGE:88==
72
6 Modelling
The parameters of the guard function are firstly the connection to which the
point should be switched (c) and secondly, the data components from the state
space of the switchbox, i.e. the identifier of the switchbox (sbid), the lock (lock)
and the associated sensor (sensor). Finally, the function has the switchbox de-
scription of the system as a parameter.
As mentioned, this version of the model is kept as flexible as possible with
regards to not imposing a specific event ordering. The switch event can, at any
time, happen anywhere along the rest of the train’s route, as long as the train has
the correct reservations. Less control could have been imposed by simply allowing
trains to request switching of points without regard to the train’s reservations.
However, this could lead to two trains, needing different connections at the same
point, “competing” over the switching of a point, cycling through the actions of
t1 requesting the switching to connection c1, then t2 requesting the switching to
connection c2, then t1 requesting again etc.
6.4.4.3
Lock Guards
The train can request a switchbox to lock a point when the same properties hold
as for switching. The guard function is therefore identical except for the name.
t_can_lock : Connection × Route × SboxMap × ResMap ×
SwitchboxID × Sboxset →Bool
t_can_lock(c,route,sboxes,res,sbid,locks) ≡
sbid /∈locks ∧sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∧
(∃seg : Segment • seg ∈dom(route) ∧
c = conn(seg,route(seg)) ∧sbid ∈dom(res) ∧
route(seg) ∈res(sbid) ∧seg ∈res(sbid))
Since this guard function is overall the same as the t_can_switch function,
the parameters are the same and do not require further explanation.
The properties for the switchbox to lock a point are similar to those for switch-
ing a point. The switchbox can lock a point when the following properties hold.
• The point at the switchbox is not locked.
• The requested connection in which to lock the point is the same as the
point’s current connection. This corresponds to the point having already
been switched (if necessary).
This is expressed in the following guard function.
sb_can_lock : Connection × Connection × TrainID →Bool
sb_can_lock(reqc,sbc,lock) ≡lock = t_none ∧sbc = reqc
==PAGE:89==
6.4 V1: First System Model
73
The parameters of the guard function are firstly the connection to which the
point should be switched (reqc) and secondly, the data components from the state
space of the switchbox, i.e. the current connection of the switchbox (sbc) and the
lock (lock).
6.4.4.4
Move Guard
As mentioned previously, there is just a single guard function for the move event.
This guard function determines if a train is allowed to move by checking whether
the state space of the train complies with the following properties.
• The train must not be at its final destination.
• The train must have a lock for the upcoming switchbox.
• The train has reservations for the next segment in its route at both relevant
switchboxes.
This is expressed in the following guard function.
t_can_move : Segment × Route × Sboxset × SwitchboxID × ResMap ×
SboxMap →Bool
t_can_move(posseg,route,locks,nextsb,res,sboxes) ≡
route ̸= [] ∧nextsb ∈locks ∧nextsb ∈dom(sboxes) ∧
posseg ∈dom(route) ∧
route(posseg) ∈res(nextsb) ∧
route(posseg) ∈res(sboxes(nextsb)),
The parameters of the guard function are the data components from the train’s
state space needed to determine the legality of the move event, i.e. the segment of
the train’s position which is furthest at the front (the segment of a single segment
position and the second segment of a double segment position), (posseg), the route
(route), the locks (locks), the next switchbox (nextsb), the reservations (res) and
the switchboxes (sboxes).
The guard function is only used when the train is in a single segment position.
When a train is in a double segment position, it can move fully onto the front
segment without further restriction, assuming that a train is always in a single
segment position before moving into a critical section. This corresponds to the
move guard for a train in a double segment always being true. Further details on
this can be seen in Section 6.4.7.4.
6.4.4.5
Reservation Updaters
When a reservation request from a train is fulfilled by a switchbox, the reservation
data structures for both the train and the switchbox must be updated. Since the
==PAGE:90==
74
6 Modelling
reservations for trains are saved in maps with switchboxes as keys, there are two
scenarios when adding a reservation to a train’s reservations:
• The train reserves a segment and has no existing reservations at the switch-
box.
• The train reserves a segment and already has one or more existing reserva-
tions at the switchbox.
Clearly, the two cases correspond to whether an entry with the switchbox
identifier as key is already found in the train’s reservation map. In the first case,
where the key does not already exist, the key 7→value is simply added as an
entry in the map. This corresponds to the following updater function.
t_res_sb_notin : ResMap × SwitchboxID × Segment →ResMap
t_res_sb_notin(res,sbid,seg) ≡
res † [sbid7→{seg}]
pre sbid /∈dom(res)
For the other case, where the key already exists the value corresponding to
the key must be modified by adding the new reservation. This corresponds to the
following updater function.
t_res_sb_in : ResMap × SwitchboxID × Segment →ResMap
t_res_sb_in(res,sbid,seg) ≡
res † [sbid7→add_to_segset(res(sbid),seg)]
pre sbid ∈dom(res)
The auxiliary function add_to_segset simply adds a segment to a segment
set. The parameters for the updater functions consist of the data component,
i.e. the train’s reservations, (res) to which changes should be made, and the data
necessary for the change, i.e. the switchbox (sbid) at which the segment (seg) is
reserved.
The update to the switchbox reservations is simpler. The identifier of the
reserving train is simply written as the new value for the segment key which was
reserved. This corresponds to the following updater function.
sb_res : SbResMap × TrainID × Segment →SbResMap
sb_res(res,tid,seg) ≡res † [seg 7→tid]
The parameters for the updater function consist of the data component, i.e.
the reservations of the switchbox, (res) to which changes should be made, and
the data necessary for the change, i.e. the train (tid) for which the segment (seg)
should be reserved.
==PAGE:91==
6.4 V1: First System Model
75
6.4.4.6
Switch Updaters
For the switch event there are no actual updater functions. When switching a
point, there are no changes to the train’s state space, and the only change to the
state space of the switchbox is to the connected component. This is simply done
by writing the new value for the connection to the component.
6.4.4.7
Lock Updaters
For the lock event there is only a function for updating the state space of the
train. The event simply causes the train’s set of locks to be extended with the
new switchbox identifier which has locked the point. This corresponds to the
following updater function.
t_lock : Sboxset × SwitchboxID →Sboxset
t_lock(locks,sbid) ≡locks ∪{sbid}
The parameters for the updater function consist of the data component, i.e.
the train’s locks, (locks) to which changes should be made, and the data necessary
for the change, i.e. the switchbox (sbid) which is locked.
The state space of the switchbox is changed as well, of course, but as for the
switch event, the change is simply to set a component, in this case the locked by
component, to a new value, so no function is necessary.
6.4.4.8
Move Updaters
The move event causes changes to both the switchbox state space and the train
state space. The specific changes depend on which one of two cases is applicable.
Either, the train moves from a single position to a double position, i.e. the train
moves into a critical section, in which case the following components are updated.
• For the train the train’s position is updated to the upcoming critical section.
This is expressed in the following updater function with the train’s route
(route) and position (pos) as parameters.
t_move_pos_single : Position × Route →Position
t_move_pos_single(pos,route) ≡double(seg(pos),route(seg(pos)))
pre seg(pos) ∈dom(route) ∧is_single_pos(pos)
• For the switchbox, the sensor value is updated to active. The update to the
switchbox sensor is, just like the connected component for the switch event
and the locked by component for the lock event, so simple that it does not
need to be handled by a function.
==PAGE:92==
76
6 Modelling
Otherwise, the train moves from a double position to a single position, i.e. the
train leaves a critical section, in which case the following components are updated
for the train.
• The position is updated such that the train moves fully onto the second
segment of the critical position it was in. This is expressed in the follow-
ing updater function with the train’s route (route) and position (pos) as
parameters.
t_move_pos_double : Position × Route →Position
t_move_pos_double(pos,route) ≡single(seg2(pos))
pre ∼is_single_pos(pos)
• The route is updated by removing the key 7→value entry, where the first
segment of the double position is the key, from the map. The following
updater function, with that segment (segm) and the train’s route (route) as
parameters, is used for this.
t_move_route : Segment × Route →Route
t_move_route(segm,route) ≡route \ {segm}
• The value for the next switchbox is updated to represent the upcoming
switchbox instead of the switchbox which has just been passed. The fol-
lowing updater function, with the next switchbox (nextsb) and the train’s
switchboxes (sboxes) as parameters, is used for this.
t_move_nextsb : SwitchboxID × SboxMap →SwitchboxID
t_move_nextsb(nextsb,sboxes) ≡sboxes(nextsb)
pre nextsb ∈dom(sboxes)
• The switchboxes along the train’s route are updated by removing the key 7→
value entry, where the next switchbox is the key, from the map. Note that
this update happens in the same atomic action as the update to the value of
the next switchbox. This updater function therefore uses the “old” value of
the next switchbox, not the updated value. The following updater function
with the next switchbox (nextsb) and the train’s switchboxes (sboxes) as
parameters, is used for this.
t_move_sboxes : SwitchboxID × SboxMap →SboxMap
t_move_sboxes(nextsb,sboxes) ≡sboxes \ {nextsb}
==PAGE:93==
6.4 V1: First System Model
77
• The train’s reservations are updated to no longer contain any reservations at
the switchbox which was just passed, i.e. the next switchbox value before
the update has happened. The following updater function with the next
switchbox (nextsb) and the train’s reservations (res) as parameters, is used
for this.
t_move_res : SwitchboxID × ResMap →ResMap
t_move_res(nextsb,res) ≡res \ {nextsb}
• The train’s locks are, similarly to the reservations, updated to no longer
contain references to the switchbox which was just passed. The following
updater function with the next switchbox (nextsb) and the train’s locks
(locks) as parameters, is used for this.
t_move_locks : SwitchboxID × Sboxset →Sboxset
t_move_locks(nextsb,locks) ≡locks \ {nextsb}
For the switchbox, the following components are updated.
• The sensor value is updated to passive, which is still handled without a
separate function.
• The switchbox lock is changed to t_none and this, as well, is handled
without a separate function.
• The switchbox reservations are updated to no longer contain any references
to the train. The following updater function with the train’s position (pos)
and the reservations of the switchbox (sbres) as parameters, is used for this.
sb_move_reservations : Position × SbResMap →SbResMap
sb_move_reservations(pos,sbres) ≡
sbres † [seg1(pos)7→t_none,seg2(pos)7→t_none]
Generally, the updater functions could, of course, be used to update the state
spaces in different ways than intended. Nothing prevents someone from using
e.g. the t_move_res function to remove entries from the reservation map with
another switchbox identifier as key. Disallowing such unintended functionality
could be achieved by creating functions which take e.g. the full train state space
as argument and base the updated state space on this. This way, the function
can take the required value directly from the state space, rather than relying on
having the correct value passed in as argument. This approach is problematic
in regard to the model checking process, though – at least if the state space
is modelled in a record type – since it requires updating all fields in the state
space, even if the field should simply keep its previous value. This increases the
complexity. See further information about this in Section 9.2.
==PAGE:94==
78
6 Modelling
6.4.5
Safety Functions
The safety functions are used for easing the task of writing assertions about the
transition system. The two safety functions no_derail and no_collide state the
absence of derailment and collision, respectively, which are the safety properties
desired of the system. The no_derail function checks that if a specific train’s
position is a critical section, then the two segments of the position are connected
by a switchbox. The function is specified as follows.
no_derail : Position × SbConnState →Bool
no_derail(pos,sbconns) ≡
∼is_single_pos(pos) ∧
are_conn_by_sb(seg1(pos),seg2(pos),sbconns),
The auxiliary function are_conn_by_sb simply checks whether two segments
are currently connected by any of the switchboxes in the system.
The no_collide function checks that two specific trains’ positions do not over-
lap. The function is specified as follows.
no_collide : TrainID × TrainID × TPosState →Bool
no_collide(tid1,tid2,tPosState) ≡
tid1 ∈dom(tPosState) ∧tid2 ∈dom(tPosState) ∧
tid1 ̸= tid2 ∧
segments_of_pos(tPosState(tid1)) ∩
segments_of_pos(tPosState(tid2)) = {},
The auxiliary function segments_of_pos extracts the segments of a position
as a set.
6.4.6
Transition System Variables
In the transition system, several local variables are declared. The initial values of
these determine the initial state of the transition system. In the generic version of
the model, these variables are uninitialised, which means that they must be given
values when the model is instantiated for model checking. Otherwise, the model
checker will choose an arbitrary value for each of them. Note that the initial
values chosen by the model checker may disagree with the wellformedness and
consistency properties – and the safety properties for that matter. The following
variables are defined for the trains of the system.
• The routes.
• The positions.
• The next switchboxes.
==PAGE:95==
6.4 V1: First System Model
79
• The switchboxes along the routes.
• The reservations.
• The locks.
This corresponds to the following variable definitions.
local
tRoute : TRouteState,
tPos : TPosState,
tNextsb : TNextsbState,
tSboxes : TSboxesState,
tRes : TResState,
tLocks : TLocksState
The following variables are defined for the switchboxes of the system.
• The connected segments at the points.
• The locks.
• The sensors.
• The reservations.
This corresponds to the following variable definitions.
local
sbConn : SbConnState,
sbLock : SbLockState,
sbSensor : SbSensorState,
sbRes : SbResState
In the different instances of the model, the variables will be initialised to their
respective values. For the example system, the initialisations will look as follows.
tRoute : TRouteState := [t1 7→[s17→s2, s27→s4]],
tPos : TPosState := [t1 7→single(s1)],
tNextsb : TNextsbState := [t1 7→sb1],
tSboxes : TSboxesState := [t1 7→[sb17→sb2,sb27→sb3]],
tRes : TResState := [t1 7→[sb17→{s1}]],
tLocks : TLocksState := [t1 7→{}],
sbConn : SbConnState := [
sb0 7→endpoint(s1),sb1 7→conn(s1,s3),
sb2 7→conn(s2,s4),sb3 7→endpoint(s4)],
==PAGE:96==
80
6 Modelling
sbLock : SbLockState := [
sb0 7→t_none,sb1 7→t_none,sb2 7→t_none,sb3 7→t_none],
sbSensor : SbSensorState := [
sb0 7→passive,sb1 7→passive,sb2 7→passive,sb3 7→passive],
sbRes : SbResState := [
sb0 7→[s17→t_none],
sb1 7→[s17→t1,s27→t_none,s37→t_none],
sb2 7→[s27→t_none,s37→t_none,s47→t_none],
sb3 7→[s47→t_none]]
6.4.7
Transition System Rules
The rules of the transition system define the possible events of the system. Each
rule consists of a guard and an effect.
This is where the guard and updater
functions, presented in Section 6.4.4, are used in order to increase the readability
of the transition system. Generally, the rules of the transition system have the
following form.
([=] sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID, ...
•
[rule_name]
t_can_e(...) ∧sb_can_e(...) =⇒
tData′(tid) = t_e (...), sbData′(sbid) = sb_e(...))
where e is some event; the ellipsis in the first line represents any extra variables
needed for that particular event; tData and sbData are examples of variables in
the train control computer state space and the switchbox state space, repectively,
changed by the transition (multiple values from each state space may be changed
by one transition); t_e and sb_e are examples of functions returning the new
value for the variables.
The exception to this is the rule for the move event which, as mentioned earlier,
is not an internal function but an external one. There is no guard function for
the switchbox in this case, since the state spaces of the switchboxes are not
checked when a train determines whether it is legal to move forward. The train
only checks its own state space. The effect does update the state space of both
train and switchbox though, but this is not triggered by a request since it is an
external event. Rather, the sensor value of the switchbox is affected by the train
movement.
6.4.7.1
Reservation Rules
As explained earlier, in Section 6.4.4, there are two scenarios when a reservation
happens. Either, the train reserves a segment and has no existing reservations
==PAGE:97==
6.4 V1: First System Model
81
at the switchbox or the train reserves a segment and already has one or more
existing reservations at the switchbox. This is reflected in the transition system,
where each scenario is handled by a separate rule. The first scenario is handled
by the following rule.
([=] sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID, seg : Segment •
[res_sbnotin]
sbid /∈dom(tRes(tid)) ∧
t_can_reserve(tSboxes(tid),sbid,seg,tRoute(tid),tRes(tid)) ∧
sb_can_reserve(sbRes(sbid),seg) =⇒
tRes′(tid) = t_res_sb_notin(tRes(tid),sbid,seg),
sbRes′(sbid) = sb_res(sbRes(sbid),tid,seg))
The second scenario is handled by the following rule.
([=] sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID, seg : Segment •
[res_sbin]
sbid ∈dom(tRes(tid)) ∧
t_can_reserve(tSboxes(tid),sbid,seg,tRoute(tid),tRes(tid)) ∧
sb_can_reserve(sbRes(sbid),seg) =⇒
tRes′(tid) = t_res_sb_in(tRes(tid),sbid,seg),
sbRes′(sbid) = sb_res(sbRes(sbid),tid,seg))
As can be seen from the two rule definitions, they each have an extra com-
ponent in their guards conjugated with the guard functions for the reservation
event. The extra component is used to determine which one of the above de-
scribed scenarios is applicable. This check could have been performed inside a
single t_res function which would then branch out to the t_res_sb_in and the
t_res_sb_notin functionality, respectively. However, given the advice from Chris
George presented in Section 2.3.3.4, the presented solution was chosen instead.
6.4.7.2
Switch Rule
The rule for the switch event follows the template rule closely and simply updates
the connection at the point of the switchbox. As an extra argument the connection
which the point should be switched to is given. The rule is specified as follows.
([=] c : Connection, sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID •
[switch]
t_can_switch(c,tRoute(tid),tSboxes(tid),tRes(tid),sbid) ∧
sb_can_switch(c,sbid,sbLock(sbid),sbSensor(sbid),switchboxes) =⇒
sbConn′(sbid) = c)
==PAGE:98==
82
6 Modelling
6.4.7.3
Lock Rule
The rule for the lock event follows the template rule closely as well, and simply
updates the locks of the train and the locked by component of the switchbox. As
an extra argument the connection which the point should be locked in is given.
The rule is specified as follows.
([=] c : Connection, sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID •
[lock]
t_can_lock(c,tRoute(tid),tSboxes(tid),tRes(tid),sbid,tLocks(tid)) ∧
sb_can_lock(c,sbConn(sbid),sbLock(sbid)) =⇒
tLocks′(tid) = t_lock(tLocks(tid),sbid),
sbLock′(sbid) = tid)
6.4.7.4
Move Rules
As explained in Section 6.4.4 there are two scenarios for the train movement
event. Either, the train moves from a single position to a double position, or the
train moves from a double position to a single position. This is reflected in the
transition system where each scenario is handled by a separate rule. The scenario
when the train moves from a single position is handled by the following rule.
([=] tid : TrainID •
[move_single]
is_single_pos(tPos(tid)) ∧
t_can_move(seg(tPos(tid)),tRoute(tid),tLocks(tid),tNextsb(tid),tRes(tid),
tSboxes(tid)) =⇒
tPos′(tid) = t_move_pos_single(tPos(tid),tRoute(tid)),
sbSensor′(tNextsb(tid)) = active)
The scenario when the train moves from a double position is handled by the
following rule.
([=] tid : TrainID •
[move_double]
∼is_single_pos(tPos(tid))=⇒
tRoute′(tid) = t_move_route(seg1(tPos(tid)),tRoute(tid)),
tPos′(tid) = t_move_pos_double(tPos(tid),tRoute(tid)),
tSboxes′(tid) = t_move_sboxes(tNextsb(tid),tSboxes(tid)),
tNextsb′(tid) = t_move_nextsb(tNextsb(tid),tSboxes(tid)),
tRes′(tid) = t_move_res(tNextsb(tid),tRes(tid)),
tLocks′(tid) = t_move_locks(tNextsb(tid),tLocks(tid)),
sbSensor′(tNextsb(tid)) = passive,
==PAGE:99==
6.4 V1: First System Model
83
sbRes′(tNextsb(tid)) =
sb_move_reservations(tPos(tid),sbRes(tNextsb(tid))),
sbLock′(tNextsb(tid)) = t_none)
Note that the rule does not use a guard function in the same way as the other
transition rules. The guard is simply a check that the train’s position is a double
position. This assumes that all trains are in a single position before moving to
the next corresponding double position. With this assumption, it is possible to
be certain that the train has the proper reservations and locks, since the train
would otherwise not have been able to move into the critical section.
A final move rule is included for handling the case where the train has reached
the end of its route. In this case, neither of the other rules would be applicable.
This corresponds to a deadlock. Therefore, in order to prevent deadlocks, the
following rule has been specified.
([=] tid : TrainID •
[move_end]
tRoute(tid) = [] =⇒tPos′(tid) = tPos(tid))
The rule “updates” a train’s position by setting it to the same value. More
elegant solutions than this could have been introduced.
For example, a train
could turn around and drive through its route in the opposite direction. This
would of course require that the full route of each train was saved somewhere.
The prevention of deadlocks is important for the results of the model checking
process. If the system has deadlocks, the results from the model checker might
not be trustworthy. This will be discussed further in Chapter 8.
6.4.8
Transition System Assertions
The assertions are logical statements about the transition system which are at-
tempted to be verified with the model checker. The assertions can be divided
into wellformedness checks, consistency checks and safety checks which should all
be globally true. Additionally, for each train in the system there is an assertion
stating that the train does not reach its final destination. These assertions are
used to generate counter examples, demonstrating the possibility of the trains
reaching their destination.
6.4.8.1
Safety Assertions
The safety assertions are the assertions used to verify the safety of the system, i.e.
absence of collisions and derailments.
• The absence of collisions (which only applies for systems with more than
one train) can be shown by the following assertion:
==PAGE:100==
84
6 Modelling
[no_collide] TS ⊢(∀tid1,tid2 : TrainID •
G(tid1 ̸= tid2 ∧tid1 ̸= t_none ∧tid2 ̸= t_none ⇒
no_collide(tid1,tid2,tPos)))
• The absence of derailments can be shown by the following assertion:
[no_derail] TS ⊢(∀tid : TrainID •
G(∼is_single_pos(tPos(tid)) ⇒no_derail(tPos(tid),sbConn)))
6.4.8.2
Wellformedness Assertions
The wellformedness assertions are the assertions used to verify the wellformedness
of the system data such as the network, the train route etc. Note that for the
static system data, i.e. the network and the switchbox description, it is possible
to verify the wellformedness by e.g. SML testing instead of model checking.
• The network wellformedness can be shown by the following assertion:
[wf_net] TS ⊢G(network_wf(network))
• The switchbox description wellformedness can be shown by the following
assertion:
[wf_sbdesc] TS ⊢G(sb_desc_wf(switchboxes,network))
• The train route wellformedness can be shown by the following assertion:
[wf_route] TS ⊢(∀tid : TrainID •
G (tid ̸= t_none ⇒route_wf(tRoute(tid),network))),
• The train position wellformedness can be shown by the following assertion:
[wf_pos] TS ⊢(∀tid : TrainID •
G (pos_wf(tPos(tid),network)))
• The train switchboxes wellformedness can be shown by the following asser-
tion:
==PAGE:101==
6.4 V1: First System Model
85
[wf_train_sbs] TS ⊢(∀tid : TrainID •
G (train_sbs_wf(tSboxes(tid),switchboxes)))
• The switchbox connection wellformedness can be shown by the following
assertion:
[wf_sb_connection] TS ⊢(∀sbid : SwitchboxID •
G(sb_connection_wf(sbid,sbConn(sbid),switchboxes)))
6.4.8.3
Consistency Assertions
The consistency assertions are the assertions used to verify the consistency of the
distributed data such as the reservations, the locks etc.
• The train switchboxes and route consistency can be shown by the following
assertion:
[c_sbs_route] TS ⊢(∀tid : TrainID •
G(train_sbs_route_consistent(tSboxes(tid),tRoute(tid),switchboxes)))
• The train position and route consistency can be shown by the following
assertion:
[c_pos_route] TS ⊢(∀tid : TrainID •
G(consistent_pos(tPos(tid),tRoute(tid))))))
• The train next switchbox and position consistency can be shown by the
following assertion:
[c_nextsb_pos] TS ⊢(∀tid : TrainID •
G(consistent_next_sb(tNextsb(tid),tPos(tid),tSboxes(tid),
switchboxes)))
• The train reservations and route consistency can be shown by the following
assertion:
[c_res_route] TS ⊢(∀tid : TrainID •
G(tid ̸= t_none ⇒
consistent_t_reservations(tRes(tid),tRoute(tid))))
==PAGE:102==
86
6 Modelling
• The train locks and switchboxes consistency can be shown by the following
assertion:
[c_locks_sbs] TS ⊢(∀tid : TrainID •
G(consistent_t_locks(tLocks(tid),tSboxes(tid))))
• The train and switchbox reservations consistency can be shown by the fol-
lowing assertion:
[c_res] TS ⊢(∀sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID •
G(cons_sb_t_res(tid,sbid,tRes,sbRes) ∧
cons_t_sb_res(tid,sbid,tRes,sbRes)))
• The train and switchbox locks consistency can be shown by the following
assertion:
[c_locks] TS ⊢G(cons_locks(tLocks,sbLock))
• The switchbox sensor and train position consistency can be shown by the
following assertion:
[c_sensor_pos] TS ⊢G(cons_sensor(tPos,tNextsb,sbSensor,switchboxes))
6.4.8.4
Example Arrival Assertions
The assertions used to generate counter examples demonstrating the possibility of
the trains reaching their destinations are specified for each instance of the system.
As an example, the arrival-assertions could be specified as follows for a system
with a train t1 with final destination s4 and a train t2 with a final destination s1.
[arrive_t1] TS ⊢G(tPos(t1) ̸= single(s4)),
[arrive_t2] TS ⊢G(tPos(t2) ̸= single(s1)),
[arrive_both] TS ⊢G(∼(tPos(t1) = single(s4) ∧tPos(t2) = single(s1)))
where the assertion [arrive_t1] can be used to generate a counter example
just for t1, and similarly [arrive_t2] can be used for generating a counter ex-
ample just for t2. The assertion [arrive_both] can be used to generate a counter
example where both trains have reached their destination.
It would be possible to specify the first two assertions, i.e. [arrive_t1] and
[arrive_t2], generically in e.g. the following manner.
==PAGE:103==
6.5 V2: Refined System Model
87
[ arrive ] TS ⊢(∀tid : TrainID •
G(tPos(tid) ̸= final_pos(tid)))
where final_pos extracts the final destination of the train as a position. How-
ever, the quantification in this specification works against the purpose of specify-
ing a property for which a counter example is desired: at best, it will be random
which counter example (i.e. for which train) is generated when the assertion is
attempted verified. Should one wish to specify a more useful property regarding
arrival, the following would be more suitable.
[arrive_prove] TS ⊢(∀tid : TrainID •
G(F(tPos(tid) = final_pos(tid))))
This assertion states that for all trains in the system, at some point in the
future, the train will reach its final destination – so in this case, the assertion
should be proved, and no counter example should be generated.
Note, how-
ever, that finding counter examples for e.g.
both the assertions [arrive_t1]
and [arrive_t2] does not correspond to the above assertion [arrive_prove]
being provable for the system.
Rather, generating the counter examples for
[arrive_t1] and [arrive_t2] corresponds to proving that there exists a path
in the system where each train reaches its destination.
6.5
V2: Refined System Model
The model V 1 has been refined by using the event refinement method as explained
in Section 5.4.
Specifically, the method of atomic event refinement has been
applied. The aim is to refine the model to include an explicit communication
scheme between the control components, i.e. the train control computers and the
switchboxes. To achieve this, the events reserve, switch and lock have all been
partitioned into sub-events more closely modelling the communication protocol
between the train control computers and the switchboxes.
Since move is an
external action and is not triggered by a request it has not been changed from
the previous model. The base model specification, found in its generic form in
Appendix A.1, is the same, so the same types and functions are used. The changes
necessary for the refinement are carried out in the specification of the transition
system only. Thus, a great part of the model has already been explained and
this section will only concern the changes to the transition system. The generic
transition system specification for V 2 can be found in Appendix A.3.
==PAGE:104==
88
6 Modelling
6.5.1
The Communication Scheme
The intended communication scheme is a simple request-acknowledge protocol.
More elaborate communication protocols could without doubt have been used,
but for simplicity, this protocol was chosen. Given a system event e, a train t and
a switchbox sb, the protocol is conducted as illustrated in Figure 6.8.
t
sb
t can e / request e
sb can e / sb do e
acknowledge e
t do e
(a) A successful request and subsequent event.
t
sb
t can e / request e
!sb can e / neg. acknowledge e
(b) An unsuccessful request.
Figure 6.8: Examples of a fulfilled and an unfulfilled request.
As can be seen from Figure 6.8, the train control computer is the initiating
party. The train determines whether a given event is possible from its point of
view, and in the positive case sends a request. The request is received by the
switchbox, which then determines the possibility of the event from its point of
view. In the positive case, the switchbox updates its state space as necessary
and sends a positive acknowledgement, which is received by the train, which then
updates its own state space accordingly.
In the negative case, the switchbox
does not update its state space, but sends a negative acknowledgement, which is
received by the train, which, of course, does not update its state space either.
==PAGE:105==
6.5 V2: Refined System Model
89
6.5.2
Additional Assumption
The changes to the model give rise to an additional assumption. This version of
the model adds explicit communication between the switchboxes and train control
computers, and this communication is assumed to be perfect, i.e. to have no loss
of information or other faults.
6.5.3
Transition System Variables
The local transition system variables from the previous model version are kept,
but in order to ensure atomic even refinement, there is need for some extra vari-
ables. The extra variables are used for modelling the communication between
train control computers and switchboxes and for holding data necessary for the
communication. The additional variables can be partitioned into three categories:
interface variables, data variables and action variables.
The interface variables
consist of request, acknowledge and negative acknow-
ledge variables for each train control computer and switchbox pair. These vari-
ables model the communication signals sent by the train control computers and
switchboxes respectively. The following variables are specified:
req : TrainID →
m SwitchboxID = [],
ack : SwitchboxID →
m TrainID = [],
nack : SwitchboxID →
m TrainID = []
As can be seen, three maps have been specified. The train control computers
send requests by setting request variables.
The map req models that there is
a request variable, req(t) for each train identifier, t, which can be set to the
switchbox identifier of the switchbox with which the train control computer wants
to communicate. That is, when req(t) = sb, where sb is a switchbox identifier, it
models that the train control computer with identifier t has sent a request to the
switchbox with identifier sb.
Similarly, the switchboxes send positive and negative acknowledgements by
setting acknowledgement variables. The maps ack and nack model that there is
an acknowledgement and negative acknowledgement variable for each switchbox
identifier which can be set to the train identifier of the train control computer
with which the switchbox wants to communicate.
Note that the maps are each set to a value, namely the empty map. Since
these variables should always have the same value in each instantiation’s start
state, the value is part of the generic model.
The data variables
are used for saving extra data which is necessary in the
communication about events. For example, in the case of a reservation event, the
==PAGE:106==
90
6 Modelling
segment which should be reserved must be saved such that it is accessible in e.g.
the transition rules for the acknowledgement of the request. These variables are
a way of modelling the additional data passed to the switchbox when the train
sends a request and to the train when the switchbox sends an acknowledgement.
The following two data variables are used.
tmpSeg : Segment,
tmpConn : Connection
It it sufficient to have a single variable for each type of data only because the
events are known to be atomic. Both the switch event and the lock event use
the tmpConn variable, but since two events cannot take place at the same time,
and the data variables are always set to the value needed in that particular event
before using them, this does not pose a problem.
As mentioned, the tmpSeg variable is used for saving the segment up for reser-
vation in a reservation event. The tmpConn variable is used in the switch and lock
events for saving the connection which the point should be switched to and the
connection in which the point should be locked, respectively. In the instantiations
of the model the data variables can be initialised to arbitrary values since they
will always be set before being used the first time. These values cannot be given
in the generic model since they both depend on which segments are included in
the specific configuration.
It is advisable to set the variables to some value though, since the model
checker will otherwise be able to choose any arbitrary value. In order to only
have a single starting state for the system, the variables should be defined with a
value. It is unclear whether multiple starting points increases the complexity of
model checking with SAL.
The action variables
are flags used for determining which action, or event,
is currently ongoing, if any. These variables are used for preventing interleaving
of other events when a request has been sent by a train control computer. The
following action variables are specified.
resAction : Bool = false,
switchAction : Bool = false,
lockAction : Bool = false
The exact use of each of the variables will be visible from the presentation of
the transition system rules below. However, the overall idea is that when a train
control computer sends a request, the corresponding action variable is set to true.
When the train control computer receives some form of acknowledgement from
the switchbox, the action variable is set to false.
==PAGE:107==
6.5 V2: Refined System Model
91
Similarly to the request and acknowledgement maps, these variables should
always have the same value, namely false, in each instantiation’s start state, and
therefore their value is part of the generic model.
6.5.4
Transition System Rules
The rules of the transition system have undergone the atomic event refinement
process as presented in Section 5.4. For each previous rule for the events reserve,
switch and lock, there is now several separate rules of the following form.
• req_e, which is the starting point for the event. This corresponds to the
train control computer sending a request signal to a specific switchbox with
any relevant information for the event in question.
• ack_e, which is the positive acknowledgement rule for the switchbox. This
corresponds to the switchbox accepting the request, changing its own state
space accordingly and sending the positive acknowledgement signal to the
train control computer in question.
• nack_e, which is the negative acknowledgement rule for the switchbox. This
corresponds to the switchbox not being able to accept the request, and
therefore sending the negative acknowledgement signal to the train control
computer in question.
• end_e is an auxiliary action for “consuming” the negative acknowledgement
signal from a switchbox and not changing the state space of the train control
computer.
• e, which concludes the event. This corresponds to the train control com-
puter receiving the positive acknowledgement signal from the switchbox and
updating its own state space accordingly.
As an example, the refined event rules for the reservation event are presented.
The remaining rules for lock and switch can be found in Appendix A.3.
The train uses the rule req_res for requesting a reservation. The rule is ap-
plicable whenever it is possible for the train to request a reservation of a segment
according to the guard function t_can_reserve, the system is idle (i.e. no action
variables are true) and the train has not already sent a request which has not
been processed.
([=] sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID, seg : Segment •
[req_res]
∼resAction ∧∼lockAction ∧∼switchAction ∧
t_can_reserve(tSboxes(tid),sbid,seg,tRoute(tid),tRes(tid)) ∧
tid /∈dom(req) =⇒
req′ = req † [ tid 7→sbid],
resAction′ = true,
==PAGE:108==
92
6 Modelling
tmpSeg′ = seg)
The switchbox “receives” the request and processes it with one of the two
rules ack_res and nack_res. When a request has been set for a switchbox and a
reservation event is ongoing, depending on whether the switchbox can fulfil the
reservation request according to the guard function sb_can_reserve, the switch-
box will either update its state space and send a positive acknowledgement or do
nothing and send a negative acknowledgement.
([=] sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID •
[ack_res]
tid ∈dom(req) ∧req(tid) = sbid ∧resAction ∧
sb_can_reserve(sbRes(sbid),tmpSeg) =⇒
req′ = req \ {tid},
ack′ = ack † [sbid 7→tid],
sbRes′(sbid) = sb_res(sbRes(sbid),tid,tmpSeg))
[=]
([=] sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID •
[nack_res]
tid ∈dom(req) ∧req(tid) = sbid ∧resAction ∧
∼sb_can_reserve(sbRes(sbid),tmpSeg) =⇒
req′ = req \ {tid},
nack′ = nack † [sbid 7→tid])
The train receives the acknowledgement with one of the rules end_res, res_sbin
and res_sbnotin. If the acknowledgement is negative, the end_res rule is used,
which just wraps up the reservation event by removing the negative acknow-
ledgement and resetting the action variable. If, on the other hand, the acknow-
ledgement is positive, one of the functions res_sbin and res_sbnotin are used,
depending on whether the train already has a reservation at the switchbox in
question. These functions update the train’s state space accordingly, remove the
acknowledgement and reset the reservation action variable.
([=] sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID •
[end_res]
sbid ∈dom(nack) ∧nack(sbid) = tid ∧resAction =⇒
nack′ = nack \ {sbid},
resAction′ = false)
[=]
([=] sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID •
[res_sbin]
sbid ∈dom(tRes(tid)) ∧sbid ∈dom(ack) ∧
ack(sbid) = tid
∧resAction =⇒

6.5 V2: Refined System Model

tRes′(tid) = t_res_sb_in(tRes(tid),sbid,tmpSeg),
ack′ = ack \ {sbid},
resAction′ = false)
[=]
([=] sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID•
[res_sbnotin]
sbid /∈dom(tRes(tid)) ∧sbid ∈dom(ack) ∧
ack(sbid) = tid ∧resAction =⇒
tRes′(tid) = t_res_sb_notin(tRes(tid),sbid,tmpSeg),
ack′ = ack \ {sbid},
resAction′ = false)
6.5.5
Transition System Assertions
As an effect of the event refinement, some of the assertions about the transition
system have been changed, and assertions have been added. The assertions for
wellformedness and safety remain unchanged. However, many of the consistency
assertions have changed such that they only hold in the idle states, i.e. when
none of the action variables are true. This is expressed with an implication, such
that any assertion which was before of the form
[consistency_rule] TS ⊢G(consistency_funtion(...))
now instead has the form
[consistency_rule] TS ⊢G(∼resAction ∧∼lockAction ∧∼switchAction ⇒
consistency_funtion (...))
It is important to note that these assertions are not less strict than they were
in the earlier version of the system.
In fact, they assert that their respective
properties hold in exactly the same states as before, namely all the states where a
full action has been finished. All the intermediate states which have been added
as an effect of the event refinement are precisely the states in which one of the
action variables will be true.
The new assertions as seen below express that whenever an action is started,
it will eventually end, i.e. they can be used to prove that the system does not
just stop in the middle of an action.
[ finish_res ] TS ⊢G(resAction ⇒F(∼resAction)),
[finish_lock] TS ⊢G(lockAction ⇒F(∼lockAction)),
[finish_switch] TS ⊢G(switchAction ⇒F(∼switchAction))
==PAGE:110==
94
6 Modelling
6.5.6
Correctness of the Refinement
This section will informally argue that the new model version which has just been
introduced is indeed a refinement of the previous version, cf. Section 2.4. The
intention is that V 2 should be a refinement of V 1, i.e. that V 1 simulates V 2.
Continuing with the use of the reservation event as an example, see Figure
6.9 for a comparison between the successful reservation event in model V 1 and
model V 2.

6.6
V3: Restricted System Model
Model V 2 has been refined further.
In this version of the model the guards
from the system model of V 2 have been strengthened in order to model a more
restricted control protocol. In the previous versions of the model, any order of
legal events has been possible. This means, for example, that nothing has been
preventing a train from reserving the last segment of its route as the first event
(other than if the segment was already reserved, of course). Thus, the model
versions up until now have been rather flexible, but this also means that less-
than-optimal decisions were allowed to be taken. The aim is now to decrease the
flexibility and refine the model to obey a control strategy. It should be noted
that the strategy to which the model is restricted is just one of many choices.
This should be seen more as a proof of concept, i.e. that it is possible to make
such a restriction, rather than as a testament that the strategy described in
Section 6.6.1 is optimal. As for model V 2, this refinement still uses the same
base specification which can be found in its generic form in Appendix A.1. This
section only presents the changes from V 2 to V 3, so anything not mentioned in
the following can be assumed to be unchanged. The changes are carried out in
the transition system specification, and the generic version of this for model V 3
can be found in Appendix A.4.
6.6.1
Control Strategy
The concrete control strategy to which the system has been restricted in this
version is based on train control computers making reservations, and requesting
switching and locking of points just in time. That is, a train must only make
reservations of exactly the next upcoming segment in its route, and must only
switch and lock points at exactly the next upcoming switchbox. The strategy is
described below in more detail and applies to a single train, i.e. if a system has
multiple trains, each of them must follow the steps separately and in parallel.
Initially
the train must be in a single position (i.e. not in a critical section) for
which it has a reservation at the relevant switchbox(es). The train must have no
locks, and no events (for any train) may be ongoing (i.e. all action variables are
false).
The steps
described below comprise the algorithm from the point of view of
the train control computer.
==PAGE:112==
96
6 Modelling
1. The train requests the reservation of the upcoming track segment with re-
spect to its route
a) at the next switchbox.
b) at the switchbox following the next switchbox.
The order of the reservations does not matter, cf. Figure 6.11. The train
waits for acknowledgement. In the case of negative acknowledgement, the
train can re-send the request(s) (i.e. repeat this step).
2. The train requests the switching and locking of the upcoming point (i.e.
the point at the next switchbox) to the correct connection according to the
train’s route. The train waits for acknowledgement. In the case of negative
acknowledgement, the train can re-send the request (i.e. repeat this step).
3. The train checks that it has the proper reservations and lock (which it
should if the two previous steps have been completed) and moves into the
critical section.
4. The train moves out of the critical section, and fully onto the next track
segment. The train is now in a single position and can repeat the steps from
the top.
a
b
b
a
Figure 6.11: Reserving a segment at the two upcoming switchboxes in both orders
leads to the same state.
Termination
happens when the train has reached its final destination, i.e.
its route is empty.
In that case, the only possible transition is the deadlock-
prevention transition rule (explained previously), which updates the train position
to the same value.
Note
that the switch and lock events are presented in a single step in the
strategy, even though these events have been separate in previous versions of the
system. Due to the specification of the system, trains do not “remember” when
they have requested and received acknowledgement for a switch event (since no
data about this, which is observable to the trains, is saved). This conflicts with
the restriction to a concrete control strategy because it allows the switch event
to happen e.g. multiple times in a row even if it is not necessary, i.e. the point
in question has already been switched. This will be explained further in Section
6.6.2 below.
==PAGE:113==
6.6 V3: Restricted System Model
97
6.6.2
Restriction
The train guard functions have been made stricter in order to limit the amount of
possible events such that they match the steps of the described control strategy.
The method of event restriction as presented in Section 5.5 has been applied.
The restriction of the guard functions should generally be accomplished by
using the following pattern. If the guard function was previously of the form
can_e : ... →Bool
can_e(...) ≡...
then the new, restricted guard function is of the form
restricted_can_e : ... →Bool
restricted_can_e(...) ≡
can_e(...) ∧new_restriction_1 ∧... ∧new_restriction_n
The extra conjugation(s) can, in some cases, lead to the possibility of the prop-
erties of can_e to be reduced. This is the case when one of the new restrictions
implies (parts of) the properties found in the can_e guard function. Reducing
the old guard in order to avoid essentially double checking by checking both a
weaker property and a stronger one (i.e. where the stronger implies the weaker),
is generally advisable for achieving simplicity.
6.6.2.1
Reservation Guard
As an example of a case where it is possible to reduce the properties of the original
guard function, the reservation guard for train control computers is considered.
The original guard function is specified as follows.
t_can_reserve : SboxMap × SwitchboxID × Segment × Route ×
ResMap →Bool
t_can_reserve(sboxes,sbid,segment,route,res) ≡
∼(sbid ∈dom(res) ∧segment ∈res(sbid)) ∧
(sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∨sbid ∈rng(sboxes)) ∧
segment ∈segments_of_route(route)
The restrictions to be included in the updated guard function consist of only
allowing reservations at the two upcoming switchboxes and only allowing reser-
vations of the upcoming segment with respect to the train’s position. Following
the restriction pattern above, the restricted guard function should be specified as
follows.
==PAGE:114==
98
6 Modelling
restricted_t_can_reserve : SboxMap × SwitchboxID × SwitchboxID × Segment ×
Route × ResMap →Bool
restricted_t_can_reserve(sboxes,sbid,nextsb,segment,route,res) ≡
t_can_reserve(sboxes,sbid,segment,route,res) ∧
(sbid = nextsb ∨(nextsb ∈dom(sboxes) ∧sbid = sboxes(nextsb))) ∧
is_single_pos(pos) ∧seg(pos) ∈dom(route) ∧segment = route(seg(pos))
However, the following implications hold between properties of the restricted
guard function and the original guard function.
(sbid = nextsb ∨(nextsb ∈dom(sboxes) ∧sbid = sboxes(nextsb))) =⇒
(sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∨sbid ∈rng(sboxes))
For a single segment position:
seg(pos) ∈dom(route) ∧segment = route(seg(pos)) =⇒
segment ∈segments_of_route(route)
For a double segment position:
false =⇒
seg ∈segments_of_route(route)
Therefore the properties (sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∨sbid ∈rng(sboxes)) and
segment ∈segments_of_route(route) can be removed from the restricted guard
function. This results in the following restricted guard function for the reservation
event.
restricted_t_can_reserve : SboxMap × SwitchboxID ×
SwitchboxID × Segment × Route × Position ×
ResMap →Bool
restricted_t_can_reserve(sboxes,sbid,nextsb,segment,route,pos,res) ≡
∼(sbid ∈dom(res) ∧segment ∈res(sbid)) ∧
is_single_pos(pos) ∧
(sbid = nextsb ∨(nextsb ∈dom(sboxes) ∧sbid = sboxes(nextsb))) ∧
seg(pos) ∈dom(route) ∧segment = route(seg(pos))
6.6.2.2
Switch and Lock Guards
The other train guard functions for the switch and lock events now remain. They
should be restricted in a similar manner to the reservation event guard function.
==PAGE:115==
6.6 V3: Restricted System Model
99
However, as mentioned previously, the system as of now does not make it possible
for a train to “remember” when it has requested and received acknowledgement
for a switch event. Without this context it is not possible to neatly follow the
strategy’s steps of reservations, then switch, then lock etc. This has been solved
by merging the two events switch and lock such that any necessary switching of
a point happens implicitly whenever a train requests the locking of a point to
a specific connection. This ensures that the switching of a point to a requested
connection always happens exactly once before the point is locked.
Multiple
identical lock requests in a row can easily be prevented (as to follow the control
protocol steps) since trains can observe whether they have a lock for a point at a
switchbox. A discussion of an alternative solution can be found in Section 10.2.1.
s1
s2
s3
req_switch
ack_switch
switch
req_lock
ack_lock
lock
(a) Switch and lock as separate events.
s′
