switch_and_lock
(b) Switch and lock as a merged event.
Figure 6.12: Comparing switch and lock as separate events to the merged event.
In Figure 6.12a, a portion of the transition system of the previous version of
the model is shown. The states and transitions which are illustrated represent a
successful switch event, directly followed by a lock event. In the states s1, s2 and
s3 the system is idle, i.e. no action variables are true. In Figure 6.12b, a merged
switch and lock event is shown. As can be seen, the state (s2) between the two
events in the previous system version is omitted from the restricted system version
as an effect of the merging of events. However, the state s′
1 in the restricted system
directly corresponds to the state s1 in the previous system version, as does s′
3 to
s3. Since the rules of either system do not allow interleavings of other events
from the intermediate states (between the idle states), the safety of the restricted
system is not compromised by the event merging.
The merging of the switch and lock events does not make a big difference in
regards to the guard function restriction since the train guard functions for the
switch and lock event are identical except for the function names in the previous
versions of the model, as mentioned in Section 6.4.4. The original switch and lock
==PAGE:116==
100
6 Modelling
guard functions for the train were specified as follows (here only the lock guard
function is shown).
t_can_lock : Connection × Route × SboxMap × ResMap ×
SwitchboxID × Sboxset →Bool
t_can_lock(c,route,sboxes,res,sbid,locks) ≡
sbid /∈locks ∧sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∧
(∃seg : Segment • seg ∈dom(route) ∧
c = conn(seg,route(seg)) ∧sbid ∈dom(res) ∧

The restricted guard function for the merged switch and lock event is specified
as follows.
t_can_switch_and_lock : Connection × Route × SboxMap × ResMap ×
Position × SwitchboxID × SwitchboxID × Sboxset→Bool
t_can_switch_and_lock(c,route,sboxes,res,pos,sbid,nextsb,locks) ≡
sbid /∈locks ∧sbid = nextsb ∧
is_single_pos(pos) ∧sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∧seg(pos) ∈dom(route) ∧
c = conn(seg(pos),route(seg(pos))) ∧sbid ∈dom(res) ∧
seg(pos) ∈res(sbid) ∧
route(seg(pos)) ∈res(sbid) ∧
route(seg(pos)) ∈res(sboxes(sbid)),
Note that it is the restriction itself which comprises the refinement step of this
model. It can be discussed whether the merging of two events as described above
can be characterised as a refinement step. As can be seen from Figure 6.12, there
are some parallels between the event refinement described in Section 5.4 and the
merging of events. The merging of events can be seen as kind of an opposite of the
event refinement since multiple transitions are condensed into fewer transitions.
The important thing to observe here, however, is that the merging of the events
could just as well have happened as a separate step. The fact that it happened
as part of the final refinement is simply an effect of the restriction giving rise to
the realisation that these two events should have probably been linked from the
start.

6.6.4
Transition System Variables
As an effect of merging the switch and lock events, the action variables have now
changed to the following.
resAction : Bool = false,
switchLockAction : Bool = false
That is, the two variables switchAction and lockAction are replaced by a single
variable, switchLockAction. The use of the variables is the same as described in
Section 6.5, and they are also set to false since this should always be their start
value in the instantiations of the model.
6.6.5
Transition System Assertions
Since the action variables have changed, the additional assertions as presented in
Section 6.5.5 have also changed. They are now specified as follows.
[ finish_res ] TS ⊢G(resAction ⇒F(∼resAction)),
==PAGE:118==
102
6 Modelling
[finish_switch_and_lock] TS ⊢G(switchLockAction ⇒F(∼switchLockAction))
The assertions express, as before, that whenever an action is started, it will
eventually end.
6.6.6
Correctness of the Refinement
This section will informally argue that the restricted model version just introduced
is a refinement of the previous version, cf. Section 2.4. The intention is that V 3
should be a refinement of V 2.
The argument is similar to that of V 2 being a refinement of V 1. Considering
first the merging of the switch-and-lock event: for each atomic switch-and-lock
event in V 3 there is an equivalent state transformation in V 2 when restricting
the states to the variables of V 2. For the restriction of the events, it is clear, with
the strengthening the guards in V 3, that the possible transitions of V 3 are simply
a subset of V 2.
6.7
Instantiation
The generic models have now been explained. Before the process of verification
can begin, the models must be instantiated. This section presents the require-
ments for instantiations and summarises the instantiation which was used as an
example throughout the chapter.
6.7.1
Instantiation Requirements
In this section, the requirements for the system instantiations are listed. This
corresponds to the properties which must be satisfied in the start state of the
transition system.
The wellformedness, consistency and safety properties, as presented in Section
6.4.8, must hold. Additionally, the following requirements must be satisfied for
all trains of the instantiation:
• The train’s position must be a single segment position.
• The train’s route must be non-empty.
• The train has exactly the reservation required for its starting position (i.e.
at the next switchbox).
• The train has no locks.
All switchboxes of the system should be consistent with this, so for example,
all sensors are passive since all trains are in a single segment position.
==PAGE:119==
6.7 Instantiation
103
The satisfaction of these requirements makes it possible to deduce parts of
the configuration data of an instantiation from just the trains of the system,
their routes, the connection at the point of each switchbox, and the network
configuration (i.e. the network and the switchbox description). For the trains of
the system, the components can be deduced as follows.
• The position of each train can be deduced from the route of each train by
using the segment which is an element in the domain, but not in the range,
of the route.
• The switchboxes for each train’s route can be found by, for each (key, value)-
pair in the route, finding a switchbox in the switchbox description which is
associated with those segments as stem and branch, respectively.
• The next switchbox can be deduced from the switchboxes.
• The initial reservation for each train can be deduced from the next switch-
box and the starting position for each train.
• The initial set of locks of each train is assumed to be empty, so there is
nothing to deduce.
For the switchboxes of the system, the components can be deduced as follows.
• For the locked by components, there is nothing to deduce since all trains
are assumed to have no locks in the start state. Therefore, all locked by
components should have the value t_none.
• Similarly for each of the sensor states: since all trains are assumed to be in
a single position, all sensors should have the value passive.
• The reservations should simply be consistent with the train reservations, so
they are deduced in the same way.

==PAGE:121==
CHAPTER 7
Debugging Process
In this chapter, the principles used for debugging are presented. The principles
will, where necessary, be demonstrated using the example instantiations.
Before beginning the process of symbolic model checking different model in-
stances, other tools have been used to gain confidence in the correctness of the
function and transition system rule specifications. Generally, the tools which were
applied in this debugging process cannot be used alone for the total verification
of a system model since they do not explore the full extent of the state space.
However, exactly because the tools do not explore as many states as the symbolic
model checking tool, it is possible to receive an answer from them faster. This
makes the tools useful for investigating systems which are suspected to have bugs
since problems with the model can be found within minutes, rather than waiting
for the symbolic model checking of the model, which might take several hours,
depending on the total size of the state space. Naturally, whenever an error is
discovered in a subset of the total state space, this error will also be present in
the total state space.
Different tools have been used for different parts of the debugging process:
• SML testing has been used for verifying mainly the wellformedness and
consistency functions.
• The SAL Path-finder and Bounded Model Checker have been used for dis-
covering errors in the transition rules.
• The SAL Simulator has been used to verify the restriction of the rules
regarding the restricted version of the model.
The background for the tools is explained in Section 2.3.4 and Section 2.3.5.
The application for each of the tools will be explained in depth below. The SML
testing is described in Section 7.1, the path-finder and bounded model checker in
Section 7.2 and finally the simulator in Section 7.3.
7.1
Testing with SML
When the complexity of a system model grows as an effect of specifying more
functionality, naturally, it becomes harder to maintain the overview of the full
==PAGE:122==
106
7 Debugging Process
system and the states it can enter. During the development of especially the first
system model many functions were initially specified without taking anything
other than very obvious edge cases into account. In order to gain more confidence
in the function specification and discover unhandled edge cases, testing with SML
test cases was used. See Section 2.3.5 for a brief introduction to SML testing with
RSL.
A refined (and definitely more correct) system specification should be the
result of testing each of the relevant functions separately on system configura-
tions. Both configurations expected to satisfy and not satisfy e.g. wellformedness
functions were tested.
The SML testing was a very important and useful tool before beginning the
thorough verification by model checking since it could be used to show that each
function worked as expected, or to discover shortcomings causing the function
not to work as expected. Consider the case of model checking an assertion which
uses a non-trivial consistency function.
If the model checker claims that the
assertion does not hold, how can one be certain whether it is because the desired
property does not hold in the system, or whether it is because the function does
not correctly express the property? This illustrates the usefulness of first testing
the functions and ensuring that they express the desired property (in case of
wellformedness, consistency and guard functions) or express the desired change
in the system (in case of updater functions).
7.1.1
Wellformedness and Consistency Functions
SML testing of wellformedness and consistency functions has been very useful for
finding and correcting several bugs in the first system model. Each wellformed-
ness and consistency function has been tested separately on both configurations
expected to satisfy the functions and configurations expected to dissatisfy the
functions. The tests have been specified such that they should all result in true,
i.e. statements expected to be true are left as is, and statements expected to be
false are negated. The order with which the configurations are presented below
reflects the order in which the configurations have been tested. The configura-
tions and tests can be found in Appendix C.1. The tests results can be found in
Appendix C.2.
7.1.1.1
Network Wellformedness
The network wellformedness function has been tested with the following network
configurations expected to dissatisfy the wellformedness function.
• An empty network.
• A network with two symmetric connections, i.e. both a connection of the
form (a, b) and a connection of the form (b, a), where a and b are segments.
==PAGE:123==
7.1 Testing with SML
107
• Two networks with non-directional loops (as explained in Section 6.4.3).
• A network with a connection with no endpoints, i.e. an incomplete network.
• A network with a single connection with only one endpoint, i.e. an incom-
plete network.
The network wellformedness function has been tested with the following net-
work configurations expected to satisfy the wellformedness function.
• The example network used throughout this report.
• A minimal network with a single connection and two endpoints.
• A network with two straight connections and no loops.
• A network with a junction with three branches.
• A network similar to one of the networks with a non-directional loop tested
above, but with the segments given in the correct direction.
• A partitioned network, i.e. a network with two disjunct sub-networks.
The network wellformedness tests are named [nwf0]-[nwf11] in Appendix
C.1.
7.1.1.2
Switchbox Description Wellformedness
The switchbox description wellformedness function has been tested with the fol-
lowing switchboxes and network configurations expected to dissatisfy the well-
formedness function.
• A switchbox description which contains fewer switchboxes than required in
the given network.
• A switchbox description which contains more switchboxes than required in
the given network and which also contains a switchbox associated with a
segment not found in the network.
• A switchbox description which contains more switchboxes than required in
the given network but without having a switchbox associated with a seg-
ment not found in the network. Instead, there exists a duplicate switchbox
connection for two different switchbox identifiers.
The switchbox description wellformedness function has been tested with the
following switchboxes and network configurations expected to satisfy the well-
formedness function.
• The example switchbox description and network used throughout this re-
port.
==PAGE:124==
108
7 Debugging Process
• A minimal switchbox description and network (a single connection and two
endpoints).
• An empty switchbox description and an empty network. Even though the
empty network in itself is not wellformed, the empty switchbox description
with respect to the empty network is viewed as wellformed.
• A switchbox description correctly describing the switchboxes in a not well-
formed network (specifically a network with a symmetric description). This
follows the same reasoning as above – even if the network itself is not well-
formed, a switchbox description can well be wellformed with respect to it.
• A switchbox description and network with a junction with three branches.
• A switchbox description for a partitioned network and that partitioned net-
work.
The switchbox description wellformedness tests are named [sdwf0]-[sdwf8]
in Appendix C.1.

• A disconnected route in a network (the network does not make a difference
in this case).
• A route containing segments which are not contained in the network.
• A route “connecting” two segments which are not connected (but are con-
tained) in the network.
The train route wellformedness function has been tested with the following
route and network configurations expected to satisfy the wellformedness function.
• The empty route in some network.
• The example train route and network used throughout this report.
The train route wellformedness tests are named [trwf0]-[trwf4] in Appendix

• A single segment position where the segment is not contained in the network.

Train Position and Route Consistency
The train position and route consistency function has been tested with the fol-

• A double position of which one of the segments is not in the route.

The train position and route consistency function has been tested with the

• A single position of which the segment is in the domain of the route.
• A double position of which the first segment is in the domain of the route,
and the second segment is in the range of the route.

has reached its final destination.

7.1.1.9
Train Next Switchbox and Position Consistency
The train next switchbox and position consistency function has been tested with
the following switchboxes, positions and switchbox descriptions expected to dis-
satisfy the consistency function.
• A single segment position where the segment is associated (in the switchbox
description) with the given next switchbox identifier, but the switchbox is
not in the train’s switchboxes.
• A double segment position where none of the segments are associated (in
the switchbox description) with the given identifier for the next switchbox.
The train next switchbox and position consistency function has been tested
with the following switchboxes, positions and switchbox descriptions expected to
satisfy the consistency function.
• A single segment position where the segment is associated with the given
identifier for the next switchbox and the switchbox is in the train’s switch-
boxes. This corresponds to the train being in the position strictly before
the given next switchbox.
• A double segment position where both the segments are associated with the
given identifier for the next switchbox and the switchbox is in the train’s
switchboxes. This corresponds to the train being in the critical section at
the given next switchbox.

7.1.1.10
Train Reservation and Route Consistency
The train reservation and route consistency function has been tested with the
following reservations and routes expected to dissatisfy the consistency function.
• An empty reservation map with some non-empty route. The reservations
should never be empty since they must at least contain the reservation for
the train’s current position, so the route, in this case, does not matter.
• An empty reservation map with an empty route.
• A reservation for a segment which is not in the route (while the route is
non-empty).
The train reservation and route consistency function has been tested with the
following reservations and routes expected to satisfy the consistency function.
• The initial reservation map and route from the example configuration, i.e.
a reservation map containing a reservation for a segment which is in the
route.
• A reservation map containing a reservation while the route is empty. This
corresponds to the case where the train has reached its final destination and
has a reservation for the segment of its current position.
The train reservation and route consistency tests are named [trrc0]-[trrc4]
in Appendix C.1.

• A set of locks where the train has a lock for all the switchboxes in the map.
This includes a lock for the last switchbox in the switchbox map which
should never be locked by the train.
The train locks and switchboxes consistency function has been tested with the
following locks and switchbox maps expected to satisfy the consistency function.
• A set of locks where the train has a lock for all the switchboxes except for
the last switchbox in the switchbox map.

7.1.2
Scenario
Besides testing the wellformedness and consistency functions, the guard and up-
dater functions have also been tested by executing a small scenario using test
cases. Since effects on variable values from one test case persist in the following
test cases, it is rather straightforward to simulate the state changes which would
happen in the transition system by means of test cases.
The scenario which has been simulated corresponds to the example scenario
presented in Section 4.2. Between each event and before starting the simulation
the conjunction of all consistency functions are tested.
In short, the scenario
corresponds to the following steps.
1. The train T1 reserves segment S2 at SB1.
2. The train T1 reserves segment S2 at SB2.
3. The train T1 switches and locks the point at SB1.
4. The train T1 moves into the critical section (S1,S2).
5. The train T1 moves out of the critical section, and onto segment S2.
6. The train T1 reserves segment S4 at SB2.
7. The train T1 reserves segment S4 at SB4.
8. The train T1 switches and locks the point at SB2.
9. The train T1 moves into the critical section (S2,S4).
10. The train T1 moves out of the critical section, and onto segment S4.
The scenario test cases can be found in Appendix C.1. The test results can
be found in Appendix C.2.
7.2
Error Discovery in the Transition Systems
After gaining confidence in the function specifications, it is time to gain confidence
in the transition system rules which is where the functions are used. For this
==PAGE:132==
116
7 Debugging Process
purpose, the path finder (see Section 2.3.4.4) and the bounded model checker
(see Section 2.3.4.2) have been used.
7.2.1
Bounded Model Checking
Bounded model checking has been used as a tool for discovering bugs in the
transition system rules. This was especially useful before optimisations had been
applied to the first system model. The unoptimised version of the model in some
cases took several hours to model check with the symbolic model checker, whereas
bounded model checking of the assertions could be performed in a few minutes.
Since it was suspected that the system contained some not-yet-discovered bugs,
a good starting point was to use bounded model checking of a reasonable depth.
From the user’s point of view, the bounded model checking process does not
differ greatly from that of symbolic model checking.
The major difference is
the extent to which the total state space is explored, which naturally affects the
time and memory consumption. With the bounded model checking the possible
paths in the transition system are only explored to a certain, given depth. It is
attempted to verify the model against the same properties, given by the assertions
presented throughout Chapter 6.
More specifically speaking, the bounded model checking was performed on
the example instantiation of the model V 1 presented in Section 6.7.2. The safety,
wellformedness and consistency assertions were model checked, exploring depths
such that at least the shortest paths, describing scenarios where the train reaches
its final destination, were explored. It can be calculated how many transitions
are at least necessary for the train to be able to reach its final destination. In the
case of the instantiation in question, the least amount of necessary transitions
can be calculated as follows.
2 reservations for S2 + 2 reservations for S4+
1 switch at SB1 + 1 switch at SB2+
1 lock at SB1 + 1 lock at SB2+
4 movement steps =
12 transitions
Therefore, when performing bounded model checking on the instance of the
model, it was done with depth 12, meaning that the states of any legal interleaving
of the 12 transitions above would be explored.
To show the difference between finding bugs with the bounded model checker
and the symbolic model checker, the example instantiation with another train
added of model V 2 is used. This instantiation is shown later, in Section 8.1.1.
Consider the case where the initial reservation for one of the trains, say T2, is
==PAGE:133==
7.2 Error Discovery in the Transition Systems
117
forgotten in the configuration data. This leads to a state where the trains can
collide if the route of T1 overlaps the starting position of T2 since there will be
nothing stopping the T1 from reserving the segment where T2 starts out. Thus,
the model checking tools should find a counter-example when trying to verify the
no collide assertion.
In Table 7.1, the time to find a counter-example when attempting to verify
the no collide property is shown. The raw data can be found in Appendix D.1.
As can be seen, the bounded model checker uses much less memory and finds
a counter-example much faster than the symbolic model checker. This should
illustrate well why the bounded model checker is a useful bug-finding tool. The
counter-examples found by the bounded and the symbolic model checker are, in
this case, not the same, but do of course reveal the same problem, namely that
the trains are able to enter the same segment.

Table 7.1: Execution time in minutes:seconds and memory consumption in GB
for the attempted verification (i.e.
finding a counter-example) for the no col-
lide assertion for the symbolic model checker and the bounded model checker,
respectively.
7.2.2
Path-Finder
The path-finder tool has been used for gaining confidence in the event ordering
and the event effects, especially before optimisations were applied to the system.
At that point, it was not possible to start the simulator tool (of which the use
is discussed in Section 7.3), so viewing traces of transitions was otherwise not
possible. Even though the path-finder simply selects an arbitrary path of the
specified length, it helped discover inconsistencies and errors in the system since
the applied transition rule and the effect of the rule are shown for each step taken.
For example, an overly weak guard function was discovered using the path finder,
which showed a transition rule being applied in an unexpected state.
On the other hand, the path finder was also used to discover a guard function
which was too restrictive, resulting in a deadlock. A path of a particular length
is requested when applying the path-finder tool, and if no path of such length is
possible, the tool will return with an error message.
To show how the path-finder can be used to discover a deadlock in a system,
a larger instantiation (specifically the shared segment instantiation presented in
==PAGE:134==
118
7 Debugging Process
Section 8.1.2 and found in Appendix B.3) is made of model V 3. The model is
altered slightly such that the req_res rule is too strict. In fact, “false
∧” is
simply added before the rest of the guard of the transition rule, such that this
rule can never be applied. This is of course only done for the purpose of this
experiment.
In Table 7.2, the time to fail to find a path of length 10 with the path-finder
and the time before the deadlock checker ran out of memory is shown along with
the memory consumption. The raw data can be found in Appendix D.2. As can
be seen, the deadlock checker failed to find a deadlock since it simply ran out
of memory before being able to complete. However, the path-finder quickly fails
to find a path of length 10 in the system. Since the system (without the too
restricted guard) is certainly expected to have a trace with more than 10 steps,
the path-finder results indicate that there is an error.

Table 7.2: Execution time in minutes:seconds and memory consumption in GB
for running the deadlock checker and the path-finder, respectively.
7.3
Demonstrating the Transition System Structure
The simulator tool, introduced in Section 2.3.4.5, can be used for viewing the
possible transitions from a state, selecting the desired transition and viewing
the effects of that transition as a new state. Essentially, one can simulate the
exploration of a specific path in the system. This was especially useful for the
development of the model V 3, where the system was restricted to much fewer
possible transitions.
It is possible, in that case, to use the simulator tool for convincing oneself that
the system is indeed restricted to the desired control protocol by going through
the system. It is then visible that the only possible transitions are those dictated
by the control protocol. The simulation trace for the example instance of the
restricted model version can be seen in Appendix D.6.
==PAGE:135==
CHAPTER 8
Model Checking
Instantiations of the generic models described in Sections 6.4, 6.5 and 6.6 have
been translated to SAL and subsequently model checked against the assertions
presented throughout Chapter 6 using the SAL Symbolic Model Checker. This
chapter presents the instantiations, besides the one presented in Section 6.7.2, on
which model checking has been performed. Furthermore, the verification results
are presented for each model version and each instantiation. As mentioned in
Section 2.3.3.1, the RSL-SAL translator produces three different versions. The
first version is the one on which model checking against the assertions is performed.
On the other versions, the confidence conditions (generated by the translator) can
be checked. Results are presented for both the assertion checking and confidence
condition checking.
Besides model checking the models against the assertions
and confidence conditions, the models are also attempted to be proven free of
deadlocks by using the SAL Deadlock Checker. Finally, the verification metrics,
i.e. the time and memory consumption for checking the properties, are presented
for each model and each instantiation.
The model checking has been performed on a DTU Compute server with an
Intel(R) Xeon(R) CPU E5-2660 v3 @ 2.60GHz and 125 GiB of memory. It should
be noted that there are other users running other processes on the same server,
which may affect the model checking process. Therefore, the verification metrics
should be viewed in this context.
8.1
Model Instantiations
In order to be able to model check, the generic models must be instantiated with
concrete system configuration data. Previously, the instantiation corresponding
to the example system which has been used throughout the report has been
introduced. The instantiation is shown in Section 6.7.2.
In this section, the additional instantiations of the model versions which have
been model checked are presented. For clarity, the instantiations will be given
in the form of illustrations of the initial state of the configuration data.
The
instantiations have of course been specified in a similar manner as the example
instantiation shown in Section 6.7.2, but this will not be shown here. The full
instantiations can be found in Appendix B.

• For model V 1 is was possible to verify all assertions for the shared segment

quantified over e.g. all train identifiers. For example, attempting to verify
the quantified no derail assertion resulted in memory exhaustion. Therefore,
the assertion was instead specified for each of the two train identifiers of the
system, and these assertions were checked seperately.

the assertions using the least memory. Therefore, no other assertions were
attempted to be checked for this instantiation of V 1.
• For both model V 2 and V 3 it was possible to verify the safety properties
(i.e. no derail and no collide), even without unfolding the assertions, for the
shared segment instantiation. However, proceeding to attempt to verify the
wellformedness and consistency assertions resulted in memory exhaustion.
• For V 2 the larger instantiation was not attempted verified since this resulted
in memory exhaustion for V 1 (which is a smaller model in terms of state
space).
• For V 3 the larger instantiation was attempted checked against the no collide
assertion for experimentation’s sake. The process was stopped manually
after approximately 72 hours of no result.
8.2.2
Showing Arrival
The properties expressing that trains do not arrive are expected to produce
counter-examples. I.e. if the model checker cannot prove these assertions and
instead produces a counter-example trace, this shows that it is possible for trains
to reach their destination. This corresponds to the existence of a scheduling of
the train routes such that the trains do not deadlock. The results of attempting
to verify these properties are presented in Table 8.5. As can be seen, counter-
examples were found for the example instantiations with one and two trains for
each of the model versions, and for the shared segment instantiation for model
V 1 and V 3. It was not attempted to find counter-examples for the larger instan-
tiation since this instantiation could not be verified for any of the model versions.
The counter-example traces can be found in Appendix F.

8.2.3
Checking Confidence Conditions
The CC-versions of the instances of the models should be checked in order to
ensure soundness of the model checking results. It was attempted to model check
the simple CC-version of the example instance of the model V 1. The process was
manually terminated after approximately 19 hours of no result. Another attempt
was made at checking the simple CC-version of the example instance of the model
V 3. This gave the following output.
The assertion 'V3_SingleTrain_switchboxes_cc_check ' located at
[Context: V3_SingleTrain_cc_simple , line(1068), column(0)] is valid.
The assertion 'V3_SingleTrain_network_cc_check ' located at
[Context: V3_SingleTrain_cc_simple , line(1069), column(0)] is valid.
The assertion 'V3_SingleTrain_TS_cc_check ' located at
[Context: V3_SingleTrain_cc_simple , line(1671), column(0)] is valid.
The assertion 'V3_SingleTrain_TS_cc_check_guards ' located at
[Context: V3_SingleTrain_cc_simple , line(1688), column(0)] is invalid.
As seen in the last line of the output, there is one of the assertions which is
invalid. The SAL file V3_SingleTrain_cc_simple was investigated at line 1688,
but it is not clear which part of the (very long) assertion cannot be verified. The
regular CC-version of the same instance of the same model version was attempted
verified since this should give more verbose information. However, this process
exhausted memory after approximately 13 hours. The raw data can be seen in
Appendix E.5.
The fact that one of the CC assertions is invalid indicates that there may
exist a problem such as a function being called without its preconditions being
fulfilled. Investigating the system specification, in particular where functions with
pre-conditions are called, did not result in the discovery of any problems.
The outcome is that it has not been possible to verify the confidence conditions
for the models. Therefore the verification results for the assertions presented in
this chapter should be viewed in this context. Only under the assumption of the
confidence conditions can the models be said to be verified.
8.2.4
Checking for Deadlocks
Normally, since LTL formulas are only defined for infinite traces, the model in-
stances must be shown to be free of deadlocks for the verification results for the
assertions to be sound. SAL does produce sound results over finite traces for
G-properties, however [21]. SAL will declare any F-property true if there exists
deadlocks in the model instance. In Table 8.6 the results for checking for dead-
locks in the different instances of each of the model versions can be seen. The
raw data can be found in Appendix E.4.

As can be seen from Table 8.6, the example and example with two trains in-

with the deadlock checker for the shared segment instance for any of the model ver-
sions within a reasonable time. The process of checking for deadlocks was started
for each of the model versions, but for model V 1 and V 2 the process was stopped
manually after several hours. For model V 3 memory was exhausted. However,
intuitively, all the shared segment instances must contain deadlock states. This is
caused by the possibility for T1 to reserve segment S3 at SB2 and SB3 before T2.
After switching and locking the point at SB2, T1 can enter S3, but subsequently
cannot proceed to its final destination, S4, since this is where T2 is. Additionally,
T2 has no possibilities to become able to move since T1 is in the way.
For the larger instantiation the deadlock checker has not been run since it has
not been possible to show the presence or absence of deadlocks in the shared seg-
ment instantiation. It should be noted, though, that the same deadlock situation
(as described above) exists in the larger instantiation as in the shared segment
instantiation.

for the small example instance. The differences in execution time and memory
consumption may as well be attributed to the measurements being performed on
different days where the simultaneous usage of the server by other users can vary.
However, the difference in the complexity of model checking the different model
versions becomes clear when adding another train to the same network (i.e. the
example with two trains instantiation). Especially for model V 2 the time and
memory consumption grow rapidly. It is not surprising that model checking in-
stances of V 2 gives the worst performance – after all, it is the model version with
the largest state space and the most transition rules. The reason for the fact that
the time and memory usage for instantiations of model V 3 grow more rapidly
than that of model V 1 might be less obvious. Model V 3 is the restricted model,
so even though each event consists of three sub-transitions, the number of possible
==PAGE:142==
126
8 Model Checking
transitions from each state should be smaller than in model V 1 where events are
not restricted to a control protocol as strict. However, V 3 contains more variables
than V 1, and updates to these variables happen in a lot of the transition rules.
As mentioned in Chris George’s advice (Section 2.3.3.4), the number of variable
updates per transition rule has an effect on the model checking performance.

Section 6.6.5, respectively). Figure 8.11 gives an overview of the time used for
checking safety, wellformedness/consistency and liveness assertions, respectively,
for the example with two trains instance for each model version. As can be seen,
the checking of the liveness properties for model V 2 and V 3 does, of course,
contribute to the total time taken for model checking, but even without the
liveness properties, the model checking for the instances of V 2 and V 3 has poorer
performance.

CHAPTER 9
Experiments
Throughout this project, several experiments with different specification styles, or
approaches, have been performed. As can be seen from the advice given by Chris
George (see Section 2.3.3.4), the way the specifications of the transition system
rules are formulated makes a difference for the model checking performance. It
is unclear whether this has to do with the SAL model checker tool itself, or if
it reflects a problem with the RSL-SAL translator. This chapter presents the
results of the experiments conducted and, based on these, gives some general
recommendations for anyone writing formal specifications in RSL-SAL with the
intention of model checking the specifications with SAL.
9.1
Quantifications in Assertions
The assertions to be verified for the transition system can be specified in different
ways when they are concerned with all variables of a certain type. Of course,
the assertions can be specified for each variable individually, but they can also
be specified with a universal quantifier. As an example, consider the first model
version (V 1) of this project instantiated with the data for the small example
system as given in Section 6.7.2. Furthermore, consider the consistency functions
cons_t_sb_res and cons_sb_t_res which check that the train and switchbox
reservations are consistent from the point of view of the train and of the switchbox,
respectively. Measurements have been made for the verification of the assertions
specified in the following different ways.
• An assertion for each of the two consistency functions for each tid,sbid pair
where tid is a train identifier and sbid is a switchbox identifier, i.e. assertions
of the following form for each tid,sbid pair.
In the case of the example
instantiation this results in a total of eight assertions (four switchboxes and
one train).
[cons1] TS ⊢G(cons_t_sb_res(tid,sbid,tRes,sbRes)),
[cons2] TS ⊢G(cons_sb_t_res(tid,sbid,tRes,sbRes)),
...
==PAGE:146==
130
9 Experiments
• An assertion for each of the two consistency functions quantified over each
tid,sbid pair, i.e. assertions of the following form.
[cons1] TS ⊢(∀sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID •
G(cons_t_sb_res(tid,sbid,tRes,sbRes))),
[cons2] TS ⊢(∀sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID •
G(cons_sb_t_res(tid,sbid,tRes,sbRes)))
• A single assertion for both of the two consistency functions quantified over
each tid,sbid pair, i.e. an assertion of the following form.
[cons1] TS ⊢(∀sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID •
G(cons_t_sb_res(tid,sbid,tRes,sbRes) ∧
cons_sb_t_res(tid,sbid,tRes,sbRes)))
• A single assertion for both of the two consistency functions quantified over
each tid,sbid pair, but with the quantification “inside” the global operator,
i.e. an assertion of the following form.
[cons1] TS ⊢G(∀sbid : SwitchboxID, tid : TrainID •
cons_t_sb_res(tid,sbid,tRes,sbRes) ∧
cons_sb_t_res(tid,sbid,tRes,sbRes))
For each case, the corresponding assertions were model checked, and the time
and maximum memory usage was recorded. Each assertion was checked three
times, and the recorded metrics for each were then averaged. In the following,
the results for time and memory consumption are presented. The raw data can
be seen in Appendix D.3.
In Figure 9.1 and Figure 9.2 the results for time and the results for memory,
respectively, for each approach to formulating the assertions can be seen. The
first column, Individual, represents the sum of the average times for all eight
individual assertions. Similarly, for the second column, Quantified functions –
this represents the sum of the average times for both assertions of that form.
The results are very interesting: the two semantically equivalent assertions
with the universal quantifier outside and inside the global operator, respectively,
have vastly different running times. It is surprising that this difference in formula-
tion makes such a difference in regard to the performance. It is hard to say what
exactly causes this difference, but something different must be going on behind
the scenes in the SAL symbolic model checker for each formulation.
Another result that should be mentioned is the fact that checking a single,
individual assertion (i.e. one of the eight assertions which are summed in the
==PAGE:147==
9.1 Quantifications in Assertions
131
Individual column), takes, on average, about 1 minute and 5 seconds, whereas the
assertion with quantified, conjuncted functions takes just 49 seconds on average.
Thus, it seemingly takes longer to check consistency from one point of view (either
of the train or the switchbox) of an individual tid,sbid-pair than it does to check
the consistency from both points of view for all tid,sbid-pairs. The same pattern
is seen for the memory consumption. Again, some optimisation must be going on
behind the scenes in SAL.
Figure 9.1: Time in minutes for checking the assertions formulated individually,
quantified for each function, quantified for both functions and quantified for both
functions with the quantification inside.
Figure 9.2: Maximum memory usage in GB for checking the assertions formu-
lated individually, quantified for each function, quantified for both functions and
quantified for both functions with the quantification inside.
It should be noted, though, that even though the total time and memory for
==PAGE:148==
132
9 Experiments
running the individual assertions are greater than those of running the quantified
versions, it may still be sensible to unfold the quantifications for larger systems.
In the case of this small model instance, each individual assertion uses more time
and memory than the quantified version. However, for larger system instances the
amount of time and memory needed for checking the quantified assertions may rise
more rapidly than the amount for checking unfolded assertions. During the model
checking process, it was seen that unfolding the quantifications can allow one to
check assertions which otherwise exhausted the memory. For example, for the
shared segment instance of model V 1, it was not possible to check the quantified
no derail assertion, but checking the unfolded no derail for each individual train
identifier was indeed possible.
9.2
Data Structures
In the transition system rules, different system variables are typically observed
(in the guards) and updated (in the effects). When variables are saved in data
structures, the lookup time for a value will usually depend on the data structure.
The following experiments serve to investigate the effects on the model checking
performance of saving the same data in different manners in the RSL specification.
The following approaches have been investigated.
• Maps in the form they have been used in the model versions presented in
Chapter 6.
• Data records which was the initial approach for saving train data and switch-
box data in separate structures as mentioned in Section 2.3.3.4.
• Arrays which are part of Jacob Hansen’s extension of RSL-SAL as men-
tioned in Section 3.2.
Additionally, it would have been interesting to investigate saving the data as
separate variables. This approach does not allow for generic specifications of e.g.
the transition rules, and therefore required extensive changes to the specifications.
Because of time constraints of the project, this was not investigated further.
In Figure 9.3 and Figure 9.4 the results for time and memory, respectively, for
each approach to saving data can be seen. The same assertion has been checked
in all cases. Specifically, an assertion stating that a train in the system reached
some segment not in its route was used. The raw data can be found in Appendix
D.4.
As can be seen from the figures, saving data in maps is by far the optimal
solution of the three investigated ones. As was already noted previously along
with Chris George’s advice (in Section 2.3.3.4), records with many fields should
not be used in models where it is often only one or a few fields which are updated at
the time. The worst performance, both in terms of time and memory consumption
==PAGE:149==
9.2 Data Structures
133
is seen when using arrays. It should be taken into account that the model was
written and optimised for using maps. Therefore, altering the model to save data
in arrays instead may not have been done the optimal way. However, some of
the difference in performance may also be explained by the fact that arrays are a
“non-native” extension to RSL-SAL, and that the translation might not function
optimally as a result.
Figure 9.3: Time in minutes:seconds for checking the assertions with the data
saved in maps, records and arrays.
Figure 9.4: Memory usage in GB for checking the assertions with the data saved
in maps, records and arrays.
==PAGE:150==
134
9 Experiments
9.3
Unfolding of Top-level Functions
As part of his advice about writing RSL-SAL specifications (see Section 2.3.3.4),
Chris George recommends that the transition system is split into multiple trans-
itions instead of having branching functions as transition rule effects when the
split transitions will affect fewer state variables. This section investigates the ef-
fects on performance on following this advice. The raw data for the performance
measurements made can be found in Appendix D.5.
Originally, at the very start of the modelling process, the advice was applied to
a system model using data records for train data and switchbox data as described
in Section 6.4.2.1.
As mentioned, using the record type, all fields had to be
updated even if only a single field had to be changed. Therefore, in order to
follow George’s advice and make the collection of state updates smaller in the
split transitions, the data, which was before fields in a record, had to be saved
in separate variables for each field (the same way they now are in the three
presented model versions).
Then, each split transition could update only the
relevant variables instead of updating all fields of the record. A comparison of
the performance measurements (time and memory consumption) can be seen in
Figure 9.5. A measurement is made for instances of two different systems:
• The system with data records and a single transition rule for move, where
branching decisions are made based on whether the current position of the
train is a single segment position or a double segment position.
• The system with the data in separate variables (maps) and split transitions
for each branching of the top-level move effect function.
Figure 9.5: Execution time in minutes:seconds for checking a single assertion in
the system with un-split (merged) transitions and data records, and in the system
with split transitions and maps.
==PAGE:151==
9.3 Unfolding of Top-level Functions
135
As seen from Figure 9.5, the changes made based on the advice did indeed
improve the performance for model checking an instance of the system model.
However, this improvement was probably, at the time, taken as a too strong im-
plication of the positive effects of applying the piece of advice. When the trans-
itions were first split, the difference in performance was not measured formally
and in-depth, but simply observed to have improved (i.e. reduced) the running
time of checking an assertion.
Therefore, the split transitions were kept throughout the modelling process, as
can be seen, for example, by the two transitions res_sbin and res_sbnotin in all
three versions of the model. As part of the experiments conducted in this section,
it was investigated which effect the split transitions have on the model checking
of the developed system models. These experiments were conducted quite late
in the thesis period, because the main aim was to substantiate the advice given
by Chris George, rather than comparing the performance of verifying different
development stages of the system models.
The effect of splitting the transitions was tested by “un-applying” the advice,
and merging the transitions for move and reserve back together in a copy of model
V 1. Note that the variable data types were kept, though, and not changed back
to records. In Figure 9.6, a comparison between the models with and without the
advice applied can be seen for two instantiations – the experiment instantiation
and the experiment with two trains instantiation, respectively.
Figure 9.6: Execution time in minutes:seconds for checking a single assertion on
two different instantiations (example and example with two trains) of a version of
V 1 with un-split (merged) transitions and of V 1 with split transitions.
As seen from Figure 9.6, splitting the transitions is, in fact, negatively affecting
the time it takes to check an assertion. It should be noted that it is not the piece
of advice from Chris George which is faulty – it is indeed true, that changing
fewer state variables per transition and, as an effect, having more transitions, has
a positive effect on the execution time of checking an assertion. However, the
==PAGE:152==
136
9 Experiments
results presented here indicate that it is not the splitting of transitions in itself
which makes the difference – rather, it is the change from using records (and being
forced to update all fields of the record), to using another approach (making it
possible to change a single variable’s value independently), making the difference.
9.4
Recommendations
Based on the above experimental results, this section will briefly present the
recommendations for writing formal specifications in RSL-SAL.
Quantify assertions
for smaller model instances with the universal quantifier
outside the global operator.
Unfold assertions
for larger model instances when quantified assertions be-
come too complex to verify within a reasonable time.
Use maps
as data structures if only a few entries in the data structure should
be updated at the time.
Do not split transitions
unnecessarily, but do focus on having each transition
update as few state variables as possible.
In the author’s experience, following this advice along with the other advice of
Chris George is advisable and will result in a model checking process with better
performance.
==PAGE:153==
CHAPTER 10
Discussion
This chapter contains a discussion of the model checking performance and results
along with the optimisation experiments performed. Furthermore, some essential
design choices which were made will be critically assessed, and several future work
items will be presented.
10.1
Performance
In this section, the results from the model checking process (Chapter 8) and the
conducted experiments (Chapter 9) will be discussed. From the verification results
and verification metrics presented in Section 8.2 and Section 8.3, respectively, it
is clear that there are some problems with the scalability of the verification of the
system models. In spite of attempting to optimise the system specifications as
much as possible, both by following the advice of Chris George (Section 2.3.3.4)
and by e.g. using the most optimal data structure available (Section 9.2), it has
only been possible to verify very small example system configurations within a
reasonable time (and with a reasonable memory consumption).
These limitations prevent the developed system model specifications from be-
ing used for real-world applications – at least when using the SAL model checker
as a verification tool. In order to make such applications viable with SAL, the
system models must be substantially optimised. This gives rise to the challenge of
identifying the underlying cause – or causes – of the poor verification performance.
Whether it is due to the RSL-SAL translation or the SAL model checking tool is
difficult to determine. Without having a deeper understanding of the syntax of
SAL it is not possible to determine whether the generated translations contain
some sub-optimal constructs, and without having a deeper understanding of the
inner workings of the SAL model checker, it is not possible to identify what are
sub-optimal constructs – if any.
Admittedly, the developed RSL specifications might affect the performance
as well. As seen in Section 9.3, the transition splitting has negatively affected
the performance of model checking the developed system models. However, it is
doubtful that mending this mistake would solve all of the performance issues, even
though it would most likely allow slightly larger instantiations to be successfully
checked.
==PAGE:154==
138
10 Discussion
In Jacob Bøgelund Hansen’s thesis [13] (see Section 3.2), where RSL-SAL
and the SAL model checker were also used, the performance results are in line
with those of this thesis. This indicates that there is a tendency for sub-optimal
performance when using the aforementioned tools, although one cannot directly
compare the results of the theses since the system model developed by Hansen
is quite different to the ones developed in this thesis. Furthermore, Hansen has
experimented with verifying the same system model with the RT-Tester tool and
obtained better performance results.
Therefore, it is plausible that one could
obtain better performance with the system models of this thesis as well.
Another way of potentially obtaining better performance could be to specify
the system models in a native input language for another model checking tool such
as UMC. In Michel Nielsen’s thesis [18] (see Section 3.3), a similar interlocking
system to the one of this thesis, was modelled and verified with UMC. Nielsen’s
results indicate that it is possible to verify more complex system configurations
in UMC than in SAL, and therefore it would be interesting to investigate the
differences in performance if one were to specify the system modelled in this
thesis in UMC as well.
When comparing the performance results of this thesis to the results of the
work of Haxthausen et al. in the article used as the case study [16], it is clear,
that model checking as a verification approach of more complex systems has some
weaknesses compared to theorem proving. The state space explosion problem very
quickly prevented even small systems from being successfully verified with model
checking.
However, theorem proving has the disadvantage of being very time
consuming (as well) and requires a lot of effort since the verification process must
be guided by a human. To say whether model checking is a viable alternative
for verifying complex systems such as railway interlocking systems, further invest-
igations are necessary. Other model checker back-ends for RSL or other model
checking tools might yield better results in terms of performance than the ones
presented in this thesis.
10.2
Design
In this section, some essential design choices of the system models which have
been specified in this thesis will be considered. It will be discussed whether the
modelling design choices made are well-founded, and alternatives to the chosen
approaches will be described.
10.2.1
Alternative to Merging Events
In Section 6.6 the switch and lock events were merged into a single event as a
way of ensuring the order of events, and ensuring that switch was not requested
multiple times unnecessarily.
It would have been possible to keep the events
==PAGE:155==
10.2 Design
139
separate and independent of each other by adding a variable to the state space of
the train control computers. This variable could then be used for remembering
that a train had already successfully had a point switched (i.e. that the train had
received a positive acknowledgement of the switch event from the switchbox in
question). This variable would have to be reset whenever the train moves from a
double segment position to a single segment position.
Solving the problem of restricting the event ordering with just one extra vari-
able would probably have been more straight-forward than merging the two events.
This way, the refinement step from model V 2 to V 3 could also have been clearer.
However, the approach of merging the events was chosen as the solution since the
restriction step gave rise to the realisation that the switch and lock events should
have been interconnected from the beginning of the modelling process.
10.2.2
Event Refinement
In the refinement step between model V 1 and model V 2, described in Section
6.5, the method of atomic event refinement was applied. However, the question
remains whether it was necessary to use atomic event refinement over the non-
atomic refinement (see Section 5.4). Atomic event refinement is the simpler of
the two approaches, but admittedly, there are some disadvantages of using atomic
event refinement. For example, the atomic refinement approach which was applied
prevents two different trains from requesting reservations of different segments at
different switchboxes at the same time. This is, of course, not desirable since
these two events should not affect each other – and therefore there should be no
problem interleaving them.
Atomic event refinement is necessary when two events access the same shared
resource, requiring mutual exclusion.
To answer the question of whether the
choice of atomic event refinement is justifiable, it must be investigated which re-
sources (variables) are accessed by each event (in each transition rule) and whether
this happens in a way requiring mutual exclusion. Looking at the transition rules
in model V 1, it can be seen that they correspond to each train control computer
making changes to its local state space, and, similarly, each switchbox making
changes to its local state space. At no point do the control components access
a shared resource. This indicates that non-atomic event refinement would have
been a viable approach.
If the model V 1 were to be refined with the non-atomic event refinement
approach instead, it would be necessary to handle requests and acknowledgements
in a different way.
Furthermore, the communication data, which is saved in
tmpSeg and tmpConn in model V 2, would have to be saved in another way. The
current approach to the communication variables and the data variables assumes
that events cannot interleave with other events. There would, for example, have
to exist request and acknowledgements variables for each of the events (except
move), instead of only having one request variable, one acknowledge variable, and
==PAGE:156==
140
10 Discussion
one negative acknowledge variable for each (train, switchbox)-pair.
The disadvantage of choosing non-atomic event refinement would have been
the vast amount of interleavings of events.
It is possible that this approach
would have affected the verification performance negatively – it might even have
prevented verification of the event refined model version.
10.3
Future Work
This section presents work items which are natural next steps if building upon
the work of this thesis. Some of the work items are directly related to the work
of the thesis and would contribute to the investigation of the viability of using
the SAL model checker for verifying RSL specifications, whereas other work items
are concerned with producing results which could be compared with those of this
thesis.
10.3.1
State Space Explosion
Given the results of Chapter 8 where state space explosion quickly becomes un-
manageable, it would be interesting to investigate which optimisations could be
applied to the models and how effective these optimisations would be. Proposals
for some initial optimisations are presented here.
• Naturally, a contributor to the size of the state space is the amount of
variables in the system.
In order to model the distribution of the state
spaces, data is of course duplicated as explained previously. However, the
system models have some unnecessary data duplication. For example, the
network configuration and the switchbox description describe a lot of the
same information. Therefore, one proposal for optimisation is to come up
with a way of specifying the network topology (including the switchboxes
and their related track segments) such that as little data as possible is
duplicated.
• From Chris George’s advice (presented in Section 2.3.3.4) it is evident that
the transition rules should be specified in such a way that each rule only up-
dates a few variables. The rule move_double, which updates nine variables,
is not following this advice. An attempt was made of partitioning this rule
into multiple rules, but as of now, the updates to the variables depend on
the fact that they all happen atomically – for example, a lot of the called
updater functions refer to the “old” value of the next switchbox variable.
Because of this, to partition the rule a lot of auxiliary variables were needed
for keeping track of both the “old” variable values and which “step” of the
rule was the next. This had the effect of no notable optimisation being
visible, so the more readable rule was kept for simplicity. If one can devise
==PAGE:157==
10.3 Future Work
141
a better way of achieving the partitioning of the rule, this would lead to
optimisation, though.
• In the current system models, the rules are quantified over all train identi-
fiers. Of course, this includes the auxiliary t_none value. Strictly speaking,
the transition rules should not be applied to t_none, but the guard func-
tions will fail for the value, and therefore, none of the transition rule effects
will be applied to the value. However, it might still be more optimal to
simply add ∧tid ̸= t_none to the guard functions.
10.3.2
Using the Bounded Model Checker and k-induction
In this thesis, the bounded model checker of SAL has been used for searching
for counter-examples and fixing bugs, but the bounded model checker also has
another use – it can be used to perform verification of models by k-induction [20].
With k-induction
[...] one attempts to prove that a property holds in the current
state, assuming that it holds in the previous k consecutive states. In
addition, for completeness, one has to add an additional constraint
that specifies that the states along a path must be unique. [2]
Thus, it can be possible to verify properties without exploring all the states
of the system. Because symbolic model checking and bounded model checking do
not use the same underlying technologies, with one approach it might be possible
to verify a property much faster than with the other, depending on the system in
question [20]. Therefore, a very natural way to proceed with the investigations
of this thesis would be to experiment with using the bounded model checker of
SAL for k-induction and compare the resulting metrics with those of the symbolic
model checker presented in Section 8.3.
With SAL k-induction can be performed by using commands of the form
sal-bmc --depth=<k> --induction <context name> <assertion name>.
10.3.3
Model Checking Performance on a Centralised System Model
It could potentially be interesting to compare the performance of model checking
on a model of a centralised system to the model checking of the model specified
in this thesis. For obvious reasons, the state space of a centralised system would
be smaller, so in all likelihood, the model checking would perform better. This
could be done with the V 1 model as a starting point, where the data duplication
from the state spaces of switchboxes and train control computers should then be
eliminated, such that a centralised control computer was more closely modelled.
==PAGE:158==
142
10 Discussion
10.3.4
Different Control Algorithms
In model V 3 the system was restricted to a just-in-time allocation principle. It
could be interesting to compare the effects of restricting the system to different
control protocols, both in regards to the performance of the model checking, but
also in regards to avoiding deadlocks and having all trains be able to reach their
destination. An example of a control protocol which could be tested is the more
traditional allocation principle of reserving the full train route and locking all
necessary points before starting to move.
Finding optimal control protocols which ensure nice scheduling of the trains
is outside the scope of this project. However, it could be interesting to see how
advanced control protocols the specification allows for.
10.3.5
Restructuring for Configuration Data Tool
A nice feature of the generic models would be the ability to specify the configur-
ation data for the instantiations in a single file, rather than having to specify the
data at placed spread around different files. With a bit of restructuring of the
model specifications, this could be achieved such that the configuration data can
be given as a single file, which the other file(s) of the system extend. With this in
place, a simple tool could be developed, which could generate the configuration
file based on user input. This could be implemented in such a way that the user
does not need to know the syntax of RSL-SAL in order to input configuration
data.
10.3.6
Unfolding Assertions
Since the size of the total state space of the model instances has the potential to
make model checking of some of the quantified assertions impossible (at least with
a reasonable memory and time consumption), a small tool could be developed for
unfolding the quantified assertions according to the configuration data.
10.3.7
Other Verification Frameworks
There is a need for a well-founded comparison between the usefulness, possib-
ilities and performance of the different verification back-ends available in RSL.
For example, the other verification frameworks RT-Tester and nuXmv should be
investigated. However, since the subsets of RSL which are translatable to each
input language are slightly different, it will require some work to formulate a
specification which is simultaneously translatable to all of them. Due to time
constraints, this has not been possible to investigate during the course of this
thesis.
==PAGE:159==
10.3 Future Work
143
10.3.8
Modelling Natively for Model Checker
It would be very interesting to model a system as similar as possible to the one
produced in this project in another language – specifically, a language which is a
direct (native) input language to a model checking tool. This could be done both
for another model checker than SAL, but also for SAL itself. The latter would
hopefully help clear up whether it is the translation process from RSL-SAL to
SAL which results in poor performance, or whether it is the SAL model checker
tool itself. Modelling the system in the input language for another model checker
than SAL would, of course, serve nicely as a comparison with the native SAL
model.
10.3.9
More Realism
For the model of the interlocking system to be useful for implementing a system
in the real world, it would be necessary to include in the model some additional
nuances.
Many of the abstractions and assumptions presented in Section 6.2
are simplifications of the real world. It should be carefully considered which of
these simplifications are hiding possible fault-scenarios (which could affect the
interlocking), and which of the simplifications are solely easing the modelling
process. The abstractions which hide fault-scenarios should either be eliminated
by making the model more concrete (such that the scenarios are handled by the
interlocking system) or it should be ensured that the fault-scenarios are handled
by other means than the interlocking system.
For example, in this thesis, it was assumed that the communication between
the control components is perfect and no information is lost. This is hardly the
case in the real world and could be handled by setting up a more fault-tolerant
communication protocol.
As another example, the intermediate position of points while they are switch-
ing is abstracted away from in this thesis. It is important that the interlocking
system prevents trains from entering a point which is in the middle of switching
its position, so this is another natural abstraction to take into consideration.
It should be noted, though, that improving the realism (and thereby probably
the complexity) of the model is not advisable before some substantial performance-
improving changes have been done – if they are even possible. As of now, it is
not possible to verify system configurations of the size they are found in the real
world.
==PAGE:160==
144
==PAGE:161==
CHAPTER 11
Conclusion
In this thesis, model checking has been investigated as a method for verifying a
geographically distributed railway interlocking system with respect to safety. A
railway interlocking system has been modelled in RSL-SAL and checked against
specified safety properties using the SAL model checker.
With a case study as the starting point, a generic state transition system
model has been developed in three steps, where each step is a refinement of the
former. Each of the three versions of the system models has been instantiated with
configuration data, and safety and consistency assertions have been verified for
the instantiations. During the verification process, the performance of the SAL
model checker was measured. These measurements were used for investigating
how the size of the transition system affects the SAL model checking tool.
In addition to specifying and verifying the system models, several experiments
with different specification approaches were conducted. Verification metrics of
models with different specification approaches were measured in order to invest-
igate the effects on verification performance.
It was possible to verify the safety properties and the additional consistency
properties of instantiations of each of the versions of the system models. However,
only small example system configurations could be verified within a reasonable
time. The limited scalability of the system models prevents them from being used
in real-world applications, before having been substantially optimised.
The results from the experiments with different approaches to writing the spe-
cifications showed that both approaches which affect the performance positively
and negatively were used in the system specifications. However, the results indic-
ate, in conjunction with results from other pieces of work (Jacob Hansen’s thesis
[13] and Michel Nielsen’s thesis [18]), that even if no approaches with negative
effects had been chosen, the scalability of the systems would still be limited – at
least using RSL-SAL and the SAL model checker.
The outcome of this thesis is three different versions of a generic system spe-
cification which can be instantiated with concrete configuration data and sub-
sequently model checked. Besides the system specifications and the associated
performance results, a valuable outcome of this thesis are the recommendations
for writing RSL-SAL specifications. The recommendations are generally applic-
able when writing RSL-SAL specifications which should be translated to SAL and
subsequently verified using the SAL model checker.

146
==PAGE:163==

/∗WELLFORMEDNESS ∗/
−−Checks whether the network is wellformed. An empty network is
−−said to be non−wellformed. Anti−symmetry is a requirement: if
−−(s1,s2) is a connection then (s2,s1) cannot be a connection.
−−The network is oriented and the connections must be given
−−in accordance with this. For example if the connections
==PAGE:165==
A.1 Base System
149
−−(s1,s2) and (s1,s3) are given, then there cannot be a
−−connection (s2,s3). The network must form a completed network
−−(which can consist of multiple, disjunct sub−networks).
network_wf : Network →Bool
network_wf(net) ≡
net ̸= {} ∧
(∀connection : Connection • connection ∈net ⇒
is_connected(connection,net) ∧
case connection of
conn(segm1,segm2) →conn(segm2,segm1) /∈net ∧
is_conn_directional(connection,net),
endpoint(e) →(∼is_fst_in_conn(e,net) ∧
is_snd_in_conn(e,net)) ∨
(is_fst_in_conn(e,net) ∧
∼is_snd_in_conn(e,net))
end),
−−Checks whether the switchbox description is wellformed wrt
−−the network. For all switchbox connections in the
−−description, the stem and each of the branches (if any) must
−−be neighbors in the network. For all connections in the
−−network there must exist a corresponding switchbox and vice
−−versa. There cannot exist duplicate switchbox connections.

∧
(∀c : Connection • c ∈net ⇒
(∃sbconn : SwitchboxConnection •
sbconn ∈rng(sbdesc) ∧
conn_matches_sb_conn(c,sbconn)))
∧

∧

−−Checks whether a train route is wellformed wrt the

−−be connected, so each key−value pair of segments must share a
−−segment with some other key−value pair in the route.

/∗CONSISTENCY ∗/
−−Checks whether a train’s switchboxes and route are consistent
−−with each other. Each switchbox should be ”pairable” with two
−−segments in sequence from the route and vice versa.
train_sbs_route_consistent : SboxMap × Route × SwitchboxDesc
→Bool
train_sbs_route_consistent(sboxes,route,sbdesc) ≡
sbs_pair_with_segments(route,sboxes,sbdesc) ∧
segments_pair_with_sbs(route,sboxes,sbdesc),
−−Checks whether a position is consistent with a route. For a
−−single segment position, this is the case either is the route
−−is empty or if the position exists in the domain of the
−−route. For a double segment position, this is the case if the
−−first segment of the position is in the domain of the route,
−−and if the second segment is the next segment in the route.
consistent_pos : Position × Route →Bool
consistent_pos(pos,route) ≡
case pos of
single (seg) →route = [] ∨seg ∈dom(route),
double(seg1,seg2) →seg1 ∈dom(route) ∧
route(seg1) = seg2 end,
−−Checks whether the next switchbox of a train is consistent
−−with the position of the train . The next switchbox must be
−−connected to one of the segments of the position. The
−−switchbox should also be in the domain of the train’s map of
−−switchboxes, unless the train is at the end of the route
−−(i.e. the map is empty).
consistent_next_sb : SwitchboxID × Position × SboxMap ×
SwitchboxDesc →Bool

consistent_next_sb(sbid,pos,sboxes,sbdesc) ≡
sbid ∈dom(sbdesc) ∧
case pos of
single (seg) →(sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∨sboxes = []) ∧
seg ∈segments_of_sbconn(sbdesc(sbid)),
double(seg1,seg2) →sbid ∈dom(sboxes) ∧
(seg1 ∈segments_of_sbconn(sbdesc(sbid)) ∨
seg2 ∈segments_of_sbconn(sbdesc(sbid))) end,
−−Checks whether the reservations of a train is consistent with
−−the train route. The reservations should only be for segments
−−along the (rest of the) route, except if the route is empty.
−−The reservations can never be empty since the train must have
−−at least a reservation for its current position.
consistent_t_reservations : ResMap × Route →Bool
consistent_t_reservations(res,route) ≡
res ̸= [] ∧
(route = [] ∨
(∀sbid : SwitchboxID • sbid ∈dom(res) ⇒
res(sbid) ⊆segments_of_route(route))),
−−Checks whether the locks of a train is consistent with the
−−switchboxes of the train. The train should only have locks
−−for switchboxes on the (rest of the) route. Furthermore, the
−−train should never have a lock for the last switchbox in the
−−switchboxmap.
consistent_t_locks : Sboxset × SboxMap →Bool

locks ⊆sbs_of_sboxmap(sboxes) ∧
(∀sbid : SwitchboxID •
sbid ∈(rng(sboxes) \ dom(sboxes)) ⇒
sbid /∈locks ),
−−Checks whether the reservations for the trains in the system
−−are consistent with the reservations for the switchboxes in
−−the system, and vice versa.
cons_res : TResState × SbResState →Bool
cons_res(tResState,sbResState) ≡
(∀sbid : SwitchboxID • sbid ∈dom(sbResState) ⇒
(∀tid : TrainID • tid ∈dom(tResState) ⇒
cons_t_sb_res(tid,sbid,tResState,sbResState) ∧
cons_sb_t_res(tid,sbid,tResState,sbResState))),
−−Checks whether the reservations of a train are consistent
==PAGE:169==
A.1 Base System
153
−−with the reservations of all switchboxes. If a switchbox ID
−−is found in the train reservations then each of the segments
−−which are reserved from the train’s POV must be reserved by
−−that train ID from the switchbox’ POV.
cons_t_sb_res : TrainID × SwitchboxID × TResState ×
SbResState →Bool
cons_t_sb_res(tid,sbid,tResState,sbResState) ≡
sbid ∈dom(tResState(tid)) ⇒
(∀seg : Segment • seg ∈tResState(tid)(sbid) ∧
seg ∈dom(sbResState(sbid)) ⇒

−−Checks whether the reservations of a switchbox are consistent
−−with the reservations of all trains . If a segment is reserved
−−in the switchbox data (i.e. maps to something other than
−−t_none) then the segment must be in the set of reserved

cons_sb_t_res : TrainID × SwitchboxID × TResState ×
SbResState →Bool
cons_sb_t_res(tid,sbid,tResState,sbResState) ≡
(∀seg : Segment • seg ∈dom(sbResState(sbid)) ⇒
tid ̸= t_none ∧tid = sbResState(sbid)(seg) ⇒
sbid ∈dom(tResState(tid)) ∧
seg ∈tResState(tid)(sbid )),
−−Checks whether the locks of a train is consistent with the
−−locks of the switchboxes and vice versa. If a train has a
−−lock at a switchbox from the train’s POV, then the switchbox
−−must also in fact be locked by that train ’s ID. If a
−−switchbox is locked by a train ID, then that train must also
−−have a lock for that switchbox.
cons_locks : TLocksState × SbLockState →Bool
cons_locks(tLocksState,sbLockState) ≡
(∀sbid : SwitchboxID • sbid ∈dom(sbLockState) ⇒
(∀tid : TrainID • tid ∈dom(tLocksState) ⇒
sbid ∈tLocksState(tid) ⇒sbLockState(sbid) = tid
∧
tid = sbLockState(sbid) ⇒sbid ∈tLocksState(tid))),
−−Checks whether the sensors’ status and the trains’ positions

−−position with the stem and one of the branches of the
−−switchbox segments. Likewise, if a train is in a critical

−−section then the sensor of the train ’s next switchbox should

(∃seg : Segment •

(tPosState(tid) = double(stem(sbdesc(sbid)),seg) ∨

double(segm1,segm2) →
sbSensorState(tNextsbState(tid)) = active,

/∗GUARDS ∗/
−−Checks whether a train can move. Given a single position,
−−trains can move if the train has a lock at the upcoming
−−switchbox. The route must not be empty since the position of
−−the train is then the last segment in the route. For a double
−−segment position, the train can always move, since a train
−−must have always been at a single position just before and
−−had the corresponding lock in order to move partly onto the
−−second segment.
t_can_move : Segment × Route × Sboxset × SwitchboxID × ResMap ×
SboxMap →Bool
t_can_move(posseg,route,locks,nextsb,res,sboxes) ≡
route ̸= [] ∧nextsb ∈locks ∧nextsb ∈dom(sboxes) ∧
posseg ∈dom(route) ∧
route(posseg) ∈res(nextsb) ∧
route(posseg) ∈res(sboxes(nextsb)),
−−Checks whether a train can make a reservation at a given
==PAGE:171==

155
−−switchbox. The train must not already have reserved the
−−segment at the switchbox. A train can make a reservation only
−−if the switchbox is one of the switchboxes along the train ’s
−−route.
t_can_reserve : SboxMap × SwitchboxID × Segment × Route ×
ResMap →Bool
